Additional Proofs and the Benefit of Coinage Objection
Dispute on whether metaphorical usage requires prior literal usage

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate the secondary proofs (such as 'asa', 'ni'ma', and compound metaphors) and refute the objection that majaz would nullify the benefit of coinage.

Core Idea
The Hanafi stance is supported by multiple linguistic examples, and the claim that majaz destroys the benefit of coinage is false.

In this lesson, we continue exploring the Hanafi stance that majaz does not require prior haqiqah. We examine additional proofs: words like 'asa' and 'ni'ma' which are coined for informing but used only in constructions of hope and praise, and the case of ambiguous words. We also address the opponents' strongest objection—that without literal usage, the very purpose of coinage (to convey meaning) would be lost. The Hanafi response shows that the benefit of coinage is preserved through metaphorical usage itself.

Guided Path
1
Examine the proof from 'asa' and 'ni'ma'
These words are coined for informing (ikhbar) but are used only in constructions like 'asa' for hope and 'ni'ma' for praise. They have no record of being used literally. This supports that majaz does not require prior haqiqah.
The author notes this is a mere claim if not proven, but it serves as a plausible example.
2
Consider the proof from ambiguous words (mubhamaat)
Some scholars argue that ambiguous words like 'man' (who) are coined for universal concepts and then used for specific individuals. This too shows that a word can be used in a non-literal sense without prior literal usage.
The text notes this opinion is not strong evidence alone.
3
Analyze the opponent's compound metaphor argument
Opponents cite compounds like 'qamat al-harbu 'ala saq' (the war stood on a leg) as metaphors that require prior literal composition. The Hanafi response: these are metaphors in the compositional structure, not in individual words; the individual words ('shaba', 'lamma') are used literally.
This keeps the dispute focused on individual words.
4
Refute the benefit-of-coinage objection
Opponents argue that without prior literal usage, the benefit of coinage—conveying meaning—would be lost. The Hanafi response: the benefit is not lost because metaphorical usage itself conveys meaning and is a valid benefit. Also, if coinage is from God, it is not possible for His coinage to lack benefit.
The text concludes that the negation of benefit is not necessary.
5
Conclude with the overall tarjih
The Hanafi position that majaz does not require prior haqiqah is the correct view (al-ashah). All proofs for necessity are refuted.

After agreement that the word after coinage and before usage is neither literal nor metaphorical: they are both categories of words used correctly. There is disagreement whether metaphor necessitates literal meaning and whether it is required that the word be used in its literal sense at least once. Some say it does necessitate, but the correct view is negation—it does not necessitate. Our proof: 'al-Rahman' for it is metaphorical linguistically or customarily and has no literal meaning. They presented it in two ways: First: it is only applied to God and its literal meaning is not realized, for it means 'possessor of mercy', and mercy is tenderness of heart, and He has no heart. Second: it is only applied to a specific individual of 'possessor of mercy', namely God, and was never applied to the absolute. If you say: The followers of Musaylimah the liar applied the word 'al-Rahman' to him, it became well-known until Abu Jahl said upon hearing 'al-Rahman' from the Prophet: 'We do not know al-Rahman except the Rahman of Yamama.' He replied: The Rahman of Yamama is rejected because it is not in accordance with the language, rather it is from their obstinacy and ignorance. Then the two ways are insufficient: no proof has been established that mercy is tenderness of heart; it could be coined for favor and beneficence. Indeed in humans this favor does not occur except with tenderness of heart. And its not being applied to other than Him is due to the absence of its meaning, for it considers complete hyperbole, as He is possessor of great favor. This glorification is in terms of the vastness of the objects of mercy encompassing everyone, and the favors in terms of abundance and intensity, which absolutely does not exist in other than Him. After concession: applying a general term to a specific individual is not metaphor; ponder this concession. And our proof: 'asa' and 'ni'ma' because they are formulas coined for informing but were never used in that, only in construction—this is also a mere claim with no proof. And our proof: the ambiguous words—according to one opinion—that they are coined for universal concepts to be used for individuals, and it is obvious that one opinion is not a proof, especially an opinion refuted by clear proof. As for proving the claim by compounds like 'the war stood on a leg' and 'the forelock of night turned white'—they are metaphors and these structures were never used in their literal meanings—this is outside the dispute because the dispute is about individual words, and here the metaphor is in the compositional structure, not in 'shaba' and 'lamma' which are used in their literal meanings. What was said that it is a shared obligation is an error: because what is required for metaphor is the knowledge of the meaning even if it is imaginary and not real in itself. That knowledge is realized, but its actual realization is not required, as in false statements. From this another answer to the proof emerges: it is possible that it has a literal meaning used by the liar and the weak narrator, so understand. And what was said in 'al-Tahrir' that it is shared—obligingly due to its necessitating coinage, and the agreement that the compound was not individually coined—the discussion about it then led to the point that metaphor must have... The third section on linguistic principles: it is coined by individual coinage, and is using it in that required or not? There is discussion. For there is no specificity of individual coinage; do you not see that they used 'al-Rahman' and 'asa' as proofs even though they are coined by generic coinage? Rather, derivatives and verbs exit from the discussion. The compelers said: if metaphor does not necessitate literal meaning, the benefit of coinage—which is conveying compositional meaning at the time of usage—would be lost, and with no usage, no benefit. We say: the conjunction is denied, for the negation of a specific benefit does not necessitate its absolute negation, because the validity of metaphorical usage is among the benefits and is not negated. It was said: the negation of the consequent is denied, for there is no impossibility in the nonexistence of benefit. I say: if the coinage is from God as is apparent, then the negation of benefit is apparent.
Source pages p. 174

Commentary
This passage is a dispute in usul al-fiqh regarding whether metaphorical usage (majaz) necessarily implies that the word was previously used in its literal sense (haqiqah). The correct Hanafi position, as stated, is that it does not necessitate such prior literal usage. The proof is given using the word 'al-Rahman' which is metaphorically applied to God but has no literal human meaning (tenderness of heart). The author critiques the two standard arguments, showing they are insufficient, and provides his own reasoning. He also addresses counterarguments from opponents such as the usage by Musaylimah's followers and the claim that compounds like 'the war stood on a leg' prove prior literal usage. He clarifies that the dispute concerns individual words, not compositional structures. Finally, he discusses the issue of individual coinage (al-wad' al-shakhsi) and refutes the objection that without prior literal usage, the benefit of coinage is lost. Throughout, the Hanafi school's preference for the negation of istilzam is upheld.

Key Terms
مجاز (majaz): A word used in other than its original coined meaning with a valid contextual connection
حقيقة (haqiqah): A word used in its original coined meaning
استلزام (istilzam): Necessitation; here, whether majaz necessitates a prior haqiqah

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT used as a proof that majaz does not require prior haqiqah?
A) al-Rahman
B) 'asa'
C) compound metaphors like 'the war stood on a leg'
D) ambiguous words (mubhamaat)

Key Takeaway
Beyond 'al-Rahman', the Hanafi school uses examples from 'asa', 'ni'ma', and ambiguous words to reject the necessity of prior literal usage, and refutes the objection that such metaphor would nullify the benefit of coinage.

Metaphorical Predication: Ibn al-Hajib vs. al-Sakkaki
Four madhahib on metaphorical predication

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between Ibn al-Hajib's and al-Sakkaki's positions on metaphorical predication in 'anbata al-rabi' and evaluate which view is preferred according to Hanafi usul.

Core Idea
Metaphor in predication can be located in the verb or subject; al-Sakkaki's view is more precise but still contested.

This passage from Fawatih al-Rahamut examines the classic example 'anbata al-rabi' al-baqal' (the spring caused the plants to grow) and the four schools of thought regarding where the metaphor lies. The discussion centers on whether the metaphor is in the predicate (verb) or the subject (spring). Understanding these positions is crucial for analyzing metaphorical language in legal texts, as it affects how we interpret the intended meaning of statements involving attribution.

Guided Path
1
Identify the example and the two main positions
The phrase 'anbata al-rabi' al-baqal' is analyzed. The first position (Ibn al-Hajib) considers the metaphor in the predicate (anbata), while the second (al-Sakkaki) considers it in the subject (al-rabi) via isti'ara bil-kinnaya.
The other two madhahib are mentioned but not elaborated in this passage.
2
Understand Ibn al-Hajib's argument and its refutation
Ibn al-Hajib argues the verb implies a capable agent, so attributing it to an incapable spring makes it metaphorical. This is refuted: the verb does not indicate capability; it only indicates attribution to some agent.
The refutation relies on the consensus of rhetoricians (ulama al-bayan).
3
Recognize the correction by al-Jawnfuri
The error is attributed to translators (al-mutarjimin). Ibn al-Hajib's correct intent was to interpret the predicate contextually, not to claim the verb inherently implies capability. The metaphor is in the predicate (anbata) meaning 'preparation' (tahayyu') instead of 'creation' (khalq).
Al-Jawnfuri's verification in al-Fara'id is cited as the correct interpretation.
4
Examine al-Sakkaki's view
Al-Sakkaki holds the metaphor is in the subject (al-rabi) via isti'ara bil-kinnaya: spring is mentioned but a capable agent is intended by claiming spring belongs to its kind. The predication is literal, but the subject is metaphorical.
This is considered more precise ('aqrab ila al-dabt') by some.
5
Evaluate the objection to al-Sakkaki
Al-Sakkaki claimed his view suffices and obviates the need for al-majaz al-'aqli (intellectual metaphor). The objection is that it does not fully replace the concept of intellectual metaphor, meaning his view does not cover all cases.
Thus, the debate remains open; the Hanafi school engages with both views.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة من قد اختلف في نحو : أنبت الربيع (البقل) أي فيما إذا أسند المسند إلى ما حقه أن لا يسند إليه على أربعة مذاهب الأول أنه مجاز في المسند فإنه أريد به غير الموضوع له (وهو التسبب العادي مثلاً وإن كان وضعه للتسبب الحقيقي وذلك قول ابن الحاجب) وقرر بأن الفعل يدخل في مفهومه النسبة إلى الفاعل القادر فإذا أسند إلى غير القادر يكون مجازاً البتة (ورد) بما اتفق عليه علماء البيان من أن الفعل لا يدل بحسب أصل الوضع على أن فاعله يلزم أن يكون قادراً أو غير قادر سبباً حقيقياً أو سبباً (غير حقيقي) فإن الفعل إنما أخذ في مفهومه النسبة إلى فاعل ما لا إلى الفاعل القادر وإذا كان الفاعل أعم من المختار وغيره والسبب الحقيقي وغيره فليس هناك تسبب حقيقي هو مدلول الفعل حتى يكون الانتقال إلى التسبب العادي مجازاً ورد أيضاً بأن من الأفعال ما ليس إسناده إلى الفاعل المختار فيلزم حينئذ أن تكون هذه الأفعال مجازات والتزامه بعيد كل البعد ورد أيضاً بأن الحكم بدخول النسبة إلى الفاعل القادر لوجود بعض الأفعال مسندة إليه ليس أولى من العكس، ثم اعلم أن الخطأ المترجمين في تقرير كلامه ومرامه ،مصون عن هذه الشناعات فإنه لم يرد أن في مدلول الفعل النسبة إلى القادر بل مراده أنه لما صدر ممن لا يعتقد ظاهره عرف أن فيه تأويلاً، فأوّل هو في المسند، وحكم بأن المراد منه ما يصلح لأن يسند إلى المذكور وههنا المذكور الإنبات وهو في اللغة والعرف خلق النبات فتجوّز عن التهيؤ والاستعداد له وهو التسبب العادي، وعلى هذا القياس يؤوّل في كل مثال ما يليق به وعلى هذا لا يرد عليه شيء فافهم وهو الذي اختاره الجونفوري في تحقيق كلامه في الفرائد ولعل المصنف إلى هذا أشار بقوله: (فتأمل الثاني أنه) أي التجوز (في المسند إليه الذي هو الربيع وهو قول السكاكي إنه استعارة بالكناية) وهي عنده ذكر أحد طرفي التشبيه، وإرادة الآخر بادعاء أنه من جنسه فههنا شبه الربيع بالقادر المختار في تلبس الإنبات في الفعل وذكر الربيع وأريد به القادر المختار بادعاء أن الربيع قادر مختار، لا أنه أريد به قادر غير الربيع فالمقصود بالذات تشبيه الربيع بالقادر، ونسبة الإنبات قرينة عليه وقال السكاكي إن هذا النحو مغن عن القول بالإسناد المجازي، فهو الأولى، فيكون أقرب إلى الضبط وأورد أنه لا يكون (مغنياً عن المجاز العقلي : (كما زعمه)
Question: They differed regarding phrases like 'the spring caused the plants to grow'—that is, when a predicate is attributed to what should not be predicated—on four schools. First: it is a metaphor in the predicate, meaning it was used for other than its subject matter (like customary causation, even though its coinage was for real causation); this is the saying of Ibn al-Hajib. He argued that the verb includes in its concept the attribution to a capable agent, so when attributed to an incapable one, it is definitely metaphorical. It was refuted by what the rhetoricians agreed upon: that the verb does not indicate in its original coinage that its agent must be capable or not, real cause or customary cause. For the verb only includes in its concept attribution to some agent, not to a capable agent. Since the agent is more general than the voluntary and otherwise, and the real cause and otherwise, there is no real causation that is the meaning of the verb such that moving to customary causation would be metaphorical. It was also refuted that some verbs are not attributed to voluntary agents; thus those verbs would have to be metaphorical, which is far-fetched. It was also refuted that the judgment that the attribution to a capable agent is part of the verb because some verbs are attributed to it is not more likely than the opposite. Then know that the error of the translators in explaining his words—his intent is protected from these objections. He did not mean that the meaning of the verb includes attribution to a capable agent; rather, he meant that when it comes from someone who does not believe its apparent meaning, it is known that there is interpretation. So he interpreted the predicate and ruled that its intended meaning is what is suitable to be attributed to the mentioned agent. Here the mentioned is 'inbat' (growing) which linguistically and customarily means creating plants, so he used metaphorically for preparation and readiness, which is customary causation. Similarly in every example interpret what befits it. Thus nothing is objected, so understand. This is what al-Jawnfuri chose in his verification in al-Fara'id. Perhaps the author alluded to this with his words: ('tadabbar'). Second: that the metaphor is in the subject, which is 'al-rabi' (spring), and this is the saying of al-Sakkaki: it is a metaphorical metonymy (isti'ara bil-kinnaya). According to him, it is mentioning one of the two sides of the comparison and intending the other by claiming it is of its kind. So here, spring is compared to a capable voluntary agent in its performance of causing growth, and spring is mentioned but intended to mean the capable voluntary agent by claiming that spring is a capable voluntary agent—not that a capable agent other than spring is meant. The intended purpose is the comparison of spring to a capable agent, and the attribution of growth is evidence for it. Al-Sakkaki said that this type suffices from saying that the predication is metaphorical, so it is preferable and closer to precision. It was objected that it does not suffice for al-majaz al-'aqli (intellectual metaphor) as he claimed.
Source pages p. 175

Commentary
This passage is an analysis of the phrase 'anbata al-rabi' al-baqal' (the spring caused the plants to grow) concerning metaphorical predication (isnad majazi). Four schools are mentioned. The first, attributed to Ibn al-Hajib, holds that the metaphor is in the predicate (the verb 'anbata'), because the verb implies a capable agent, but spring is not capable. This is refuted by rhetoricians who say the verb does not specifically indicate a capable agent. The error is attributed to translators; the correct interpretation is that the verb is taken metaphorically for the customary cause (preparation). The text then presents the second madhhab of al-Sakkaki: the metaphor is in the subject (spring) via isti'ara bil-kinnaya (allusive metaphor), where spring is compared to a capable agent. Sakkaki claims this view is more precise and dispenses with the need to call the predication metaphorical. However, this is objected to on the grounds that it does not cover what is called 'al-majaz al-'aqli'. The discussion shows the Hanafi engagement with rhetorical analysis in usul al-fiqh.

Key Terms
الاستعارة بالكناية (al-isti'ara bil-kinnaya): A type of metaphor where one of the two things compared is mentioned and the other is intended by claim of belonging to its kind
المجاز العقلي (al-majaz al-'aqli): Intellectual metaphor; where the predication is intellectually understood as metaphorical even if the word is used literally

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the main objection raised against al-Sakkaki's view that his analysis of 'anbata al-rabi' suffices and obviates the need for calling the predication metaphorical?
A) His view is not based on any rhetorical principle.
B) His view does not cover what is known as al-majaz al-'aqli (intellectual metaphor).
C) His view is rejected by all Hanafi scholars.
D) His view confuses metaphor and simile.

Key Takeaway
The analysis of metaphorical predication in phrases like 'anbata al-rabi' reveals two main Hanafi approaches: one locating the metaphor in the predicate (Ibn al-Hajib, corrected) and one in the subject (al-Sakkaki), with the latter being more precise but not fully comprehensive.

Condition of Conceptual Possibility for Metaphor (Imkān al-Ḥaqīqī 'Aqlan)
Defense of metaphor through rational possibility

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi condition for valid metaphor and apply it to examples like marriage using 'hibah'.

Core Idea
Metaphor is valid if the literal meaning is rationally possible, even if it never occurs.

In this passage from Fawatih al-Rahamut, the author defends the Hanafi view that metaphor (majaz) remains valid as long as the literal meaning (haqiqah) is conceptually possible ('imkān 'aqlī). The objector argues that since a free woman cannot be owned, using the word 'hibah' (gift) for marriage is invalid. The Hanafi response clarifies that they require only rational possibility, not actual occurrence. The passage provides examples from the law of emancipation and from pre-Islamic legal systems to illustrate the principle.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
The objector claims that metaphor is invalid if the literal meaning is impossible in reality. Recognize that the objection assumes actual possibility is required.
2
State the Hanafi condition
The Hanafi position distinguishes between conceptual possibility (imkān 'aqlī) and actual occurrence. Grasp that only the former is required for metaphor.
3
Examine the examples
Analyze the examples: severance (qat') as a cause for emancipation, calling a known younger son 'my son', and the permissibility of owning free persons in the Shari'a of Ya'qub and early Islam. Notice how each demonstrates rational possibility.
4
Apply to the marriage case
Even though a free woman is not given as a gift in practice, the concept of owning a free person is rationally possible (as it existed in other legal systems). Therefore, the metaphor is valid.
5
Derive the usul rule
Formulate the rule: For a valid metaphor, the literal meaning must be rationally possible ('imkān 'aqlī). This is a principle of Hanafi usul al-fiqh.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يرد أن البنوة ليست سبباً أيضاً للإعتاق مطلقاً، بل عند وجودها كالقطع فإنه سبب عند وجوده خطأ ومفض إلى المال، وجه الدفع ظاهر ، ثم نقول : هما لا يشترطان إلا إمكان الحقيقي عقلاً في ظاهر الأمر لا وقوعه في نفس الأمر ، وههنا القطع ممكن عقلاً»، وإن لم يقع فلا دخل لفقدان المعنى في عدم صحة المجاز، فإن قلت : قد اتفقا على انعقاد النكاح بلفظ الهبة في أن المعنى الحقيقي لا يصح لأنها لا توهب أجاب بقوله (وأما اتفاقهما على انعقاد النكاح بالهبة في الحرة ولا يتصور) المعنى الحقيقي فلأنهما لم يشترطاه إلا) إمكانه (عقلاً) ألا ترى أنهما قالا فيما إذا قال : أنت ابني للأصغر المعروف النسب يعتق (وهو) أي الحقيقي (ممكن عقلاً كيف لا وقد وقع التملك للحر (في شريعة يعقوب عليه السلام) أي في الشريعة الخليلية التي كان يعقوب يعمل بها، وإذا التملك صح الهبة عقلاً (و) قد وقع أيضاً (في أول الإسلام) ثم نسخ (كذا قيل).
It is objected that sonship is not a cause for emancipation absolutely; rather, when it exists it is like severance which is a cause when it exists erroneously and leads to wealth. The repudiation is apparent. Then we say: The two (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf?) do not require except the possibility of the literal meaning rationally in apparent circumstances, not its actual occurrence in itself. Here, severance is rationally possible even if it did not occur, so the absence of the meaning has no bearing on the invalidity of metaphor. If you say: They both agreed on the validity of marriage contract with the word 'hibah' in a free woman, whereas the literal meaning is invalid because she is not given as a gift. He responded with: 'As for their agreement on the validity of marriage with 'hibah' in a free woman, and the literal meaning is inconceivable, it is because they only require its possibility rationally.' Do you not see that they said when one says 'You are my son' to a known younger child of known lineage, he is freed, and the literal meaning is rationally possible. How not? And ownership of a free person did occur in the Shari'a of Ya'qub (peace be upon him), i.e., the Patriarchal law that Ya'qub practiced. If ownership is possible, then 'hibah' (gift) is rationally valid. And it also occurred in early Islam, then abrogated. Thus it is said.
Source pages p. 184

Commentary
This passage is a response to an objection against the validity of metaphor (majaz) when the literal meaning (haqiqah) is impossible in empirical reality. The objector argues that since a free woman cannot be owned, the word 'hibah' (gift) cannot be used for marriage as it would be metaphor without a possible literal meaning. The Hanafi response clarifies that they only require conceptual possibility (imkan 'aqli), not actual occurrence. They give examples: severance (qat') is conceptually possible even if it doesn't happen; calling a known younger son 'my son' is possible conceptually even though paternity is known. Furthermore, in the Shari'a of Prophet Ya'qub, free persons could be owned, and in early Islam, ownership of free persons was possible before being abrogated. Thus, the condition for valid metaphor is that the literal meaning be possible in the intellect, not that it actually happens.

Key Terms
إمكان الحقيقي عقلاً (imkān al-ḥaqīqī ‘aqlan): The conceptual possibility of the literal meaning, as opposed to actual occurrence, as a condition for the validity of metaphor.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is a marriage contract using the word 'hibah' (gift) valid according to Hanafis?
A) Because gift literally means marriage.
B) Because the literal meaning of gift is rationally possible, even if it never occurs.
C) Because the Shafi'is also allow it.
D) Because 'hibah' is a legal term for marriage.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi usul principle: For a valid metaphor (majaz), the literal meaning (haqiqah) must be conceptually possible (imkan aqli), even if it never actually occurs.

Rule of 'Mawali' in Bequest: Metaphor vs Reality
Explains rule of adding 'mawali' in wasiyyah and ikhtilaf

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi usul principle of 'jam' bayn al-haqiqa wa al-majaz' to determine the scope of terms in a bequest, and distinguish between the positions of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn.

Core Idea
Using a plural when only one exists forces a choice: literal minimum or metaphorical expansion.

When a testator uses the plural 'mawali' or 'abna'' but has only one such relative, the Hanafi jurists ask: is the plural taken literally (minimum two) or as a metaphor for all indirect relations? This passage from Fawatih al-Rahamut unpacks the ikhtilaf between Abu Hanifa and his two distinguished disciples, revealing the usul principle that we cannot combine literal and metaphorical meanings in one usage.

Guided Path
1
Identify the testator's statement and literal scope
Consider the phrase 'I bequeath to the mawali of so-and-so'. The word 'mawali' when added (idafa) literally refers to direct patrons/freed slaves. Understand that this literal meaning is the default (haqiqa).
Literal meaning is primary; any deviation must be justified by a contextual indicator (qarina).
2
Recognize the problem of a single individual
If the testator has only one mawla, the plural form cannot be literally true. The law must decide how to interpret the bequest to avoid nullification. Two options: treat the plural as metaphorical (expanding to include indirect) or take it literally as a minimum of two, making the bequest partially effective.
The key usul issue: can we combine the literal and metaphorical meanings in one utterance? Hanafi says no.
3
Compare the solutions of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn
Abu Hanifa: the plural is literal, so the bequest is for a minimum of two. With only one mawla, he gets half the bequest (share of one) and the rest goes to heirs. The Sahibayn: the plural is metaphorical, so the one mawla gets the whole, and indirect mawali are also included.
The thamarah al-khilaf (practical difference) is clear: distribution and inclusion of indirect relatives.
4
Trace the underlying usul principle
Abu Hanifa's position is rooted in the rule that 'jam' bayn al-haqiqa wa al-majaz' (combining literal and metaphorical) is impermissible. If the plural were taken as metaphorical in one aspect (for the single) and literal for the number, that would be a prohibited combination. The Sahibayn avoid this by taking the whole expression as metaphorical.
The Sahibayn rely on the context: the speaker knew he had only one, so he intended a broader meaning.
5
Apply to the parallel case of children and grandchildren
The same ikhtilaf applies when a testator says 'to my children' (abna') but has only one child. Abu Hanifa gives half to the one child, half to heirs; the Sahibayn include grandchildren and give all to the child. All agree that if there are two or more children, no metaphorical extension occurs.
This case illustrates the consistent application of the usul principle across different terms.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فرع (اختص الموالي في الوصية لهم بأن يقول : أوصيت لموالي فلان (دون مواليهم) أي لا يدخل موالي الموالي لأن المولى المنسوب إليه حقيقة من يكون منتسباً بالذات، وأما موالي الموالي فلا ينسب إليه حقيقة فيراد الموالي لكونها حقيقة ولا يراد موالي الموالي وإلا لزم الجمع (إلا أن يكون الموالي واحداً فله النصف والباقي للورثة عنده، لأنه أوصى لجماعة الموالي، وأقلها اثنان فيكون لكل واحد نصف الوصية، وإذ المولى واحد استحق النصف والباقي ميراث، وإنما كان أقلها اثنين لأن الاثنين فما فوقهما جماعة في الوصية، كما في الميراث لأن كليهما خلافتان بعد الموت في الملك ، قال مطلع الأسرار الإلهية»: لا يظهر لكون أقل الجمع في «الوصايا اثنين ،وجه والقياس على الميراث باطل، فإنه لا يلزم من استعمال لفظ في معنى تجوزاً في صورة أن يستعمل في نظيرها في ذلك المعنى ولا فيها أبداً، نعم: إن تأيد ذلك بالاستعمال فله وجه وكذا الأبناء مع الحفدة عنده أي إذا أوصى لأبناء فلان يدخل بنوه دون بني بنيه، إلا أن يكون الابن واحداً فله النصف والباقي للورثة الوجه الوجه (وعندهما يدخلون) أي موالي الموالي وأبناء الأبناء حال كونهم (مع) المولى (الواحد) أو الابن الواحد فيهما لعموم المجاز) فإنه لما أطلق صيغة الجمع وهو يعلم أن لا مولى ولا ابن إلا واحد علم أنه أراد معنى أعم بحيث يتناول موالي المولى والحفدة أيضاً (دون) موالي الموالي والحفدة (مع الاثنين (بالاتفاق إذ لا قرينة على إرادة المجاز
Branch: The Mawali are specified in the bequest such that if one says 'I bequeath to the Mawali of so-and-so', [it means] his Mawali, not their Mawali, i.e. the Mawali of the Mawali are not included, because the Mawali who are attributed to him are in reality those who are direct relatives; as for the Mawali of the Mawali, they are not attributed to him in reality. So the Mawali are intended because they are the literal meaning, and the Mawali of the Mawali are not intended, otherwise combination [of literal and metaphorical] would be necessary. Unless the Mawali are one – then he gets half and the remainder goes to the heirs according to him, because he bequeathed to a group of Mawali, and the minimum is two, so each gets half the bequest; and when the Mawali are one, he deserves half and the remainder is inheritance. The minimum being two is because two or more is a group in bequest, as in inheritance, because both are deputyships after death in ownership. The author of Matali' al-Asrar al-Ilahiyya said: 'There is no apparent reason for the minimum of a plural in bequests being two, and analogy on inheritance is invalid, because it does not follow that using a word in a meaning metaphorically in one form necessitates using it in that meaning in its analogue nor ever [in that analogue]. Yes, if that is supported by usage then it has a basis.' Similarly for children and grandchildren according to him: i.e. if he bequeaths to the children of so-and-so, his own children enter, not his children's children, unless the child is one – then he gets half and the remainder goes to the heirs. The same [ruling] with the same [justification]. And according to them [the two companions], they enter – i.e. the Mawali of the Mawali and the children's children – when they are with the one Mawla or the one child in both cases, due to the generality of metaphor. For when he uttered the plural form while knowing that there is only one Mawla or one child, it is known that he intended a more general meaning that includes the Mawali of the Mawla and the grandchildren as well – [but] not [including] the Mawali of the Mawali and grandchildren when there are two [Mawali or children], by agreement, since there is no contextual evidence (qarina) indicating the metaphorical intent.
Source pages p. 186

Commentary
This is a textual analysis of a subsidiary rule (far') concerning bequests (wasiyyah) where the testator says 'I bequeath to the Mawali of so-and-so.' The ruling: only the direct Mawali (freed slaves or close patrons) are included, not their Mawali. The reason is that the word 'Mawali' when added to a person (idafa) literally refers to those with a direct relationship; including the Mawali of the Mawali would require combining the literal and metaphorical meanings (al-jam' bayn al-haqiqa wa al-majaz), which is disallowed. However, if the testator has only one Mawla, then the word 'Mawali' (plural) must be interpreted metaphorically to include that one, so that the bequest does not fail. He receives half the bequest; the remainder goes to the legal heirs. The same applies if the bequest is to 'children' (abna') – only direct children enter, but if only one child exists, grandchildren are included according to Abu Hanifa. The two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) hold that in the case of a single Mawla or a single child, both the direct and the indirect (Mawali of the Mawla or grandchildren) are included, because the use of the plural when only one exists indicates a metaphorical extension. When there are two or more, all agree that only the direct are meant, since there is no contextual clue (qarina) to override the literal meaning.

Key Terms
الجمع بين الحقيقة والمجاز (al-jam' bayn al-haqiqa wa al-majaz): Combining literal and metaphorical usage of a single word in one utterance, which is disallowed in Hanafi usul.
الموالي (al-mawali): Plural of mawla; here refers to patrons/freed slaves or close relatives through patronage.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If Zayd bequeaths 'to my mawali' and he has exactly one mawla (patronized slave), according to Imam Abu Hanifa, what is the ruling?
A) The entire bequest goes to that single mawla.
B) Half goes to that mawla and half to the legal heirs.
C) The bequest is invalid because the plural was used incorrectly.
D) The mawla gets nothing; the bequest goes entirely to the heirs.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, when a testator uses a plural but has only one, the literal plural (minimum two) is applied, giving half to the single, unless contextual evidence (qarina) mandates a metaphorical extension—which the Sahibayn accept but Abu Hanifa limits to the single individual.

Resolving Literal-Metaphorical Conflict: The Aman Objection
Presents and refutes an objection from aman rulings

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when Hanafi usul uses shubhat al-haqiqa to permit inclusion based on common usage, and apply this reasoning to similar textual ambiguities.

Core Idea
Precaution in protecting life can override strict literalism through shubhat al-haqiqa.

A key objection against the Hanafi principle that combining literal and metaphorical meanings is impermissible arises from the law of aman (safe conduct). When a musta'min says 'grant me security over my children' (bani), does it include grandchildren? The objector argues that it must, but that would require combining literal (sons) and metaphorical (grandsons) meanings. This lesson unpacks the Hanafi response and its implications for textual analysis.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Objection
Recognize the claim: that including grandchildren under 'bani' in aman contradicts the rule against combining haqiqa and majaz.
The objector uses this as a counterexample (naqd) against a previous ruling.
2
Extract the Hanafi Response
Note that the Hanafis do not interpret 'bani' as majaz for grandchildren; instead, inclusion is based on precaution (ihtiyat) and shubhat al-haqiqa provided by common usage (e.g., 'Banu Hashim').
Aman is established by strong probability (shubha), so strict literalism is not required.
3
Analyze the Role of Shubhat al-Haqiqa
Understand that common usage creates a semblance of literal meaning, allowing inclusion without violating the principle against combining haqiqa and majaz.
This is a key Hanafi tool for textual expansion in cases of necessity.
4
Examine the Grandparent Dispute
Recognize the ikhtilaf: two narrations on including grandparents under 'fathers' and 'mothers'. One includes them, the other excludes them on grounds of being 'original in creation'.
The passage prefers the first narration, especially based on al-Hidayah's linguistic point about 'umm' meaning origin.
5
Apply the Textual Analysis Method
Use the same reasoning to assess other cases: identify the literal meaning, check for common usage or necessity, and determine if inclusion by shubhat al-haqiqa is warranted.
This method is used in Hanafi usul to resolve apparent contradictions between textual categories.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم ينقض هذا الحكم أولاً بدخول حفدة المستأمن مع بنيه في (الأمان إذا قال : أمنوني على بني، فيلزم الجمع، لأن الابن المضاف حقيقة في الابن ومجاز في الحفدة (وأجيب) بأنه لم يرد الحفدة بلفظ الابن، لكن الاحتياط في الحقن أي في حقن الدم ( أوجب الدخول) في الأمان (تبعاً لوجود شبهة الحقيقة بالاستعمال الشائع نحو : بنو هاشم فعلوا كذا والأمان مما يثبت بالشبهة، لأن أمر الدم ليس سهلاً ودخول الأجداد والجدات في الآباء والأمهات إذا قال: أمنوني على آبائي وأمهاتي (مختلف) فيه ففي رواية : يدخل، وهو ظاهر، وفي رواية لا وجهوها بأن دخول الحفدة كان تبعاً، ودخول الأجداد والجدات إن كان فبالتبع وهم أصول خلقة فلا يدخلون بالتبع، وهذا الوجه ليس بشيء، لأن الأصالة في الخلقة لا تنافي التبعية في الدخول في أحكام أخر، مع قال في «الهداية»، الأم لغة الأصل، فحينئذ الدخول بالذات لا بالتبع، فإذن الأشبه الرواية الأولى، وإن كانت الثانية ظاهر الرواية، ثم ههنا وجه آخر لو كفوا به لكان أسهل هو: أن الظاهر أن الرجل لا يؤثر حياة نفسه وأبنائه دون أبناء أبنائه، فهم يدخلون بدلالة النص، لكن الظاهر أن الأجداد والجدات أيضاً يدخلون بالدلالة، اللهم إلا أن يكونوا مفسدين ذوي رأي فيعلم أن الإمام لا يؤمن مثلهم فيخرجون عن الأمان، ولعل هذا مشترك بينهم وبين الحفدة (و)
Then this ruling is refuted firstly by the inclusion of the grandchildren of the musta'min (protected person) with his children in the aman (security) when he says: 'Grant me security over my children' – then combination [of literal and metaphorical] would be required, because 'ibn' (son) when added is literal for son and metaphorical for grandson. And it is answered: He did not intend the grandchildren by the word 'ibn', but precaution in protecting blood necessitated their entry in the aman, following the existence of a semblance of literal meaning due to common usage like 'Banu Hashim did such-and-such'. And aman is established by semblance because the matter of blood is not trivial. And the inclusion of grandfathers and grandmothers under 'fathers' and 'mothers' when he says 'Grant me security over my fathers and mothers' is disputed. In one narration: they enter, and that is apparent. In another narration: they do not, and they justify it by saying that the inclusion of grandchildren was subsidiary, and the inclusion of grandfathers and grandmothers, if it occurs, is also subsidiary, and they are original in creation, so they are not included by subsidiary. But this justification is nothing, because being original in creation does not negate being subsidiary in entering other rulings. Moreover, it is said in al-Hidayah: 'al-umm (mother) linguistically means origin.' Then the entry is essential, not subsidiary. Therefore the first narration is more likely, even if the second is the apparent narration. Then there is another aspect here: if they had sufficed with it, it would be easier – that the apparent [situation] is that a man does not prefer his own life and his children's over his grandchildren's, so they enter by implication of the text; but likewise grandfathers and grandmothers also enter by implication, unless they are mischief-makers with strong opinions, and it is known that the Imam does not grant security to such people, so they are excluded from the aman. Perhaps this is common between them and the grandchildren.
Source pages p. 186

Commentary
This is the first objection (naqd) against the earlier rule. The objector argues: if we apply the same principle to aman, a contradiction arises. When a musta'min says 'grant me security over my children' (bani), does it include grandchildren? If we say only direct children are included, then grandchildren are excluded; but in practice, grandchildren are often included for precaution. However, the word 'bani' is literal for sons, metaphorical for grandsons. So including them would require combining literal and metaphorical – which the Hanafis reject. The response: The inclusion of grandchildren is not by interpreting the word, but by necessity (ihtiyt) to protect blood, because aman is issued on the basis of probability (shubha) and common usage (like 'Banu Hashim') which provides a semblance of literal meaning. The discussion extends to including grandparents under 'parents' – there are two narrations, and the preferred one includes them. The overall reasoning shows that the earlier principle is not contradicted because the inclusion here is based on precaution and implication, not on metaphorical interpretation of the word.

Key Terms
الأمان (al-aman): Security or safe conduct granted to a non-Muslim enemy combatant entering Muslim territory.
شبهة الحقيقة (shubhat al-haqiqa): A semblance or probability of literal meaning, enough to establish a legal effect like aman.
الاحتياط (al-ihtiyat): Precaution; taking the safer course to avoid potential harm, especially in matters of life and blood.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the context of aman, why are grandchildren included under 'bani' according to the answer given?
A) Because the word 'bani' literally means grandchildren in Arabic.
B) Because of precaution (ihtiyat) to protect blood, due to common usage like 'Banu Hashim'.
C) Because the Hanafis allow combining literal and metaphorical meanings in all cases.
D) Because the testator explicitly intended grandchildren.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school resolves objections against its usul principles by using shubhat al-haqiqa and ihtiyat, thereby protecting life without violating the rules of literal interpretation.

The Customary Abandonment of Literal Meaning: Analyzing the Oath on Placing Foot
Analyzes an objection regarding oath interpretation and the principle of customary abandonment.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi usul principle of al-haqiqa al-mahjura to interpret oaths and identify when customary meaning overrides literal meaning.

Core Idea
When a literal meaning is abandoned in custom, the metaphorical meaning governs in oath interpretation.

This passage presents a sophisticated objection in Hanafi usul: if someone swears 'I will not place my foot in your house,' what happens if he enters riding or barefoot? The objection reveals a tension between literal and customary interpretation. The Hanafi resolution introduces the critical concept of al-haqiqa al-mahjura—a literal meaning that has fallen out of customary use. Understanding this principle is essential for correctly interpreting ambiguous legal language.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
State the contradiction that arises when considering both literal and customary interpretations of the oath.
The contradiction: entering riding should not break the oath literally, but it does; entering barefoot should break it literally, and it does.
2
Understand the answer based on customary abandonment
Explain how the literal meaning is abandoned in favor of 'absolute entry'.
The phrase 'placing the foot' has become mahjur in custom; the intended meaning is 'entering'.
3
Examine the nuances from later sources
Analyze the quotes from Fatawa Qadikhan, al-Kashf, and al-Muhit regarding intention and contextual clues.
If the swearer intends the literal meaning, he is believed; the abandonment may be limited.
4
Test the boundary cases
Apply the principle to the scenario of placing foot while lying down and the scenario of intended meaning.
Lying outside with feet inside does not break the oath because 'entry' did not occur.
5
Evaluate the different scholarly positions
Compare the view that the answer is valid vs. the view that the answer requires additional context.
The critique from al-Muhit suggests the answer is not universally valid; it depends on intention and qarina.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم ينقض (ثانياً بالحنث بدخوله راكباً ومنتعلاً في حلفه : لا يضع قدمه في دار فلان مع أنهما غير واضعين القدم في الدار إلا مجازاً (كما يحنث (لو دخل حافياً مع أنه واضع حقيقة فيلزم الجمع (وأجيب) بأنه أريد مطلق الدخول فيتناول العمومه بعض أفراد الحقيقة والمجاز (بهجر الحقيقة عرفاً إلى الدخول مطلقاً والحقيقة المهجورة تترك ويترجح المجاز (حتى لا يحنث لو اضطجع خارجها ووضع قدميه فيها مع أنه واضع حقيقة، كذا في فتاوى قاضيخان، قال في «الكشف» ناقلا عن المبسوط : لو نوى الدخول ماشياً فدخلها راكباً لا يحنث، لأنه نوى حقيقة كلامه وهذه حقيقة غير مهجورة، وعن المحيط»: لو نوى حقيقة وضع القدم لا يحنث بالدخول راكباً لأنه نوى حقيقة كلامه فيصدق قضاء وديانة وعلى هذا لا يصح هذا الجواب، بل يجاب بأن القرينة دلت على أن الهجران للبعض من البيت وهو يمنع مطلق الدخول لا وضع القدم فقط، وأما إذا نوى فعلى ما نوى، لأنه حقيقة الكلام فتدبر، وقد يقال : له حقيقتان عرفيتان الدخول المطلق، وهو الأشهر والدخول ماشياً، والحقيقة اللغوية، لوضع القدم متروكة مهجورة، فلو نوى الدخول ماشياً لا يحنث لأنه نوى الحقيقة العرفية، لكن لعدم شهرتها وشهرة الأولى لا يثبت بدون النية فتأمل فيه
Then it is refuted secondly by the breaking of the oath if he enters riding or wearing shoes while he swore: 'I will not place my foot in so-and-so's house' – even though they are not placing the foot in the house except metaphorically, just as he would break the oath if he entered barefoot while it is a literal placing. So combination would be required. And it is answered: What is intended is absolute entry, so it includes some individuals of the literal and the metaphorical by the general meaning, due to the customary abandonment of the literal meaning in favor of absolute entry, and the abandoned literal meaning is left aside and the metaphorical is preferred. So he does not break the oath if he lies down outside and puts his feet inside, even though it is a literal placing. This is reported in Fatawa Qadikhan. It is said in al-Kashf, quoting from al-Mabsut: If he intended to enter walking, and he entered riding, he does not break the oath, because he intended the literal meaning of his words, and this literal meaning is not abandoned. And from al-Muhit: If he intended the literal meaning of placing the foot, he does not break the oath by entering riding, because he intended the literal meaning of his words, so he is believed both judicially and religiously. Based on this, this answer is not valid. Rather he responds that the contextual clue (qarina) indicated that the abandonment pertains to a part of the house, and it prevents absolute entry, not just placing the foot. As for if he intended [the literal], then it is according to his intention, because it is the literal meaning of his words. So consider. And it may be said: He has two customary realities – absolute entry (which is more famous) and entry walking; and the linguistic reality of placing the foot is abandoned. So if he intended entry walking, he does not break the oath because he intended the customary reality. But due to its lack of fame and the fame of the first, it is not established without the intention. So ponder.
Source pages p. 187

Commentary
This is the second objection: an oath not to place one's foot in a house. If the swearer enters riding (or wearing shoes), according to the earlier principle, he would not have 'placed his foot' literally, so he should not break the oath. But in fact he does break the oath, and he also breaks it if he enters barefoot (literal placing). This creates a contradiction (al-jam') between literal and metaphorical. The answer is that the phrase 'placing the foot' has become abandoned in common usage for the meaning of 'absolute entry'. Thus the oath is understood as an oath not to enter, whether by foot or by riding. The literal meaning (placing the foot) is abandoned (mahjur). Therefore, if he enters riding, he breaks the oath; if he lies outside and puts his feet inside, he does not break it (because entry has not occurred). The text then discusses nuances from Fatawa Qadikhan and other sources: if he intends the literal meaning specifically, then he is believed according to his intention, but generally the customary meaning prevails.

Key Terms
الحقيقة المهجورة (al-haqiqa al-mahjura): The literal meaning that has fallen out of customary use and is abandoned in favor of a metaphorical or customary meaning.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If someone swears 'I will not place my foot in Zayd's house' and then lies down outside but puts his feet inside the house, according to the passage, what is the ruling?
A) He breaks the oath because he literally placed his foot inside.
B) He does not break the oath because the oath is understood as a prohibition of entry, not literal foot placement.
C) It depends on his intention at the time of the oath.
D) The oath is invalid because the wording is problematic.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, a literal meaning that is customarily abandoned (al-haqiqa al-mahjura) is superseded by the customary meaning, but this may be overridden by the swearer's intention or contextual clues.

Interpreting Possessives in Oaths: Al-Ikhtisas al-Mutlaq
Examines an oath about 'his house' and rented residence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi concept of al-ikhtisas al-mutlaq to interpret possessives in legal oaths and identify the resulting ikhtilaf between Qadikhan and al-Sarakhsi.

Core Idea
In oaths, 'my house' means what you are connected to—residence or ownership—determined by context.

The third objection in the text asks: how can an oath not to enter 'my house' be broken by entering a rented residence, which is only metaphorically 'mine'? The answer lies in the Hanafi usul principle that the possessive (idafa) denotes absolute connection (al-ikhtisas al-mutlaq), not just ownership. This lesson unpacks that principle and shows how it resolves the apparent contradiction, while also exploring a nuanced disagreement between two Hanafi masters.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Objection
The objector argues that an oath not to enter 'my house' (dari) should not be broken by entering a rented residence because the possessive literally means ownership. Yet both a rented residence and an owned-and-occupied house break the oath, forcing a combination of literal and metaphorical meanings.
2
Unpack the Hanafi Response
The Hanafi response introduces al-ikhtisas al-mutlaq (absolute connection) as the meaning of the possessive. This is supported by the contextual clue (qarinat al-hajran) that a person abandons a house only due to aversion—so the house one lives in is 'his house' by connection, whether owned or rented.
Connection includes both residence and ownership, so the rented house is included literally or by the generality of metaphor.
3
Analyze the Dispute
Qadikhan holds that absolute connection includes even an owned but unoccupied house, so the oath is broken. Al-Sarakhsi disagrees, arguing that the primary understanding (mubadar) is residence, so an unoccupied owned house does not break the oath.
The dispute hinges on the weight given to the contextual clue: does it broaden the meaning or restrict it?
4
Derive the Thamarah al-Khilaf
The practical difference: if one swears not to enter 'my house' and enters an owned house he never resides in, Qadikhan says he breaks the oath; Sarakhsi says he does not. The text advises the reader to ponder (fata'ammal), implying no explicit tarjih.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
و ينقض (ثالثاً بالحنث بدخول دار سكناه إجارة في حلفه لا يدخل داره مع أن الإضافة حقيقة في الملك، فدار السكني داره مجازاً، ويحنث أيضاً بدار مسكونة مملوكة له وهو داره حقيقة فيلزم الجمع (وأجيب بأن الإضافة للاختصاص المطلق إما حقيقة أو مجازاً بدلالة القرينة هي أن الرجل لا يهجر الدار إلا للنفرة المالك (وهو) أي الاختصاص (يعم السكنى والملك فحينئذٍ يتناول المسكونة المملوكة عن وغيرها بطريق الحقيقة أو عموم المجاز فلا ،جمع وإذا أريد مطلق الاختصاص (فيحنث بمملوكة غير مسكونة) أي بدخوله فيها، لأن له أيضاً اختصاصاً به كقاضيخان) أي كما يقول به الإمام فخر الدين قاضيخان (خلافاً للسرخسي الإمام شمس الأئمة، فإنه عنده يتبادر الاختصاص بالسكنى سواء كان مع الملك أم لا بقرينة الهجران فلا يحنث بالدخول في دار مملوكة غير مسكونة فتدبر
And it is refuted thirdly by the breaking of the oath if he enters a house he is residing in by rent while he swore 'I will not enter my house' – even though the possessive (idafa) is literal for ownership, so the rented house is 'his house' metaphorically; and he also breaks the oath with a house he owns and resides in, which is 'his house' literally. So combination would be required. And it is answered: The possessive indicates absolute connection (al-ikhtisas al-mutlaq), either literally or metaphorically, indicated by the contextual clue (qarina) that a man does not abandon a house except due to aversion. And connection encompasses both residence and ownership. Therefore it includes the owned and resided one and others by way of literalness or generality of metaphor, so no combination. And if absolute connection is intended, then he breaks the oath with an owned but not resided house (i.e., by entering it), because he also has a connection to it. This is according to Qadikhan, as Imam Fakhr al-Din Qadikhan holds, contrary to al-Sarakhsi, Imam Shams al-A'imma, for according to him, the immediate understanding (mubadar) is the connection of residence whether with ownership or not, due to the contextual clue of abandonment, so he does not break the oath by entering an owned but not resided house. So ponder.
Source pages p. 187

Commentary
This is the third objection: an oath not to enter 'my house' (dari). The testator enters a house he rents for residence. The word 'my' literally denotes ownership, so the rented house is only metaphorically his. Yet he breaks the oath, and he also breaks it if he enters a house he owns and resides in (literal). Again, combination of literal and metaphorical would be needed. The answer is that the possessive (idafa) denotes absolute connection (ikhtisas mutlaq), not just ownership. The contextual clue is that a person abandons a house only when he feels aversion (nafra), so the word 'his house' refers to whatever house he has a connection to, whether by residence or ownership or both. Thus entering a rented residence breaks the oath. The text notes a difference between Qadikhan and Sarakhsi: Qadikhan holds that even an owned but not resided house is included (since connection exists), while Sarakhsi says the primary understanding (mubadar) is connection by residence, so entering an unoccupied owned house does not break the oath.

Key Terms
الاختصاص المطلق (al-ikhtisas al-mutlaq): Absolute connection or special relation, broader than ownership or tenancy alone.
قرينة الهجران (qarinat al-hajran): Contextual clue that a person abandons a house due to aversion; indicates the house is where he resides.
الإضافة (al-idafa): The possessive construction in Arabic grammar (e.g., dari = 'my house').
الحنث (al-hinth): Breaking an oath; incurring the obligation of expiation (kaffarah).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because the oath is invalid anyway.
B) Because the possessive indicates absolute connection, which includes the rented residence as his house by contextual clue.
C) Because the oath was not serious.
D) Because ownership is transferred by rent.

Key Takeaway
The interpretation of possessives in oaths relies on the concept of absolute connection (al-ikhtisas al-mutlaq), which is broader than literal ownership and is determined by contextual clues, leading to legitimate disagreement among Hanafi scholars.

Determining the Meaning of 'Yawm' Through the Contained Action
Fourth objection and its resolution via contextual determination

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi usul principle that a time adverb's meaning is determined by the nature of the contained action (extended vs non-extended).

Core Idea
When 'yawm' is an adverb for a non-extended action, it means absolute time, not daytime.

This passage addresses a classic objection: if someone says 'You are free on the day Zayd arrives' and Zayd arrives at night, is the slave free? The literal meaning of 'day' seems to contradict. The Hanafi usul answer reveals a key principle: the contained action (mazruf) determines the intended meaning of a time adverb.

Guided Path
1
Identify the linguistic ambiguity
Recognize that 'yawm' has two senses: literal daytime and absolute time (24-hour period or moment). The objection arises from assuming only the literal sense.
2
Classify the nature of the contained action
Determine whether the action (mazruf) is extended (e.g., riding, sitting) or non-extended (e.g., arrival, emancipation). Contracts often involve non-extended actions.
3
Apply the Hanafi rule for time adverbs
If the action is extended, 'yawm' means daytime; if non-extended, it means absolute time. This resolves the apparent contradiction.
The preposition 'fi' necessitates encompassment, which is only possible if the action can be stretched over the daytime.
4
Understand the meta-linguistic preference
The correct view (from al-Kashf) prefers treating 'yawm' for absolute time as metaphorical (majaz) over being a shared term (mushtarak). This avoids ambiguity.
5
Apply to new examples
Given a conditional like 'I will go on the day you come', if the coming is instantaneous, 'day' means the moment of coming, regardless of day or night.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
و ينقض رابعاً بعتق عبده في إضافته) أي العتق (إلى يوم يقدم فلان فقدم ليلاً) مع أنه ليس نهاراً حقيقة، كما يحنث لو قدم نهاراً فيلزم الجمع وأجيب بأن اليوم شائع في مطلق الوقت فأريد به ذلك، وتحقيق الجواب أن اليوم شائع في بياض النهار وحقيقة فيه بالاتفاق ويجيء لمطلق الوقت، فعند البعض فيه حقيقة أيضاً، وعلى هذا فليس مما نحن فيه فلا إيراد أصلاً، وعند الأكثر مجاز فيه، وفي الكشف وهو الأصح ترجيحاً للمجاز على الاشتراك، ثم إنه إن وقع ظرفاً لفعل ممتد كالركوب والجلوس، أي ما يقدر بالمدة عرفاً یراد به بياض النهار، وإذا وقع ظرفاً لفعل غير ممتد فلمطلق الوقت فالاعتبار في هذا للظروف دون المضاف إليه كما توهم عبارة البعض صرح بذلك في الكشف فالمظروف إذن قرينة تعيين المراد بحيث لا ينتقل الذهن معه غير ممتد إلا إلى مطلق الوقت، وممتداً إلى بياض النهار، وقد يؤيد بأن تقدير في يوجب الاستيعاب وههنا لما كان في مقدر أوجب استيعابه للمظروف فإذا كان ممتداً فيمكن استيعاب النهار إياه، فأمكن المعنى الحقيقي، فيحمل عليه
And it is refuted fourthly by the emancipation of his slave when he attaches it – i.e. the emancipation – to the day so-and-so arrives, and he arrives at night – even though night is not daytime literally, just as he would break [the conditional] if he arrived in the daytime, so combination would be required. And it is answered: The word 'day' (yawm) is common to absolute time, and intended here as such. The verification of the answer is that 'day' is common for white daylight and is literal there by consensus, and it also comes for absolute time. Some hold it is also literal for absolute time; on this basis, it is not from our subject, so no objection at all. According to the majority, it is metaphorical for absolute time. In al-Kashf, the correct view is to prefer the metaphorical over the shared (mushtarak). Then, if it occurs as a time adverb for an extended action (something measured by duration, like riding or sitting), it means the white daylight. If it occurs as a time adverb for a non-extended action, it means absolute time. The consideration here is for the contained (mazruf) not the container (mudaf ilayh), as some expressions suggest. This is clarified in al-Kashf. Thus the contained is the contextual clue that determines the intended meaning, such that the mind does not move from it for non-extended actions except to absolute time, and for extended actions to white daylight. This is supported by the fact that the preposition 'fi' (in) necessitates encompassment; here, since the action is measured (muqaddar), it necessitates its encompassment of the contained. If the action is extended, it is possible for the daytime to encompass it, so the literal meaning is possible, so it is interpreted accordingly.
Source pages p. 187

Commentary
This is the fourth objection: a man emancipates his slave 'on the day so-and-so arrives', and the person arrives at night. The literal meaning of 'day' is daytime; night is not day. So if the emancipation is conditional on arrival, it should only take effect if arrival occurs during daytime. Yet in practice, the emancipation is considered to take effect even if arrival is at night. This would require combining literal and metaphorical (day for daytime and day for the whole 24-hour period). The answer is that the word 'yawm' (day) is used for absolute time (muthlaq al-waqt) in many contexts. The detailed analysis: when 'day' is used as a time adverb (zarf) for an action that is not extended (like immediate arrival), it means absolute time (the moment); when used for an extended action (like riding or sitting), it means the actual daytime. Here the action (arrival) is not extended, so 'day' means the time of arrival, whether day or night. Therefore no combination is needed. The text notes that some scholars consider 'day' to be literally for absolute time, avoiding the objection entirely; but the more correct view is to consider it metaphorical, and the contextual clue (the nature of the action) determines which sense is intended.

Key Terms
المظروف (al-mazruf): The contained action; here, the action that 'day' is an adverb for (e.g., arrival, riding).
اليوم (al-yawm): Can mean either the daylight period or a full 24-hour day; determined by context.
الاشتراك (al-ishtirak): A word that has two or more literal meanings (shared term).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Only the daytime period.
B) Absolute time (the moment of the action).
C) It becomes void because of ambiguity.
D) It requires a clarifying intention.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi usul principle that a time adverb's meaning is determined by the nature of the contained action (mazruf) resolves apparent contradictions in conditional statements.

Interpreting 'Day' in Oaths: The Extended vs. Non-Extended Action Rule
Resolving objection about oath 'on the day' when arrival occurs at night.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that when 'yawm' is used as an adverbial for a non-extended action, it denotes absolute time, and derive the ruling for whether the oath is broken by a night event.

Core Idea
The nature of the action (extended or instantaneous) decides whether 'day' means daytime or includes night.

A common objection in usul is how to interpret the word 'yawm' in oaths. If someone swears 'my slave is free on the day Zayd arrives' and Zayd arrives at night, does the oath break? The literal meaning of 'yawm' is daytime, but Hanafis argue that for a non-extended action like arrival, the word is used metaphorically for absolute time. This lesson unpacks the linguistic and logical reasoning behind this rule, showing how the contained action (mazruf) serves as a contextual indicator (qarīna) to disambiguate temporal adverbs.

Guided Path
1
Identify the linguistic ambiguity of 'yawm'
Recognize that 'yawm' has a literal meaning (daytime) and a metaphorical meaning (absolute time). The Hanafi rule selects between them based on the action's duration.
The passage notes some scholars treat the absolute sense as literal, but the majority (and the relied-upon view) treat it as metaphorical.
2
Classify the action as extended or non-extended
Determine whether the action attached to 'yawm' is customarily extended (e.g., riding, sitting) or instantaneous (e.g., arrival, freeing). This classification is based on custom (urf), not strict logic.
Extended actions can be measured by duration; non-extended actions occur at a single moment.
3
Apply the encompassment test using the particle 'fi'
The preposition 'fi' (in) implies encompassing. If 'yawm' is the container, its meaning must be able to encompass the contained action. Extended actions fit within daytime; non-extended actions do not, so they require the broader meaning of absolute time.
This is a Hanafi-specific linguistic analysis; other schools may differ.
4
Recognize the contained action (mazruf) as a qarīna
The nature of the verb (mazruf) becomes a contextual indicator that diverts from the literal meaning. For non-extended actions, the qarīna is strong enough to override the literal priority.
This refutes the objection that the literal meaning always takes precedence.
5
Apply the rule to the given oath example
In the oath 'my slave is free on the day Zayd arrives,' 'arrival' is non-extended, so 'yawm' means absolute time. Therefore, if Zayd arrives at night, the oath is broken.
The alternative answer based on 'joy' is specific to that example and not the general rule.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(و) ينقض رابعاً بعتق عبده في إضافته) أي العتق (إلى يوم يقدم فلان فقدم ليلاً) مع أنه ليس نهاراً حقيقة، كما يحنث لو قدم نهاراً فيلزم الجمع وأجيب بأن اليوم شائع في مطلق الوقت فأريد به ذلك، وتحقيق الجواب أن اليوم شائع في بياض النهار وحقيقة فيه بالاتفاق ويجيء لمطلق الوقت، فعند البعض فيه حقيقة أيضاً، وعلى هذا فليس مما نحن فيه فلا إيراد أصلاً، وعند الأكثر مجاز فيه، وفي الكشف وهو الأصح ترجيحاً للمجاز على الاشتراك، ثم إنه إن وقع ظرفاً لفعل ممتد كالركوب والجلوس، أي ما يقدر بالمدة عرفاً یراد به بياض النهار، وإذا وقع ظرفاً لفعل غير ممتد فلمطلق الوقت فالاعتبار في هذا للظروف دون المضاف إليه كما توهم عبارة البعض صرح بذلك في الكشف فالمظروف إذن قرينة تعيين المراد بحيث لا ينتقل الذهن معه غير ممتد إلا إلى مطلق الوقت، وممتداً إلى بياض النهار، وقد يؤيد بأن تقدير في يوجب الاستيعاب وههنا لما كان في مقدر أوجب استيعابه للمظروف فإذا كان ممتداً فيمكن استيعاب النهار إياه، فأمكن المعنى الحقيقي، فيحمل عليه للأصالة، وأما إذا كان غير ممتد فلا يمكن استيعاب النهار إياه فلا يحمل عليه، بل على مطلق الوقت الأعم من أجزائه وأجزاء الليل والعلاقة العموم فإن مطلق الوقت عام من النهار، وهذا يرشدك أيضاً إلى أن العبرة لعامله المظروف لا لما أضيف إليه وعلى ما قررنا لا يتوجه ما أورد المصنف بقوله : (أقول الحقيقة المستعملة عنده أولى من المجاز المتعارف) فلا ينفع الشيوع، فينبغي أن يحمل على بياض النهار، وذلك لأن أولوية الحقيقة المستعملة عنده إذا لم تدل قرينة سوى الشهرة على إرادة المجاز وههنا عدم امتداد المظروف قرينة عليها، ولا يتوجه أيضاً ما أورده الشيخ الهداد وارتكب لدفعه تكلفات من أن الحمل على المجاز لا بد له من قرينة صارفة، وههنا حملوا بنفس الملاءمة، فإن غير الممتد إنما يلائمه مطلق الوقت، وذلك لأنا بينا أن عدم امتداد المظروف قرينة صارفة عن إرادة بياض النهار، فافهم، ولا يتوجه أيضاً أن الحقيقة لا تحتاج إلى قرينة فلم احتاجوا إلى بيانها، وذلك لأنهم إنما يحتاجون إلى نفي القرينة الصارفة عنها، وأما الحمل عليها فللأصالة فافهم، وإذ لم يتم هذا الجواب عنده أشار إلى جواب آخر وقال (فالأولى) أن يقال (إنه ) أي اليمين مسوق (للسرور) بقدوم فلان (فلا يختص بالبياض هذا مختص بهذا المثال، وأما إذا قال: أنت حرّ يوم أموت، فلا سرور فيه إلاّ أن يقال إنما ذكر السرور مثلاً لتحققه في خصوص هذا المثال، والمراد القرينة الجزئية مطلقاً، وههنا إرادة القربة قرينة فلا يختص بالنهار فتدبر
And it is objected (fourthly) by the freeing of his slave in its attachment (i.e., the freeing) to the day when so-and-so arrives, and he arrived at night, while it is not daytime literally, just as he breaks the oath if he arrived during the day, so combination would be required. And it is answered: that 'day' is common for absolute time, so that is intended. The verification of the answer is that 'day' is common for the whiteness of daytime and literal in it by consensus, and it also comes for absolute time. According to some, it is also literal for absolute time, and on this it is not from our subject, so there is no objection at all. According to the majority, it is metaphorical for absolute time. In al-Kashf, it is the most correct, giving preference to metaphor over polysemy. Then, if it occurs as an adverbial for an extended action like riding and sitting, i.e., what is customarily measured by duration, it means the whiteness of daytime; and if it occurs as an adverbial for a non-extended action, then it means absolute time. So the consideration in this is for the adverbial [itself] and not for the word it is attached to, contrary to what some phrases suggest. This is stated explicitly in al-Kashf. Thus the contained [action] is then a contextual indicator determining the intended meaning such that the mind does not move with it—if non-extended—except to absolute time, and if extended to the whiteness of daytime. And it may be supported by the fact that the particle 'fi' requires encompassment, and here since it is in an estimated [particle], it necessitates encompassing the contained [action]. So if it is extended, it is possible for daytime to encompass it, so the literal meaning is possible, so it is interpreted accordingly due to the principle of originality. As for if it is non-extended, it is not possible for daytime to encompass it, so it is not interpreted accordingly, but rather as absolute time, which is more general than its parts and parts of the night. The relationship is generality, because absolute time is general than daytime. This also guides you that the criterion is for the factor, the contained action, not for that to which it is added. And according to what we have established, the criticism raised by the author in his saying: 'I say the literal meaning used by him is more deserving than the common metaphor' does not apply. So the prevalence does not help, and it should be interpreted as the whiteness of daytime. That is because the priority of the literal meaning as used by him applies only when there is no contextual indicator other than fame indicating the intention of metaphor, and here the non-extension of the contained action is an indicator. And also the criticism raised by Shaykh al-Haddad (and he undertook unnecessary complications to refute it) that the interpretation as metaphor requires a diverting qarina, while they interpreted by mere suitability does not apply, because non-extended is only suitable for absolute time. That is because we have shown that the non-extension of the contained action is a diverting qarina away from intending the whiteness of daytime. So understand. And also the criticism that the literal does not need a qarina, so why did they need to explain it? That is because they only need to negate the diverting qarina from it; the interpretation as literal is due to the principle of originality. So understand. And since this answer was not satisfactory to him, he indicated another answer and said: 'The better [answer] is to say that the oath is motivated by joy at the arrival of so-and-so, so it is not specific to daytime.' This is specific to this example. As for if he says: You are free the day I die, there is no joy in that, except that it may be said that he mentioned joy as an example for its actuality in this particular example, and the intended is the partial contextual indicator in general. And here the intention of drawing closer to Allah is a qarina, so it is not specific to daytime. So reflect.
Source pages p. 187

Commentary
This is a usul rule passage resolving an objection about the word 'day' in oaths. The objector says: if someone says 'my slave is free on the day Zayd arrives,' and Zayd arrives at night, does the oath break? Literal 'day' means daytime, but night is not day. Yet if he arrives during the day, it breaks. So the same phrase would be sometimes literal, sometimes metaphorical? The Hanafi answer unfolds in several layers. First, 'day' (yawm) has multiple meanings: it literally means daylight by consensus, and also can mean absolute time (including night) by metaphor or some say literally. The majority treat the absolute sense as metaphorical. Then a rule is introduced: if 'day' is used as an adverb for an extended action (like riding, sitting), it means daytime; if for a non-extended action (like arriving, which is instantaneous), it means absolute time. The rationale is that the preposition 'fi' (in) implies encompassment; a non-extended action cannot be encompassed by daytime, so it must refer to absolute time. This is supported by the principle that the contained action (mazruf) serves as a contextual indicator. Further, the passage refutes objections: that the literal meaning is always preferable is countered because here the non-extension is a qarina diverting from the literal. Finally, an alternative answer is offered: the oath's purpose is joy at arrival, so it includes night because joy is not confined to daytime. However, this is specific to the example; for oaths like 'You are free the day I die,' the qarina is seeking reward, which also includes night. This illustrates the Hanafi method of using contextual indicators (qara'in) to interpret ambiguous temporal expressions in oaths.

Key Terms
المظروف (al-mazruf): The contained action that fills the time adverbial, used as a contextual indicator (qarina) to disambiguate the meaning of the time word.
قرينة صارفة (qarīna ṣārifa): A contextual indicator that diverts from the literal meaning to a metaphorical or broader meaning.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi rule in the passage, if someone swears 'My slave is free the day Zayd arrives' and Zayd arrives at night, is the oath broken?
A) Yes, because 'day' literally includes night.
B) No, because arrival is non-extended, so 'day' means absolute time including night.
C) Yes, because the oath is motivated by joy which is not confined to daytime.
D) No, because the literal meaning of 'day' is always daytime.

Key Takeaway
When 'day' (yawm) is used as an adverbial for a non-extended action, it is interpreted as absolute time (including night) due to the impossibility of encompassment, and the contained action serves as a contextual indicator.
