The Kalima Problem: Linguistic Analysis and Five Hanafi Resolutions
Analyzing the linguistic problem of the kalima and providing five scholarly answers

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the linguistic problem in 'La ilaha illa Allah', explain five Hanafi resolutions, and evaluate how each preserves tawhid through usul principles.

Core Idea
The kalima's perfection requires careful analysis: neither 'existent' nor 'possible' alone suffices; five resolutions ingeniously reconcile language and theology.

The kalima 'La ilaha illa Allah' is the foundation of tawhid, yet a straightforward reading presents a subtle problem: the implied predicate seems to either fail to negate possible gods or fail to affirm Allah's existence. Hanafi scholars, deeply engaged in usul al-fiqh and linguistics, have proposed five distinct resolutions that draw on the custom of the Lawgiver, self-evident truths, grammatical analysis, and necessary existence. This lesson walks through each resolution, revealing the depth of Hanafi method in defending the core creed.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Linguistic Problem
Understand the two possible implied predicates for the noun of 'la al-nafi lil-jins' in the kalima: either 'existent' or 'possible'. Each leads to a deficiency: 'existent' does not negate the possibility of another god; 'possible' does not affirm Allah's necessary existence.
This sets up the dilemma that the five answers aim to resolve.
2
Analyze the First Resolution: Custom of the Lawgiver
The first answer relies on the custom (urf) of the Lawgiver (shar'), which combines both meanings: the kalima negates any god that is both possible and existent except Allah, who is necessary existent. This avoids the need to choose one predicate.
Uses usul principle that shari' terms have specific meanings.
3
Analyze the Second Resolution: Self-Evident Existence
Some Hanafis argue that Allah's existence is self-evident (badihi) to rational minds; the kalima only intends to negate partners. Thus the predicate 'possible' suffices because existence is conceded and not the focus.
Appeals to innate human reason, reducing the kalima's rhetorical purpose.
4
Analyze the Third Resolution: Zamakhshari's No-Predicate
Zamakhshari proposes that the kalima needs no implied predicate; it is originally 'Allah is God' with 'la' and 'illa' for restriction. The negation operates on the entire phrase. But this still faces the same problem and is criticized.
Highlights grammatical alternative but deemed insufficient by the author.
5
Analyze the Fourth and Fifth Resolutions: Necessary Existence and Necessary Divinity
The fourth answer uses the concept that what is possible for the Necessary Being is actually necessary, so implying 'possible' yields existence. The fifth answer states that divinity is inherently necessary (essential, not contingent), so 'existent' implies impossibility of change. Both achieve complete tawhid.
These draw on rational theology (kalam) to resolve the dilemma.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

(و) يجاب (ثانياً) كما هو منقول عن بعض الحنفية أن وجوده تعالى (تقرر في بداهة العقول) لأن المنكر لم يكن دهرياً (والمقصود منه (نفي الشريك لأن المخاطب مشرك، فإذن يختار أن المقدر الإمكان، وصلوح الوجود فيلزم منه نفي إمكان إله سواه تعالى، وأما وجوده تعالى فلكونه مسلماً لا يحتاج إلى التنبيه فتأمل فيه (و) يجاب (ثالثاً) كما هو منقول (عن الزمخشري بأن لا حاجة ههنا إلى الخبر بل أصل التركيب الله إله وهو المقصود (فدخل) عليه (لا وإلا للحصر ) أي لحصر الألوهية فيه تعالى (فالمسند إليه هو الله، والمسند هو إله) وهذا الجواب بابداء شق ثالث بأنه لا حاجة إلى تقدير الخبر (وهذا الكلام (مما يتعجب منه) فإنهم يعدونه ماهراً بالعربية ذا يد طولى فيها كيف لا يتعجب منه فإن الاستثناء يقتضي الحكم في الصدر (بالضرورة) ولعله بنى كلامه على لغة من ينفي خبر لا التي لنفي الجنس، ومقصوده أن المعنى انتفى الاله الموصوف بالألوهية إلا الله الموصوف بها وحينئذ لا وجه لهذا الاستبعاد، لكن يرد عليه شيء آخر وهو أنه لا يلزم منه نفي إمكان الغير، فالإشكال كما كان، ولك أن تقول: إن لا التي لنفي الجنس تفيد نفي الجنس في حد نفسه وهو الامتناع والاستثناء منه وهو وجوده، في نفسه بنفسه، فيفيد وجود المستثنى ولا يحتاج إلى الخبر فتدبر (وما قيل في تصحيحه لو بدل لا وإلا بإنما) وقيل : إنما الإله الله لكان كلاماً تاماً البتة من غير تقدير، وإنما هو النفي وكلمة (إلا) أي ليس مفادها إلا مفاد لا وإلا، فلا وإلا أيضاً لا يحتاج إلى الخبر (فأقول مدفوع) هذا القول بأن المراد من قولهم إنما كلا وإلا أن حاصله في التخصيص) والقصر (كلا وإلا فالملازمة بين تمامية الكلام لا وإلا بين بين تماميته من إنما (ممنوعة) كما لا يخفى (و) يجاب (رابعاً كما أقول مما حقق في الكلام إن ما يمكن للواجب بالإمكان العام فهو ضروري، فيلزم من الإمكان الوجود أي يلزم من إمكان وجود الواجب وجوده بالضرورة، فلنا أن نختار تقدير الإمكان، ويلزم وجوده تعالى بالوجوب ويلزم من عدمه عدمه أي من عدم الوجود عدم الإمكان، فلنا أن نختار تقدير الوجود، ونقول ما انتفى وجود إله سواه انتفى إمكانه، لأن الموصوف بالألوهية لا يكون ممكناً مخلوقاً البتة بالضرورة ونبه عليه في علم الكلام أيضاً، وهذا الجواب بالآخرة يؤول إلى أن نفي الإمكان يفهم من خارج، وإنما المقصود منه نفي الإله سوى الله تعالى ردّاً لزعم الحمقاء المشركين فتأمل (و) يجاب (خامساً: إن مطلقات الإلهيات ضرورية للتعالي عن التغير والتبدل) فإن الإله ليس من شأنه أن يوجد تارة ويعدم أخرى فيكون (الإيجاب هناك ضرورياً كالسلب فهذه القضايا وإن كانت مطلقات صورة ضرورية معنى فنختار تقدير الوجود، والمعنى: لا إله موجود بالضرورة إلا الله بالضرورة، فلزم امتناع إله آخر غير الله سبحانه ووجوبه تعالى وتم التوحيد (فتدبر).
Then here are benefits: The first benefit: Concerning the word of tawhid, there is a famous problem. The implied predicate (for the noun of la al-nafi lil-jins) is either 'existent', so the meaning is 'There is no god except Allah', but this does not necessitate the impossibility of a god other than Allah, so complete tawhid is not achieved. Or it is 'possible', so the meaning is 'There is no possible god by the general possibility restricted to existence except Allah', but this does not necessitate His existence, so it does not benefit tawhid at all. First answer: as transmitted from the commentator of al-Mukhtasar, that the word of tawhid is based on the custom of the Lawgiver, so you may choose either: if you wish, imply 'existent', and if you wish, imply 'possible', and say: the meaning is not what was mentioned; rather the custom of the Lawgiver established that the meaning is 'There is no god that is possible and existent except Allah', for He is existent and necessary. And note that the custom of the Lawgiver is contingent. Second answer: as transmitted from some Hanafis, that His existence is established in the basic rationality because the denier was not a materialist, and the purpose is to negate partners, because the address is to polytheists. So we choose to imply possibility and suitability for existence, so it necessitates the negation of the possibility of a god other than Him. As for His existence, it is conceded and does not need attention. Third answer: as transmitted from al-Zamakhshari, that there is no need here for a predicate; rather the original composition is 'Allah is God', and that is the intended meaning. Then 'la' and 'illa' enter upon it for restriction. So the subject is Allah, and the predicate is God. This answer presents a third way: that there is no need to imply a predicate. This statement is astonishing, for they consider him skilled in Arabic. How can they not be astonished? For exception necessitates a ruling in the first part necessarily. Perhaps he based his speech on the language of those who negate the predicate of la al-nafi lil-jins. His intent is that the meaning is the negation of the god described with divinity except Allah described with it. Then there is no ground for this unlikelihood, but another objection arises: it does not necessitate the negation of the possibility of another, so the problem remains as it was. But you may say: la al-nafi lil-jins indicates the negation of the genus itself, which is impossibility, and the exception from it is its existence itself; so it indicates the existence of the excepted and does not need the predicate. Fourth answer: as I say from what is established in theology, that whatever is possible for the Necessary Being by general possibility is necessary. So from the possibility of existence, existence follows necessarily. So we may choose to imply possibility, and His existence follows necessarily. And from its non-existence, its non-existence follows, i.e., from non-existence, non-possibility. So we may choose to imply existence, and say: what negated the existence of a god other than Him negated its possibility, because that described with divinity cannot possibly be created at all, necessarily. And it is also pointed out in theology. This answer ultimately leads to the understanding that the negation of possibility is understood externally, and the purpose is only to negate a god other than Allah, in refutation of the foolish polytheists. Fifth answer: The universal propositions of divinity are necessary due to transcendence above change and alteration. For a god is not such that it exists once and ceases to exist another time. So affirmation there is necessary like negation. These propositions, although universal in form, are necessary in meaning. So we choose to imply existence, and the meaning is 'There is no god existing necessarily except Allah necessarily.' So the impossibility of another god besides Allah and His existence are necessitated, and tawhid is complete. Reflect.
Source pages p. 338

Commentary
This passage discusses the famous linguistic and theological problem in the kalima 'La ilaha illa Allah'. The problem is that if the implied predicate is 'existent', the statement does not negate the possibility of other gods; if it is 'possible', it does not affirm Allah's existence. The author presents five answers from various scholars, demonstrating the depth of Hanafi usul in analyzing language. The first answer relies on the custom of the Lawgiver, the second on the self-evident nature of Allah's existence, the third by Zamakhshari reinterprets the structure without an implied predicate, the fourth uses necessary-existence logic, and the fifth posits that divinity is inherently necessary. The passage emphasizes that the kalima, when properly understood, perfectly establishes tawhid by negating any possibility of other gods and affirming Allah's necessary existence.

Key Terms
التوحيد (al-tawhid): The oneness of Allah, the central concept that the kalima aims to establish.
الاستثناء (al-istithna'): Exception, a grammatical and legal tool that makes an exception from a general statement.
الخبر (al-khabar): Predicate, the part of a nominal sentence that gives information about the subject.
عرف الشارع (urf al-shar'): The custom of the Lawgiver (the Shari'ah's usage of terms).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the fourth answer in the text, what follows from the possibility of existence for the Necessary Being?
A) That existence is contingent
B) That existence is necessary
C) That existence is impossible
D) That existence is possible

Key Takeaway
The kalima, when properly analyzed using Hanafi usul principles, perfectly establishes tawhid by negating all possible rivals and affirming Allah's necessary existence.

Istithna' as Indication and Explicit Statement in Hanafi Usul
Explains that exception indicates a contrary ruling incidentally, not primarily.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify whether a given istithna' construction conveys its ruling as 'ishara or 'ibara and explain the Hanafi criterion.

Core Idea
Exception indicates a contrary ruling incidentally, not by primary intent, unless both parts are equally intended.

This passage from 'Fawatih al-Rahamut' delves into the nature of exception (istithna') in Hanafi usul. The key question: when we say 'X except Y', is the ruling about Y the main point or just an implication? The verified Hanafis position that istithna' primarily functions as an indication ('ishara) — the contrary ruling is entailed incidentally, not directly intended. However, in certain contexts like the kalima, both parts are equally intended, making it explicit ('ibara). Understanding this distinction is crucial for correctly interpreting legal statements involving exception.

Guided Path
1
Understand the two modes of istithna'
Recognize that in Hanafi usul, istithna' can convey the ruling of the excepted item in two ways: as an indication ('ishara) where the contrary ruling is incidental, or as an explicit statement ('ibara) where both affirmation and negation are equally intended.
The passage uses the analogy of a limit (ghaya) to illustrate that exception marks the end of the first part's ruling without directly stating the opposite.
2
Apply to explicit examples
Analyze the example 'Aliya 'asharatun illa thalatha' (I owe ten except three). The primary intent is to affirm seven; the negation of three is incidental. This is the default case of 'ishara.
Contrast with 'La ilaha illa Allah' where both affirmation and negation are intended — this is 'ibara.
3
Recognize the special case of istithna' mufaragh
Identify the empty exception (istithna' mufaragh) where the first part is negative, e.g., 'Ma anta illa hurr' (You are nothing but free). Here the excepted item's ruling (affirmation of freedom) is the primary intent, not incidental.
This shows that the default 'ishara can be reversed when the construction is designed to highlight the excepted item.
4
Address the objection of Sadr al-Shari'ah
Understand Sadr al-Shari'ah's objection that the incidental view requires a specific theory of predication (mentioning the whole then ruling on a part). The passage refutes this by showing that the longer expression (mentioning the whole then excluding a part) inherently signals the exception's ruling as an indication.
This step deepens understanding of the underlying logic of exception in legal language.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفائدة (الثانية : الحنفية المحققون الذين وافقوا الجمهور) في أن الاستثناء يفيد الحكم المخالف في المستثنى قالوا : الحكم الذي بعد إلا إشارة لأنه أي الاستثناء (بمنزلة الغاية) فإنها لانتهاء حكم الصدر ، وتفيد عدم دخول ما بعده فيما قبله كذلك الاستثناء وغاية الوجود عدم وبالعكس فلزم فيه الحكم المخالف (إلا) أن الصدر ثابت قصداً وهذا لا بل تبعاً، فيكون إشارة (والأوجه) على ما في «التحرير» أن هذا ليس على الإطلاق بل (أنه إشارة مرة) إن لم يكن مقصوداً (نحو : علي عشرة إلا ثلاثة ، لأن المقصود منه (سبعة) أي الإقرار به، وأما نفي ما زاد فيلزم تبعاً (و) أنه (عبارة) ومقصود مرة أخرى ككلمة التوحيد، فإن الإثبات والنفي) المفهومين فيها (كلاهما مقصودان) وقد يقال : لا قصد إلا إلى النفي، لأن المخاطب غير لكنه مشرك، فالمقصود منهما رد زعمه، واكتفى في الإثبات بمجرد الإشارة، وهذا محتمل غير ضار لأصل المقصود، إذ لا يزيد على المناقشة في المثال فتأمل (بل قد يقصد الثاني) بالذات (فقط) دون الأول إلا تبعاً في الاستثناء المفرّغ (نحو : ما أنت إلا حر فافهم وتحقيق كلامهم قدس أسرارهم أنك قد عرفت أن الألفاظ في الاستثناء مستعملة في معانيها ويحصل من المركب مفهوم تقييدي يحكم عليه وعرفت أنه يقيد المستثنى منه بإخراج البعض فيحصل مقيد هو المستثنى منه المنقوص منه البعض، ويعبر به عن الباقي، وفي هذا التعبير الأطول بذكر الكل، ثم إخراج البعض إشارة إلى أن المخرج مخالف للصدر في الحكم، وهذه النكتة في الإطناب واختيار طريق أطول فحينئذ اندفع ما قال صدر الشريعة إن هذا إنما يصح باختيار القول الثاني هو أن يذكر الكل ويحكم على البعض، وأما على اختيار أن المجموع المركب هو الدال فالتخصيص بمفهوم اللقب ولا يكون إشارة، ووجه الدفع ظاهر فتأمل جداً، ولعل من قال إنه لا حكم فيه لغة إنما يفهم عرفاً ،مراده، هذا يعني ليس اللفظ موضوعاً لإفادة الحكم التام بالذات، بل إنما هو قيد يستفاد الحكم ضمناً وإشارة، ويؤيده ما اتفقوا عليه أن المفرد لا يدل على جملة، ثم هذا الذي ذكر هو الأصل في الاستثناء، وقد يعدل عنه فتقصد هذه الإشارة في خصوص التركيب بالذات، فلا إشكال عليهم قدس أسرارهم إلا من جهة عدم التدبر في كلامهم.
The second benefit: The verified Hanafis who agreed with the majority on the point that exception conveys a contrary ruling for the excepted item. They said: the ruling that comes after 'illa' is an indication because exception is like the ultimate limit, for it [the ultimate limit] marks the end of the ruling of the first part and indicates that what comes after is not included in what precedes. Likewise exception, and the ultimate limit of existence is non-existence and vice versa, so the contrary ruling is entailed. Except that the first part is established intentionally, while this [contrary ruling] is only incidental, so it is an indication. And the most appropriate, according to 'al-Tahrir', is that this is not absolute, but rather it is an indication sometimes when not intended, like 'I owe ten except three' because the intended meaning is seven—i.e., affirmation of it—while the negation of the excess is entailed incidentally. And it is an explicit statement (expression) and intended at other times, like the word of tawhid, for the affirmation and negation understood in it are both intended. But it may be said: there is no intent except negation, because the addressee is a polytheist, so the intended meaning from the two is the refutation of his claim, and he sufficed in affirmation with mere indication. And this is possible and does not harm the core intent, for it does not exceed discussion of the example, so reflect. Rather, the second [item] may be intended by itself only, without the first except incidentally, in the case of empty exception (istithna' mufaragh), like 'You are nothing but free', so understand. And the realization of their speech (may their secrets be sanctified) is that you have known that the words in exception are used in their literal meanings, and from the compound a restrictive concept is obtained about which a ruling is made. And you have known that it restricts the excepted-from by removing a part, so a restricted [item] is obtained which is the excepted-from minus that part, and phrase it expresses the remainder. And in this longer expression of mentioning the whole then removing a part, there is an indication that the removed item is contrary to the first part in ruling. And this subtle point of verbosity and choosing a longer path thus refutes what Sadr al-Shari'ah said: that this [contrary ruling] is only valid if one chooses the second opinion that the whole is mentioned and a ruling is made about a part. But on the choice that the composite whole is what indicates, then specification is by concept of the label and is not an indication. And the rebuttal is clear, so reflect deeply. Perhaps whoever said that there is no ruling in it linguistically, and that it is only understood by custom, his meaning is that the word is not placed to convey the complete ruling by itself, but rather it is a restriction from which the ruling is obtained implicitly and as an indication. And it is supported by their agreement that a single word does not indicate a complete sentence. Then, this is the principle in exception, and sometimes one deviates from it such that this indication is intended in the specific construction itself, so there is no problem for them (may their secrets be sanctified) except from lack of reflection on their words.
Source pages p. 339

Commentary
This passage discusses the nature of exception (istithna') in Hanafi usul, specifically whether the ruling of the excepted item is conveyed primarily or incidentally. The 'second benefit' clarifies the position of the 'verified Hanafis' who align with the majority: exception indeed provides a ruling contrary to the first part, but this ruling is often not the primary intent—it is merely an indication ('ishara). The author uses the analogy of a limit (ghaya), which marks the end of a ruling without explicitly stating the opposite. In a statement like 'I owe ten except three', the primary intent is to affirm seven; the negation of three is incidental. In the case of the kalima 'La ilaha illa Allah', both affirmation and negation may be intended, or only negation may be primary. The passage refutes Sadr al-Shari'ah's objection that this analysis requires a specific theory of predication. The author concludes that the Hanafi master's words must be understood correctly: exception inherently signals a contrary ruling indirectly, unless the speaker specifically intends to emphasize that ruling. This nuanced view prevents misunderstanding about the meaning of istithna' in legal language.

Key Terms
إشارة (ishara): An indication; the ruling of the excepted item is not explicitly stated but understood indirectly from the exception construction.
عبارة (ibara): Explicit expression; when the ruling of the excepted item is directly intended, as in 'La ilaha illa Allah' where affirmation is also intended.
استثناء مفرغ (istithna' mufaragh): An empty exception where the first part is negative or incomplete, e.g., 'Ma anta illa hurr' (You are nothing but free).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the Hanafi view of istithna', when is the ruling of the excepted item considered 'ibara (explicit statement) rather than 'ishara (indication)?
A) Always, because exception inherently gives a ruling.
B) Only when the first part is a general positive statement.
C) When the speaker intends both the affirmation and negation equally, as in the kalima 'La ilaha illa Allah'.
D) Only when the exception is mufaragh (empty) and the first part is negative.

Key Takeaway
Istithna' primarily indicates a contrary ruling incidentally ('ishara), but can be explicit ('ibara) when both parts are equally intended, as in 'La ilaha illa Allah'.

The Default Scope of Exception After Conjoined Sentences: Hanafi Usul
Explaining the rule that exception after conjoined sentences applies to the last unless there is a shared purpose.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule regarding the scope of an exception after conjoined sentences and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-position.

Core Idea
An exception after conjoined sentences defaults to the last clause unless the sentences share a common purpose.

When a speaker says 'Honor Banu Tamim and hire them except Zayd', does the exception apply to both actions or just hiring? This seemingly simple linguistic question has profound implications for interpreting legal texts. The Hanafi school, through a careful analysis of connection (ittisal) and purpose (gharad), establishes a default rule that the exception attaches only to the last sentence. However, this default can be overridden when the sentences share a common objective. In this lesson, we will unpack the Hanafi arguments and their dispute with the Shafi'is.

Guided Path
1
Identify the contested ruling
State the Hanafi default (exception applies only to the last sentence) and the Shafi'i alternative (exception applies to all unless a preventer exists).
2
Examine the Hanafi proof
Analyze the three arguments: (1) the ruling of the first is apparent and removal is doubtful; (2) the condition of connection is only fulfilled with the last; (3) applying to all would cause conflict (tanazu').
Focus on the first argument as it is most foundational.
3
Critically evaluate the Shafi'i objection
The Shafi'is argue that the exception attaches to all by default. Understand their basis (general rule of inclusion) and how the Hanafis respond.
4
Apply the rule to the Quranic example
Consider the verse of qadhf (24:4-5) where the exception 'except those who repent' applies to all preceding sentences due to shared purpose.
Note how the shared purpose (humiliation/revenge) overrides the default.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Identify a practical scenario where the Hanafi and Shafi'i rules lead to different legal outcomes.
E.g., a statement like 'Zayd went to the market and bought food except bread'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقالة الثالثة في المبادىء اللغوية
الصالح للاستثناء عنه نحو أكرم بني تميم واستأجرهم إلا زيداً (ولا) يكون (اشتراك) بينهما (في الغرض المسوق له (فللأخيرة) أي يكون حين ظهور الإضراب للأخيرة (وإلا) ظهر الإضراب إما بأن لا يختلفا نوعاً واسماً وحكماً، أو يختلفا في أحدها، لكن يكون في الثاني الأول، أو يختلفا، ولا ضمير للأول في الثاني، لكن يشتركان في الغرض المسوق له (فللجميع) أي فيكون للجميع في الصور الثلاث (ومنه آية القذف) وهي قوله تعالى: ﴿وَالَّذِينَ يَرْمُونَ الْمُحْصَنَاتِ ثُمَّ لَمْ يَأْتُوا بِأَرْبَعَةِ شُهَدَاءَ فَاجْلِدُوهُمْ ثَمَنِينَ جَلْدَةً وَلَا نَقْبَلُوا لَمْ شَهَدَةً أَبَدًا وَأَوْلَيْكَ هُمُ الْفَسِقُونَ إِلَّا الَّذِينَ تَابُوا ﴾ [النور: ٤ - ٥] لأن الغرض من الجمل (وهو الإهانة والانتقام واحد، فهو أي أبو الحسين يوافق الشافعية إذ الحاصل) لكلامه (تعلقه بالكل إلا لمانع) وهو قول الشافعية (إلاّ أنه قصر المانع فيما فصل بخلاف الشافعية فإنهم لم يقصروا فالخلاف إن كان ففي تعيين الموانع وهو أمر آخر لنا أولاً أن حكم الأولى ظاهر في الثبوت عموماً (ورفعه عن البعض بالاستثناء مشكوك لجواز كونه للأخيرة فقط) فلا يرفع حكم الأولى، كما يجوز تعلقه بالكل، فيرفع حكم الأولى أيضاً، وإذا كان الرفع مشكوكاً فلا يعارضه لأن الظاهر لا يعارضه المشكوك، وهذا أحسن مما قالوا حكم الأولى متيقن ورفعه بالاستثناء مشكوك، فإنه يرد على ظاهره أن التيقن بحكم الأولى ممنوع ، إذ احتمال ارتفاعه بالاستثناء ولو بصارف موجود، ولا قطع مع الاحتمال، وإن كان يجاب عنه بأن المراد بالتيقن الظهور فتأمل (بخلاف الأخيرة) فإن حكمها غير ظاهر لأن الرفع ظاهر فيها، إذ الكلام فيما لا صارف عنها) وحينئذ يتعلق بها (ولذا لزم فيها اتفاقاً وإذا ثبت أن الأخيرة ظاهرة الرفع (فاندفع ما في المختصر» أن الأخيرة أيضاً كذلك) أي حكمها ظاهر والارتفاع بالاستثناء مشكوك (الجواز رجوعه إلى الأولى بدليل) فلا يرفع الأخيرة، ووجه الدفع ظاهر، وهذا الدليل بظاهره لا يدل على عدم التعلق بما عدا الأخيرة، بل على التوقف، وقد يقرر بأن رفع الأولى مشكوك ، فلا يرتفع إلا عند ظهور قرينة التعلق بها، وحينئذ فالتعلق بها إما مجاز أو حقيقة، وعلى الثاني الاشتراك، لأن المنفرد لا يحتاج إلى قرينة فتعين الأول، فلزم الظهور في التعلق بالأخيرة فتأمل فيه، ثم اعترض عليه بأن ظهور حكم الأولى ممنوع، بل ارتفاعها بالاستثناء ظاهر عند الخصم، كيف وهذا في قوّة أصل المطلوب ولك أن تقرر ،هكذا إن تعلق المتعلقات بالقريب أصل متأصل عند أهل العربية، وقد يعدل عنه أيضاً، فحكم الأولى ظاهر الثبوت لعدم تعلق المغير به، وارتفاعه بالاستثناء مشكوك، لأن الكلام فيما لا صارف عن الأخيرة فيتعلق به، وهو القريب ولا يتعلق بما عداه إلا بقرينة، وهذا يدلّ على عدم التعلق بما عداها فتأمل فيه، فإنه موضع تأمل (و) لنا (ثانياً) الاتصال من شرطه) أي الاستثناء كما (وهو في الأخيرة) فقط، لأنه متأخر عن الأول بالأخذ في جملة أخرى فلا يتعلق بما عداها وهذا يدل على عدم التعلق بما عدا الأخيرة، فإن قلت : الاتصال بالعطف موجود قال والاتصال بالعطف فقط ضعيف) لا يكفي لتعلق الاستثناء تحققه الصارف عنه فيعتبر بدليل آخر موجب لاعتبار هذا الاتصال، والسر في مع ضعف هذا الاتصال أن العطف في الجمل لا يفيد إلا تحققها في الواقع، وهذا حاصل إن لم يعطف أيضاً، وفي صورة عدم العطف لا تعلق لأحدهما بالآخر فكذا في العطف، واعترض بأن الشرط في الاستثناء الاتصال العرفي وهو متحقق فإن العرف لا يعده متأخراً عن الأول، وجوابه ظاهر، لأن الجمل المتعاطفة قد تستوعب الساعات إذا ذكر الاستثناء بعدها، ولا يحكم عاقل بأنه متصل بالأولى لا حقيقة ولا عرفاً وغير المتعاطفة اثنين أو ثلاثة إذا قرن بعدها استثناء، مثل المتعاطفة اثنين أو ثلاثة فإن اكتفى بهذا الاتصال العرفي فالمتعاطفة وغيرها سيان فيحكم برجوعه إلى الكل إذا كانت الجمل قليلة، بحيث يقال في العرف إنه كلام واحد وإن لم تكن متعاطفة بخلاف الكثير وإن كانت متعاطفة أن غير المتعاطفة لا يجوز فيها الرجوع اتفاقاً إلا إلى ما يليه، فقد ظهر أنه لا يكفي هذا الاتصال العرفي بل الذي هو شرط هو السكوت من غير عذر أو الأخذ في كلام آخر، وظاهر فيما نحن فيه أنه ترك الكلام الأول وأخذ في الآخر فلا يصح الاستثناء عنه بعده فتدبر، وهذا يكفي للناظر المنصف، واعترض أيضاً بأن الدليل لو تم لدل على عدم جواز تعلقه بالكل مع أنه يجوز بقرينة، ولك أن تجيب بأنه ربما ينزل الانفصال منزلة العدم لأمور خطابية وينزل الجمل المتعددة بالعطف منزلة جملة واحدة، فلا يعد أخذه في أخرى تركاً لها، بل إتماماً لها في المقامات الخطابية، لكن يحتاج إلى القرينة لكونه خلاف الظاهر فلا يدل دليلنا على عدم الجواز مطلقاً، بل إذا لم يكن صارف فقط، ألا ترى أنه كثيراً ما ينزل وجود الشيء منزلة العدم في المقامات الخطابية، فينزل العالم منزلة الجاهل لعدم العمل بمقتضاه وبالعكس لظهور أمره حقيقة أو ادعاء وغير ذلك مما بين في فن المعاني، فكذا ههنا فاحفظ فإنه من مزال الأقدام، ولنا ثالثاً : لو كان متعلقاً بالكل، لزم توجه الفعلين إلى متعلق واحد وهو التنازع ، ولا شك أن باب غير التنازع أكثر فيحمل عليه إلا بدليل لأن الظن تابع للأغلب فتدبر (واستدل) على المختار (أولاً : لو قال علي عشرة إلا أربعة إلا اثنين لزم ثمانية) فلم يتعلق الاستثناء إلا بما يليه وإن تعلق بالكل لزم ستة (ويجاب بأنه في غير محل النزاع لعدم العطف والجملة) ههنا (قيل) في شرح المختصر (و) لم يتعلق بالكل للتعذر) المانع إياه ( وإلا) يتعذر بل يصح (كان الاثنان مثبتاً) لكونهما مستثنيين عن الاستثناء المفيد للنفي (منفياً) لكونهما أيضاً مستثنيين من العشرة المثبتة وثبوت شيء واحد وانتفاؤه محال (أقول) في رده (وحدة الموضوع من شروط التناقض وليس الأظهر، وليست وحدة الموضوع متحققة ههنا لأن الاثنين المثبتين من جملة الأربعة المستثناة والمنفيين من جملة الستة الباقية وإن قيل نوع الاثنين واحد فوحدة الموضوع متحققة قلت: اجتماع المتنافيين في الواحد النوعي غير مستحيل كما لا يخفى فتدبر (و) استدل (ثانياً بأن عمله لعدم استقلاله ضروري) فإن غير المستقل يقتضي التعلق والارتباط (وما وجب للضرورة يقدر بقدرها ولا يتعداها (والأخيرة متعينة) للتعلق لأن الكلام فيما لا صارف وبها تندفع الضرورة، فلا يتعلق بما عداها (ويجاب بأنه وضعي أي وضع للتعلق بالجملة (لا) ضروري حتى لا يتجاوز قدرها، وفي «التحرير» إن أريد أنه وضع للتعلق بالأخيرة فتم مطلوبنا، وإن أريد أنه وضع للتعلق بالكل فهو ممنوع وظاهره غير موجه لأنه منع على المنع ووجه بأنه ممنوع وباطل لأنه يستعمل للأخيرة، والأصل
The third article on linguistic principles. That which is fit for exception from it, like 'Honor Banu Tamim and hire them except Zayd' — and there is no shared purpose between them in the objective for which the statement is made, then it is for the last, meaning when discontinuity appears it is for the last. Otherwise, if discontinuity appears either because they do not differ in type, name, and ruling, or they differ in some but the second has the first, or they differ and there is no pronoun for the first in the second, but they share in the objective for which the statement is made, then it is for all — meaning it is for all in these three cases. And from it is the verse of qadhf, which is His statement: 'And those who accuse chaste women then do not bring four witnesses, flog them eighty lashes and do not accept their testimony ever, and those are the wicked, except those who repent' [An-Nur:4-5] because the objective of the sentences (which is humiliation and revenge) is one. So he, Abu al-Husayn, agrees with the Shafi'is, for the result of his statement is that it attaches to all except for a preventer, which is the statement of the Shafi'is, except that he restricted the preventer to what he detailed, unlike the Shafi'is who did not restrict. So the dispute, if it is, is in specifying the preventers, which is another matter. For us firstly: the ruling of the first is evident in its establishment generally, and its removal from some by the exception is doubtful because it is possible that it is only for the last, so it does not remove the ruling of the first, just as it is possible that it attaches to all, so it removes the ruling of the first as well. And when removal is doubtful, it does not oppose it because the apparent is not opposed by the doubtful. And this is better than what they said: the ruling of the first is certain and its removal by the exception is doubtful, for it is replied to its apparent that certainty of the ruling of the first is denied, since the possibility of its removal by the exception, even with a diverting factor, exists, and there is no certainty with possibility, even if it is answered that what is meant by certainty is apparentness; so ponder. Unlike the last, for its ruling is not apparent because removal is apparent in it, since the speech concerns that which has no diverting factor away from it. Therefore it attaches to it, and for this reason it is necessary in it by agreement. And when it is established that the last is apparent in removal, what is in 'Al-Mukhtasar' is repelled that the last is also like that, i.e., its ruling is apparent and removal by the exception is doubtful. [The objection]: the possibility of its return to the first by evidence, so it does not remove the last. The repelling is apparent. And this evidence in its apparent does not indicate non-attachment to what is other than the last, but rather indicates suspension. And it may be established that the removal of the first is doubtful, so it is not removed except when a qarina for its attachment appears. Then its attachment is either metaphorical or real, and on the second is sharing, because the alone does not need a qarina, so the first is determined, so apparentness in attachment to the last is necessary. Then ponder on it. Then objection against it: the apparentness of the ruling of the first is denied; rather its removal by the exception is apparent according to the opponent. How so? And this is in the strength of the original claim. And you can establish thus: attachment of dependents to the nearest is a rooted principle among Arabic scholars, and it may also be deviated from. So the ruling of the first is apparent in establishment due to non-attachment of the modifier to it, and its removal by the exception is doubtful, because the speech concerns that which has no diverting factor from the last, so it attaches to it, and it is the nearest, and does not attach to what is other than it except with a qarina. And this indicates non-attachment to what is other than it. So ponder on it, for it is a place of pondering. And for us secondly: connection is from its condition, i.e., the exception, and it is in the last only, because it is subsequent to the first by taking up another sentence, so it does not attach to what is other than it. And this indicates non-attachment to what is other than the last. If you say: connection by conjunction exists, it is said: connection by conjunction only is weak, insufficient for the attachment of the exception due to the presence of a diverting factor from it. So another evidence is considered that necessitates consideration of this connection. And the secret in the weakness of this connection is that conjunction in sentences only brings about their actual occurrence, and this is obtained even if not conjoined. And in the case of non-conjunction there is no attachment of one to the other, so similarly in conjunction. And objection: the condition in exception is customary connection, and it is realized, for custom does not consider it subsequent to the first. Its answer is apparent, because conjoined sentences may occupy hours when the exception is mentioned after them, and no sane person judges that it is connected to the first, neither in reality nor customarily. And non-conjoined sentences of two or three, when an exception is appended after them, are like conjoined sentences of two or three. So if one is satisfied with this customary connection, then conjoined and others are equal, so return to all is judged when the sentences are few, such that in custom it is said that it is one speech even if not conjoined, unlike the many even if conjoined. For non-conjoined, return beyond the adjacent is not permissible by agreement. So it is clear that this customary connection is insufficient; rather the condition is silence without excuse or taking up another speech. And apparent in our case is that he left the first speech and took up the other, so exception after it is not valid from it. So reflect. And this is sufficient for the just observer. And objection also: if the evidence were complete, it would indicate non-permissibility of its attachment to all while it is permissible with a qarina. And you can answer: perhaps disconnection is treated as non-existence for rhetorical matters, and multiple sentences conjoined are treated as one sentence, so taking up another is not considered a leaving of it, but rather a completion of it in rhetorical contexts, but it needs a qarina because it is contrary to the apparent. So our evidence does not indicate non-permissibility absolutely, but only when there is no diverting factor. Do you not see that often the existence of something is treated as non-existence in rhetorical contexts, so the scholar is treated as ignorant due to lack of acting on its implication, and vice versa due to the apparentness of his matter in reality or pretension, and other things that are explained in the science of meanings. So likewise here, so remember, for it is a slippery place. And for us thirdly: if it were attached to all, then directing two actions to one object would be necessary, which is conflict, and there is no doubt that the domain of non-conflict is more common, so it is interpreted accordingly unless there is evidence, because assumption follows the more frequent. So reflect. And they argued for the preferred opinion firstly: if one says 'I owe ten except four except two', then eight is necessary. So the exception only attached to what is adjacent to it, and if it attached to all, six would be necessary. And it is answered that this is not the subject of dispute due to lack of conjunction and that the sentence here is single. It was said in the commentary of Al-Mukhtasar: it did not attach to all due to impossibility preventing it, otherwise it would be impossible; rather it is valid, the two would be positive (because they are excepted from the exception which conveys negation) and negative (because they are also excepted from the ten which is positive), and the affirmation and negation of one thing is impossible. I say in reply: unity of subject is a condition of contradiction, and it is not apparent, and unity of subject is not realized here because the two affirmed are part of the four excepted, and the negated are part of the remaining six. And if it is said that the type of two is one, so unity of subject is realized, I say: the gathering of two opposites in one type is not impossible, as is not hidden, so reflect. And they argued secondly: its operation because of its non-independence is necessary, for the non-independent necessitates attachment and connection, and what is necessary for necessity is measured by its extent and does not exceed it, and the last is specified for attachment because the speech concerns that which has no diverting factor, and with it the necessity is repelled, so it does not attach to what is other than it. And it is answered: it is conventional, i.e., it is placed for attachment to the sentence, not necessary such that it does not exceed its extent. And in 'Al-Tahrir': if it is meant that it is placed for attachment to the last, then our claim is fulfilled; if it is meant that it is placed for attachment to all, then it is denied, and its apparent is not directed because it is a denial upon denial. And it is directed that it is denyed and false because it is used for the last, and the principle is...
Source pages p. 343

Commentary
This passage from the third article on linguistic principles explains a key usul al-fiqh rule regarding the scope of an exception (istithna') when it appears after a series of conjoined sentences. The author begins by stating the default rule: if there is no shared purpose between the sentences, the exception applies only to the last sentence (فللأخيرة). This is when there is idrab (discontinuity) — meaning the speaker breaks from the first statement and starts a new one. However, if discontinuity is not clearly present — either because the sentences do not differ in type, name, and ruling, or they share a common purpose — then the exception applies to all (فللجميع). The author cites the Quranic verse on false accusation (qadhf) as an example, where the exception 'except those who repent' is understood to apply to all the preceding statements because the common purpose of humiliation and revenge unites them. The author then notes that Abu al-Husayn (al-Basri) agreed with the Shafi'is in principle that the exception attaches to all unless there is a preventer (man'), but he differed in restricting the preventers. The passage then presents three Hanafi arguments (لنا أولاً، ثانياً، ثالثاً) for why the default is attachment to the last only. The first argument is based on the principle that the ruling of the first sentence is established by apparent evidence (zahir), and removing it by an exception is doubtful (mashkuk) because the exception might only apply to the last. Since the apparent is not overridden by the doubtful, the first ruling remains. The second argument focuses on the condition of connection (ittisal) in exception; the exception is connected only to the last because the speaker takes up a new sentence, making it disconnected from the first. Mere conjunction by 'and' or 'then' does not suffice for true connection; the exception must be immediately adjacent in a single speech act without interruption. The third argument is that if the exception attached to all, it would create a conflict (tanazu') by directing two actions to the same object, and since non-conflict is more common, the exception is presumed to apply only to the last unless evidence indicates otherwise. The passage then addresses two counterarguments by the opponents. First, the example of 'ten except four except two' which yields eight, but this is distinguished as a single sentence without conjunction, so it does not apply to the case of multiple sentences. Second, the claim that exception is by necessity non-independent and thus must attach to the last to fulfill its necessity, but the Hanafi response is that the attachment is conventional (wad'i), not necessary, so it can attach to more when evidence supports it. The overall lesson is that in Hanafi usul, the default in interpreting an exception after conjoined sentences is to restrict it to the last sentence unless there is a clear indicator (qarina) of a shared purpose or other evidence that it applies to all. This reflects the Hanafi methodology of preferring the more specific and avoiding presumptuous generalization.

Key Terms
استثناء (istithnā’): An exception phrase (e.g., 'except Zayd') that removes some individuals or cases from the general ruling of a preceding statement.
إضراب (iḍrāb): Discontinuity or breaking off from a previous statement to start a new one, indicating that the exception applies only to the last sentence.
اشتراك في الغرض (ishtirāk fī al-gharaḍ): Sharing of a common purpose between sentences, which causes the exception to apply to all of them, overriding the default of restricting to the last.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Hanafi usul al-fiqh, what is the default ruling when an exception (istithna') appears after a series of conjoined sentences (e.g., 'Honor Banu Tamim and hire them except Zayd')?
A) The exception applies to all sentences.
B) The exception applies only to the last sentence.
C) The exception applies only to the first sentence.
D) The exception is invalid.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, an exception after conjoined sentences is presumed to apply only to the last sentence unless the sentences share a common purpose, which then extends the exception to all.

Defining Amr: The Hanafi Position and the Circularity Objection
Debating definitions of amr and refuting circularity objections

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definition of amr and explain how the three responses resolve the circularity objection.

Core Idea
The Hanafi definition of amr avoids circularity by taking related elements as accidental aspects.

Defining a core usul concept like amr (command) is tricky because it involves the commanded person, the commanded act, and obedience. In this lesson, we examine the Hanafi definition and the three ingenious responses to the charge of circularity (dawr).

Guided Path
1
Identify the Hanafi definition
Read the passage to find the definition: 'Command is speech requiring the addressee to perform the content of what he was addressed with.' Note that the requirement is obligatory (hatmi) unless one adopts the Shafi'i view that includes recommended acts.
The definition is found after the discussion of the third article.
2
Understand the circularity objection
If defining command requires knowing the commanded person and the commanded act, but knowing those requires knowing command, the definition is circular. This objection is raised in the passage.
The passage states that taking absolute speech is insufficient and that circularity returns if restricted speech is intended.
3
Analyze the first response: accidental aspects
The command is known by an accidental aspect (wajh 'aradi) which is speech. From that, the commanded person and act are known by being addressed and being the content. Including them in the definition does not create circularity because they are known through the command, not as independent prerequisites.
This response introduces the concept of 'accidental aspect' to break the circle.
4
Analyze the second response: pre-definition knowledge
The command is already known by a distinctive accidental feature before any definition. Thus, when we define, we can include the dependent elements because they are now known through that prior accidental knowledge.
This response emphasizes that the command is already distinguished in everyday language.
5
Analyze the third response: knowledge by presence vs. conception
The third response distinguishes between knowledge by presence (huduri) of the command and conceptual knowledge (husuli) sought in definition. The circularity is broken because the depended-upon elements are known by presence, not by conceptualization. However, this response is refuted because definition requires conceptualization of everything included.
The refutation states that what is taken in the definition must be conceptualized, so presence is insufficient.

أن الأمر معلوم بوجه عرضي وهو الكلام، والمقصود معرفته بالكنه أو بالرسم الجامع المانع، ومعرفته بهذا الوجه تستلزم معرفة المأمور بوجه كونه هو المخاطب بالكلام وكذا المأمور به بوجه كونه مضمون الكلام، وكذا الطاعة بوجه كونه إتياناً لمضمونه، فأخذت هذه الأشياء المعلومة بالأوجه المذكورة في الحد أو الرسم وخرج الحاصل أن الأمر الكلام المقتضي إتيان المخاطب بمضمون ما خوطب به فلا إيراد أصلاً لكن ينبغي أن يراد التحديد والمقصود معرفة حده فحينئذ يحصل معرفة هذه الأشياء الموقوفة على الأمر، فتؤخذ في الحد فيحصل به حقيقة الأمر، فلا دور لتغاير الموقوف والموقوف عليه، والفرق بين هذا والأول بعد الاشتراك في إحداث التغاير بين الموقوف والموقوف عليه أن الموقوف عليه هناك الأمر المعلوم بالوجه العام وههنا المعلوم بوجه جامع مانع (أقول : إن قلت : أخذ العرضي في الحد يخرجه عن الحدية لأن المجموع من الداخل والخارج (خارج) ولا يصح الحدية، وههنا قد أخذ المأمور والمأمور به وكلاهما خارجان عن حقيقة الأمر، قلت: قد تكون (الحقيقة المحدودة ذات تعلق وإضافة لا يمكن ملاحظتها إلا بلحاظ المتعلقات (فملاحظة المتعلقات لتحصيل تلك الحقيقة لا يخرجه عن الحدية) كما في حد أحد المتضايفين، فإنه لا بد فيه من أخذ المضايف الآخر فإنه لا يلزم أن يعتبر) الخارج ( محمولاً) بل إنما يعتبره متعلقاً من المتعلقات فافهم (و) أجيب (ثالثاً بأن حصول الشيء بنفسه) في العلم الحضوري (غير حصوله بصورته الذي هو التصور المطلوب في العلم الحصولي، فالمطلوب العلم بالمحدود الحصولي، والموقوف عليه بهذه الأشياء الحضوري فلا دور لاختلاف نحو الإدراك (ورد) هذا الجواب بأن ما أخذ في التعريف لا بد من تصوره) ولا يكفي حضوره (لأنه) أي التعريف (تحصيل صورة من صور متعددة فلم يختلف جهة الإدراك أصلاً (ولهذا لم يكن الحضوري كاسباً إذ لا حصول فيه أصلاً فتفكر، ثم هو منقوض بأمرتك بفعل كذا) لأنه قول يقتضي طاعة المأمور بفعل المأمور به إلا أن يخص الأمر (النفسي بالتعريف، ويلتزم كونه أمراً) واعلم أن هذا الإيراد غير مختص بتعريف القاضي، بل وارد على المختار أيضاً، فالجواب أنه خبر عن الأمر وليس فيه اقتضاء لفعل بل إخبار عنه، وكذا ليس قولاً مقتضياً لطاعة المأمور بالذات بل إخبار عن قول كذلك، ومن ههنا ظهر سقوط التزام كونه أمراً باعتبار النفسي فإنه إخبار عن أمر فإن أريد به إنشاء الأمر مجازاً فهو أمر لفظياً كان أو نفسياً، فإنه لا فرق بينه وبين افعل كذا فتدبر (و) قال (جمهور المعتزلة) الأمر (قول القائل لمن دونه افعل وأورد عليه (التهديد ونحوه) فإنه قوله لمن دونه افعل مع أنه ليس أمراً (و) أورد (قول الحاكي والمبلغ، وأمر الأدنى للأعلى (استعلاء) فإنها أوامر مع عدم صدق الحد عليها، لأن ما عدا الأخير ليس قول القائل، والأخير ليس قول الأعلى وأجيب بأن المراد فعل حقيقة) وفي «التهديد» ليس افعل حقيقة (وفي الحكاية والتبليغ ليس قوله ولا أمراً منه فإن القرآن ليس قول النبي) ولا أمره (صلى الله عليه وآله وأصحابه وسلم) بل قوله تعالى وأمره تعالى وهو القائل حقيقة (وأمر الأدنى للأعلى استعلاء (ليس عندهم أمراً لغة بل عرفاً فلا بأس بخروجه (أقول) أو يقال الأدنى أعم حقيقة أو ادعاء وفي أمر الأدنى للأعلى علو الأدنى ادعاء، وهذا إحسان إلى من لا يقبله، فهو ضائع ( وقال قوم منهم الأمر إرادة الفعل وأورد بأن الممهد لعذره في ضرب عبده وفي بعض النسخ المهدد أي من هدده غيره وعلى هذا قوله لعذره يتعلق بقوله (يأمر عبده) بفعل (ولا يريد) إتيانه به ليتم العذر لأن العاقل لا يريد تكذيب نفسه، فالأمر قد تخلف عن الإرادة فلا يكون عينه ولا يخفى أنه يجيء مثله في الطلب فإنه يأمر ولا يطلب منه الفعل فإن العاقل لا يطلب تكذيب نفسه بل التحقيق أن هذا ليس أمراً ،حقيقة، بل صورة فقط فإنه طلب صورة، فلهم أن يقولوا إرادة صورة فتدبر (والتفرقة بين الإرادة والطلب (بامتناع التخلف في الإرادة دون الطلب غير مسلم عند الخصم فإنه فسر) الإرادة بما لا يستلزم الوقوع فإن الإرادة عندنا صفة مخصصة لأحد طرفي المقدور بالوقوع فلا يتخلف المقدور عنه، ولذا قال الإمام الهمام فيما روي عنه في الفقه الأكبر المعاصي بإرادة الله تعالى دون أمره وعند المعتزلة الداعي إلى الفعل من اعتقاد نفع أو علم مصلحة، وفسر بعضهم بعدم الكراهة قالوا تعلق هذا النحو من الإرادة القديمة لا يوجب وقوع المراد كذا قالوا وفيه ما فيه، ولك أن تقول : الإرادة تكوينية وهي ما ذكر ويجب وقوع المراد في القديمة بحسبها، وتشريعية وهي تبيين المشروعات، واقتضاؤها مع الرضا أو إعطاء المثوبة، وهي لا تستلزم المراد قطعاً، ولعلهم أرادوا هذا النحو من الإرادة، وبهذا فسر بعض ثقاتنا من أهل السنة رفعهم الله تعالى وخذل أعداءهم قوله تعالى: ﴿إِنَّمَا يُرِيدُ اللهُ لِيُذْهِبَ عَنكُمُ الرِّجْسَ أَهْلَ الْبَيْتِ وَيُطَهِّرَكُمْ تَطْهِيرًا ) [الأحزاب: ۳۳] (فاندفع) بما قرروا أو قررنا (ما) في المختصر لو كان الأمر (إرادة لوقعت المأمورات كلها لأنها لا تتخلف عن اقتضاء الإرادة واستدل أبو إسحق على إبطال كون الأمر إرادة بأن الدين الحال مأمور بقضائه ولو (حلف المديون (ليقضينه غداً إن شاء الله تعالى، فإنه لا يحنث لعدم قضائه في الغد فدل على أن الشرط لم يوجد و أنه تعالى ما شاء) وإلا حنث فثبت الأمر بدون المشيئة وهي الإرادة، فالأمر غيرها (وفيه ما فيه أما أولاً : فلأن الاستثناء بالمشيئة إبطال لليمين فلا حلف فلا حنث لأجل هذا الانعدام وجود المعلق عليه، وأما ثانياً فلأن الإرادة القولية غير التكوينية كذا في «الحاشية»، وإن حققته رجع إلى ما قررنا.
The third article on linguistic principles. Absolute speech is not sufficient for knowing the reality of the commanded thing and understanding obedience, later, and if restricted speech is intended then the circularity returns definitely, as in al-Tahrir. And this is insufficient, because the intention of the answerer is that the command is known by an accidental aspect which is speech, and the purpose is to know it by essence or by a comprehensive and exclusive definition. Knowing it by this aspect entails knowing the commanded person by the aspect of being the one addressed by speech, and likewise the commanded thing by the aspect of being the content of speech, and likewise obedience by the aspect of being the performance of its content. So these things known by the mentioned aspects are taken in the definition or description, and the result is that command is speech requiring the addressee to perform the content of what he was addressed with, so there is no objection at all. But the requirement should be meant as obligatory requirement, unless it is based on what is famous from the Shafi'is that the recommended is commanded, so there is no problem in that aspect, so ponder. (And) it was answered secondly that knowing it by an accidental aspect that distinguishes it from everything else is attained by every language knower before definition, and the purpose is to know its definition; then at that time the knowledge of these things that depend on the command is obtained, so they are taken in the definition and the reality of command is obtained, so there is no circularity due to the difference between the dependent and the depended upon. The difference between this and the first after sharing in creating difference between the dependent and the depended upon is that the depended upon there is the command known by the general aspect, and here it is known by a comprehensive and exclusive aspect. (I say: if you say: taking the accidental aspect in the definition takes it out of being a definition because the whole consists of internal and external (external) and the definition is not valid, and here the commanded person and the commanded thing have been taken, both of which are external to the reality of command, I say: the defined reality may be relational and additive and cannot be observed except by considering the related objects, so considering the related objects to obtain that reality does not take it out of being a definition, as in the definition of one of the two correlatives, for it is necessary to take the other correlative, because it does not require that the external be considered as a predicate, but rather he considers it as one of the related objects, so understand. (And) it was answered thirdly that the obtaining of a thing by itself) in knowledge by presence (is not the same as its obtaining by its image which is the conceptualization required in knowledge by acquisition. So the required is knowledge of the defined by acquisition, and the depended upon by these things is by presence, so there is no circularity due to the difference in the mode of perception. (This answer was refuted) because what is taken in the definition must be conceptualized) and presence is not sufficient (because the definition is the attainment of an image from multiple images, so the mode of perception does not differ at all. For this reason, the presential knowledge is not productive because there is no obtaining in it at all, so reflect. Then it is refuted by "I commanded you to do such and such" because it is a statement that requires obedience of the commanded person by doing the commanded thing, unless he specifies the (psychical) command by definition, and he commits that it is a command. And know that this objection is not specific to al-Qadi's definition, but also applies to the chosen definition. The answer is that it is a report about the command and does not contain a requirement for action but rather an report about it, and likewise it is not a statement requiring obedience of the commanded person by essence but rather a report about such a statement. From here it becomes clear that the commitment that it is a command in terms of the psychical falls, because it is a report about a command; if it is meant by it to create the command metaphorically, then it is a command, whether verbal or psychical, for there is no difference between it and "do such and such", so ponder. (And) the majority of the Mu'tazila said) the command is (the statement of a speaker to someone lower than him: "do". And it was objected by (threat and the like) because it is his statement to someone lower than him: "do" but it is not a command (and) it was objected by (the statement of the narrator and the conveyor, and the command of the lower to the higher (out of superiority) because these are commands while the definition does not apply to them, because except the last is not the statement of the speaker, and the last is not the statement of the higher. And it was answered that what is meant is "do" in reality (and in threat it is not "do" in reality (and in narration and conveyance it is not his statement nor his command, because the Quran is not the statement of the Prophet) nor his command, peace be upon him and his family and companions, but rather the statement of Almighty and His command, and He is the speaker in reality (and the command of the lower to the higher out of superiority (is not in their view a command linguistically but customarily, so there is no harm in its exclusion) (I say) or it is said the lower is more general in reality or by claim, and in the command of the lower to the higher there is a claim of superiority for the lower, and this is a kindness to one who does not accept it, so it is wasted. (And some of them said) the command is (the intention to perform an action. And it was objected by (the one who makes an excuse for his beating of his slave, and in some copies "the threatened" i.e. the one who is threatened by others, and according to this his statement "for his excuse" is related to his statement (he commands his slave) to do (and he does not intend) its performance to complete the excuse, because the rational person does not intend to falsify himself. So the command has been separated from intention, so it is not identical to it. And it is not hidden that a similar example occurs in demand, for he commands and does not demand the action from him because the rational person does not demand his own falsification. Rather, the investigation is that this is not a command in reality but only a form, because it is a demand of form. So they can say: intention of form, so ponder. (And the distinction between intention and demand (by the impossibility of failure in intention without demand) is not accepted by the opponent, because he interpreted) intention as not entailing occurrence, because intention in our view is a quality that specifies one of two possible outcomes by occurrence, so the possible does not fail from it. For this reason, the great Imam said in what is narrated from him in al-Fiqh al-Akbar: sins are by the intention of Allah without His command. And according to the Mu'tazila, the intention is the motivator to action from belief in benefit or knowledge of benefit, and some interpreted it as lack of dislike. They said: this kind of eternal intention does not necessitate the occurrence of the intended. Thus they said, and there is something in it. And you can say: intention is creative, and it is what we mentioned, and the occurrence of the intended in the eternal must be according to it, and legislative, and it is the clarification of legislated matters, and its requirement along with pleasure or giving reward, and it does not entail the intended definitely. Perhaps they meant this kind of intention. And with this, some of our trustworthy ones from Ahl al-Sunnah, may Allah raise them and forsake their enemies, interpreted the saying of the Exalted: "Allah only intends to remove from you impurity, O people of the household, and to purify you with a thorough purification" [33:33]. (Thus it is repelled) by what they or we established (what) in al-Mukhtasar: if the command were (intention, then all commanded things would occur, because they do not fail from the requirement of intention. And Abu Ishaq argued for invalidating that command is intention: because the debt of the present time is commanded to be paid, and if the debtor (swore to pay it tomorrow if Allah wills, then he does not break his oath for not paying it tomorrow, so it indicates that the condition did not exist and that Allah did not will it) otherwise he would break his oath, so the command is established without will, which is intention, so the command is other than it. (And there is something in it. First: because the exception by the will is a nullification of the oath, so there is no oath, so there is no breaking of oath due to this non-existence of the existence of the conditioned entity. Second: because the verbal intention is other than the creative intention, as in the Hashiyah, and if you investigate it, it returns to what we established.
Source pages p. 393

Commentary
This passage is a detailed usul discussion on the definition of 'amr' (command). The author begins by rejecting a simplistic definition of 'amr' as 'absolute speech' because it fails to capture the essence and does not convey the reality of the commanded act or obedience. He then presents a refined definition: 'Command is speech requiring the addressee to perform the content of what he was addressed with.' This definition is defended against the charge of circularity (dawr) through three responses. First, the elements taken in the definition (the commanded person, the commanded act, obedience) are known by accidental aspects derived from the command itself, so the circle is not vicious. Second, the command is known by a distinguishing accidental aspect prior to definition, allowing these elements to be included without circularity. Third, knowledge of these elements is of a different type (presential vs. conceptual), breaking the cycle. The third response is refuted because definition requires conceptualization, not mere presence. The passage then addresses objections against the definition, including those from the Mu'tazila who define command as 'a speaker's statement to a lower person: do.' Objections regarding threats, narration, and a lower commanding a higher are answered. The view that command is intention is also examined with counterexamples and the distinction between creative (takwini) and legislative (tashri'i) intention is introduced. The argument of Abu Ishaq involving oath and debt is presented and critiqued. The discussion is a classic example of Hanafi usul engagement with rival definitions and logical subtleties.

Key Terms
أمر (amr): The speech act that requires the addressee to perform the content of what he was addressed with, with obligatory requirement.
دور (dawr): Circularity in definition, where the definiendum is used in the definiens or depends on things that themselves depend on the definiendum.
وجه عرضي (wajh 'aradi): Accidental aspect; a non-essential property that still distinguishes the object.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the main flaw in the third response to the circularity objection?
A) It ignores accidental aspects.
B) It confuses knowledge by presence with conceptual knowledge.
C) It assumes the command is known before definition.
D) It is based on a flawed premise about language.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi definition of amr as 'speech requiring obligatory performance' avoids circularity by incorporating related elements as accidental aspects of the command itself.

Refuting Alternative Definitions of Amr: Mu'tazila and Intention Views
Refuting Mu'tazili and intention-based definitions of amr

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the objections against the Mu'tazili and intention-based definitions of amr and explain the Hanafi responses.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazili and intention-based definitions fail to cover all instances of command; the Hanafi definition remains robust.

In addition to defending their own definition, Hanafi scholars critically examined rival definitions of amr. This lesson covers the Mu'tazili definition (speaker to lower: do) and the definition of amr as intention (irada), along with their refutations.

Guided Path
1
State the Mu'tazili definition
The Mu'tazila define amr as 'the statement of a speaker to someone lower than him: do.' This definition aims to capture the typical command scenario.
The passage explicitly attributes this to 'the majority of the Mu'tazila'.
2
Identify the three objections
The objections are: (1) threats (tahdid) like 'Do it or I will punish you' are not commands; (2) narration (hikaya) like 'He said: do' is not a command but a report; (3) a lower person commanding a higher is not a linguistic command.
These are mentioned with the phrase 'وأورد عليه التهديد ونحوه... وأورد قول الحاكي... وأمر الأدنى للأعلى'.
3
Analyze the Mu'tazili responses and the Hanafi critique
The Mu'tazila respond that in threats, 'do' is not real; in narration, the speaker is not the original commander; and a lower commanding a higher is customary, not linguistic. The Hanafi critique finds these responses ad hoc, as they require exceptions to the definition.
The passage states 'وأجيب بأن المراد فعل حقيقة...' and then 'أقول...' offering further critique.
4
Examine the intention-based definition
Some defined amr as 'the intention to perform an action.' The objection: a master commands his slave to do something but does not intend it (to create an excuse for punishment). Thus command exists without intention, so they are not identical.
The example of the master making an excuse is found in the passage: 'الممهد لعذره في ضرب عبده'.
5
Understand the Hanafi distinction of creative vs. legislative intention
The Hanafi response distinguishes between creative intention (irada takwiniyya), which necessitates the intended, and legislative intention (irada tashri'iyya), which does not. The command can be accompanied by legislative intention even without creative intention. This resolves the objection.
The passage introduces this distinction: 'الإرادة تكوينية... وتشريعية'.
