المقالة الثالثة في المبادىء اللغوية مطلق الكلام فلا يكفي في معرفة حقيقة المأمور به وفهم الطاعة ،بعد ، وإن أريد الكلام المقيد بالقيود فالدور عائد قطعاً، كذا في «التحرير»، وهذا غير وافٍ فإن مراد المجيب أن الأمر معلوم بوجه عرضي وهو الكلام، والمقصود معرفته بالكنه أو بالرسم الجامع المانع، ومعرفته بهذا الوجه تستلزم معرفة المأمور بوجه كونه هو المخاطب بالكلام وكذا المأمور به بوجه كونه مضمون الكلام، وكذا الطاعة بوجه كونه إتياناً لمضمونه، فأخذت هذه الأشياء المعلومة بالأوجه المذكورة في الحد أو الرسم وخرج الحاصل أن الأمر الكلام المقتضي إتيان المخاطب بمضمون ما خوطب به فلا إيراد أصلاً لكن ينبغي أن يراد بالاقتضاء الاقتضاء الحتمي إلا أن يبنى على ما اشتهر عن الشافعية أن المندوب مأمور به ، فلا إشكال بوجه فتدبر فيه (و) أجيب (ثانياً بأن معرفته بوجه عرضي يمتاز به عن جميع ما عداه حاصل لكل عارف باللغة قبل التحديد والمقصود معرفة حده فحينئذ يحصل معرفة هذه الأشياء الموقوفة على الأمر، فتؤخذ في الحد فيحصل به حقيقة الأمر، فلا دور لتغاير الموقوف والموقوف عليه، والفرق بين هذا والأول بعد الاشتراك في إحداث التغاير بين الموقوف والموقوف عليه أن الموقوف عليه هناك الأمر المعلوم بالوجه العام وههنا المعلوم بوجه جامع مانع (أقول : إن قلت : أخذ العرضي في الحد يخرجه عن الحدية لأن المجموع من الداخل والخارج (خارج) ولا يصح الحدية، وههنا قد أخذ المأمور والمأمور به وكلاهما خارجان عن حقيقة الأمر، قلت: قد تكون (الحقيقة المحدودة ذات تعلق وإضافة لا يمكن ملاحظتها إلا بلحاظ المتعلقات (فملاحظة المتعلقات لتحصيل تلك الحقيقة لا يخرجه عن الحدية) كما في حد أحد المتضايفين، فإنه لا بد فيه من أخذ المضايف الآخر فإنه لا يلزم أن يعتبر) الخارج ( محمولاً) بل إنما يعتبره متعلقاً من المتعلقات فافهم (و) أجيب (ثالثاً بأن حصول الشيء بنفسه) في العلم الحضوري (غير حصوله بصورته الذي هو التصور المطلوب في العلم الحصولي، فالمطلوب العلم بالمحدود الحصولي، والموقوف عليه بهذه الأشياء الحضوري فلا دور لاختلاف نحو الإدراك (ورد) هذا الجواب بأن ما أخذ في التعريف لا بد من تصوره) ولا يكفي حضوره (لأنه) أي التعريف (تحصيل صورة من صور متعددة فلم يختلف جهة الإدراك أصلاً (ولهذا لم يكن الحضوري كاسباً إذ لا حصول فيه أصلاً فتفكر، ثم هو منقوض بأمرتك بفعل كذا) لأنه قول يقتضي طاعة المأمور بفعل المأمور به إلا أن يخص الأمر (النفسي بالتعريف، ويلتزم كونه أمراً) واعلم أن هذا الإيراد غير مختص بتعريف القاضي، بل وارد على المختار أيضاً، فالجواب أنه خبر عن الأمر وليس فيه اقتضاء لفعل بل إخبار عنه، وكذا ليس قولاً مقتضياً لطاعة المأمور بالذات بل إخبار عن قول كذلك، ومن ههنا ظهر سقوط التزام كونه أمراً باعتبار النفسي فإنه إخبار عن أمر فإن أريد به إنشاء الأمر مجازاً فهو أمر لفظياً كان أو نفسياً، فإنه لا فرق بينه وبين افعل كذا فتدبر (و) قال (جمهور المعتزلة) الأمر (قول القائل لمن دونه افعل وأورد عليه (التهديد ونحوه) فإنه قوله لمن دونه افعل مع أنه ليس أمراً (و) أورد (قول الحاكي والمبلغ، وأمر الأدنى للأعلى (استعلاء) فإنها أوامر مع عدم صدق الحد عليها، لأن ما عدا الأخير ليس قول القائل، والأخير ليس قول الأعلى وأجيب بأن المراد فعل حقيقة) وفي «التهديد» ليس افعل حقيقة (وفي الحكاية والتبليغ ليس قوله ولا أمراً منه فإن القرآن ليس قول النبي) ولا أمره (صلى الله عليه وآله وأصحابه وسلم) بل قوله تعالى وأمره تعالى وهو القائل حقيقة (وأمر الأدنى للأعلى استعلاء (ليس عندهم أمراً لغة بل عرفاً فلا بأس بخروجه (أقول) أو يقال الأدنى أعم حقيقة أو ادعاء وفي أمر الأدنى للأعلى علو الأدنى ادعاء، وهذا إحسان إلى من لا يقبله، فهو ضائع ( وقال قوم منهم الأمر إرادة الفعل وأورد بأن الممهد لعذره في ضرب عبده وفي بعض النسخ المهدد أي من هدده غيره وعلى هذا قوله لعذره يتعلق بقوله (يأمر عبده) بفعل (ولا يريد) إتيانه به ليتم العذر لأن العاقل لا يريد تكذيب نفسه، فالأمر قد تخلف عن الإرادة فلا يكون عينه ولا يخفى أنه يجيء مثله في الطلب فإنه يأمر ولا يطلب منه الفعل فإن العاقل لا يطلب تكذيب نفسه بل التحقيق أن هذا ليس أمراً ،حقيقة، بل صورة فقط فإنه طلب صورة، فلهم أن يقولوا إرادة صورة فتدبر (والتفرقة بين الإرادة والطلب (بامتناع التخلف في الإرادة دون الطلب غير مسلم عند الخصم فإنه فسر) الإرادة بما لا يستلزم الوقوع فإن الإرادة عندنا صفة مخصصة لأحد طرفي المقدور بالوقوع فلا يتخلف المقدور عنه، ولذا قال الإمام الهمام فيما روي عنه في الفقه الأكبر المعاصي بإرادة الله تعالى دون أمره وعند المعتزلة الداعي إلى الفعل من اعتقاد نفع أو علم مصلحة، وفسر بعضهم بعدم الكراهة قالوا تعلق هذا النحو من الإرادة القديمة لا يوجب وقوع المراد كذا قالوا وفيه ما فيه، ولك أن تقول : الإرادة تكوينية وهي ما ذكر ويجب وقوع المراد في القديمة بحسبها، وتشريعية وهي تبيين المشروعات، واقتضاؤها مع الرضا أو إعطاء المثوبة، وهي لا تستلزم المراد قطعاً، ولعلهم أرادوا هذا النحو من الإرادة، وبهذا فسر بعض ثقاتنا من أهل السنة رفعهم الله تعالى وخذل أعداءهم قوله تعالى: ﴿إِنَّمَا يُرِيدُ اللهُ لِيُذْهِبَ عَنكُمُ الرِّجْسَ أَهْلَ الْبَيْتِ وَيُطَهِّرَكُمْ تَطْهِيرًا ) [الأحزاب: ۳۳] (فاندفع) بما قرروا أو قررنا (ما) في المختصر لو كان الأمر (إرادة لوقعت المأمورات كلها لأنها لا تتخلف عن اقتضاء الإرادة واستدل أبو إسحق على إبطال كون الأمر إرادة بأن الدين الحال مأمور بقضائه ولو (حلف المديون (ليقضينه غداً إن شاء الله تعالى، فإنه لا يحنث لعدم قضائه في الغد فدل على أن الشرط لم يوجد و أنه تعالى ما شاء) وإلا حنث فثبت الأمر بدون المشيئة وهي الإرادة، فالأمر غيرها (وفيه ما فيه أما أولاً : فلأن الاستثناء بالمشيئة إبطال لليمين فلا حلف فلا حنث لأجل هذا الانعدام وجود المعلق عليه، وأما ثانياً فلأن الإرادة القولية غير التكوينية كذا في «الحاشية»، وإن حققته رجع إلى ما قررنا.
The third article on linguistic principles. Absolute speech is not sufficient for knowing the reality of the commanded thing and understanding obedience, later, and if restricted speech is intended then the circularity returns definitely, as in al-Tahrir. And this is insufficient, because the intention of the answerer is that the command is known by an accidental aspect which is speech, and the purpose is to know it by essence or by a comprehensive and exclusive definition. Knowing it by this aspect entails knowing the commanded person by the aspect of being the one addressed by speech, and likewise the commanded thing by the aspect of being the content of speech, and likewise obedience by the aspect of being the performance of its content. So these things known by the mentioned aspects are taken in the definition or description, and the result is that command is speech requiring the addressee to perform the content of what he was addressed with, so there is no objection at all. But the requirement should be meant as obligatory requirement, unless it is based on what is famous from the Shafi'is that the recommended is commanded, so there is no problem in that aspect, so ponder. (And) it was answered secondly that knowing it by an accidental aspect that distinguishes it from everything else is attained by every language knower before definition, and the purpose is to know its definition; then at that time the knowledge of these things that depend on the command is obtained, so they are taken in the definition and the reality of command is obtained, so there is no circularity due to the difference between the dependent and the depended upon. The difference between this and the first after sharing in creating difference between the dependent and the depended upon is that the depended upon there is the command known by the general aspect, and here it is known by a comprehensive and exclusive aspect. (I say: if you say: taking the accidental aspect in the definition takes it out of being a definition because the whole consists of internal and external (external) and the definition is not valid, and here the commanded person and the commanded thing have been taken, both of which are external to the reality of command, I say: the defined reality may be relational and additive and cannot be observed except by considering the related objects, so considering the related objects to obtain that reality does not take it out of being a definition, as in the definition of one of the two correlatives, for it is necessary to take the other correlative, because it does not require that the external be considered as a predicate, but rather he considers it as one of the related objects, so understand. (And) it was answered thirdly that the obtaining of a thing by itself) in knowledge by presence (is not the same as its obtaining by its image which is the conceptualization required in knowledge by acquisition. So the required is knowledge of the defined by acquisition, and the depended upon by these things is by presence, so there is no circularity due to the difference in the mode of perception. (This answer was refuted) because what is taken in the definition must be conceptualized) and presence is not sufficient (because the definition is the attainment of an image from multiple images, so the mode of perception does not differ at all. For this reason, the presential knowledge is not productive because there is no obtaining in it at all, so reflect. Then it is refuted by "I commanded you to do such and such" because it is a statement that requires obedience of the commanded person by doing the commanded thing, unless he specifies the (psychical) command by definition, and he commits that it is a command. And know that this objection is not specific to al-Qadi's definition, but also applies to the chosen definition. The answer is that it is a report about the command and does not contain a requirement for action but rather an report about it, and likewise it is not a statement requiring obedience of the commanded person by essence but rather a report about such a statement. From here it becomes clear that the commitment that it is a command in terms of the psychical falls, because it is a report about a command; if it is meant by it to create the command metaphorically, then it is a command, whether verbal or psychical, for there is no difference between it and "do such and such", so ponder. (And) the majority of the Mu'tazila said) the command is (the statement of a speaker to someone lower than him: "do". And it was objected by (threat and the like) because it is his statement to someone lower than him: "do" but it is not a command (and) it was objected by (the statement of the narrator and the conveyor, and the command of the lower to the higher (out of superiority) because these are commands while the definition does not apply to them, because except the last is not the statement of the speaker, and the last is not the statement of the higher. And it was answered that what is meant is "do" in reality (and in threat it is not "do" in reality (and in narration and conveyance it is not his statement nor his command, because the Quran is not the statement of the Prophet) nor his command, peace be upon him and his family and companions, but rather the statement of Almighty and His command, and He is the speaker in reality (and the command of the lower to the higher out of superiority (is not in their view a command linguistically but customarily, so there is no harm in its exclusion) (I say) or it is said the lower is more general in reality or by claim, and in the command of the lower to the higher there is a claim of superiority for the lower, and this is a kindness to one who does not accept it, so it is wasted. (And some of them said) the command is (the intention to perform an action. And it was objected by (the one who makes an excuse for his beating of his slave, and in some copies "the threatened" i.e. the one who is threatened by others, and according to this his statement "for his excuse" is related to his statement (he commands his slave) to do (and he does not intend) its performance to complete the excuse, because the rational person does not intend to falsify himself. So the command has been separated from intention, so it is not identical to it. And it is not hidden that a similar example occurs in demand, for he commands and does not demand the action from him because the rational person does not demand his own falsification. Rather, the investigation is that this is not a command in reality but only a form, because it is a demand of form. So they can say: intention of form, so ponder. (And the distinction between intention and demand (by the impossibility of failure in intention without demand) is not accepted by the opponent, because he interpreted) intention as not entailing occurrence, because intention in our view is a quality that specifies one of two possible outcomes by occurrence, so the possible does not fail from it. For this reason, the great Imam said in what is narrated from him in al-Fiqh al-Akbar: sins are by the intention of Allah without His command. And according to the Mu'tazila, the intention is the motivator to action from belief in benefit or knowledge of benefit, and some interpreted it as lack of dislike. They said: this kind of eternal intention does not necessitate the occurrence of the intended. Thus they said, and there is something in it. And you can say: intention is creative, and it is what we mentioned, and the occurrence of the intended in the eternal must be according to it, and legislative, and it is the clarification of legislated matters, and its requirement along with pleasure or giving reward, and it does not entail the intended definitely. Perhaps they meant this kind of intention. And with this, some of our trustworthy ones from Ahl al-Sunnah, may Allah raise them and forsake their enemies, interpreted the saying of the Exalted: "Allah only intends to remove from you impurity, O people of the household, and to purify you with a thorough purification" [33:33]. (Thus it is repelled) by what they or we established (what) in al-Mukhtasar: if the command were (intention, then all commanded things would occur, because they do not fail from the requirement of intention. And Abu Ishaq argued for invalidating that command is intention: because the debt of the present time is commanded to be paid, and if the debtor (swore to pay it tomorrow if Allah wills, then he does not break his oath for not paying it tomorrow, so it indicates that the condition did not exist and that Allah did not will it) otherwise he would break his oath, so the command is established without will, which is intention, so the command is other than it. (And there is something in it. First: because the exception by the will is a nullification of the oath, so there is no oath, so there is no breaking of oath due to this non-existence of the existence of the conditioned entity. Second: because the verbal intention is other than the creative intention, as in the Hashiyah, and if you investigate it, it returns to what we established.
Source pages p. 393

Commentary
This passage is a detailed usul discussion on the definition of 'amr' (command). The author begins by rejecting a simplistic definition of 'amr' as 'absolute speech' because it fails to capture the essence and does not convey the reality of the commanded act or obedience. He then presents a refined definition: 'Command is speech requiring the addressee to perform the content of what he was addressed with.' This definition is defended against the charge of circularity (dawr) through three responses. First, the elements taken in the definition (the commanded person, the commanded act, obedience) are known by accidental aspects derived from the command itself, so the circle is not vicious. Second, the command is known by a distinguishing accidental aspect prior to definition, allowing these elements to be included without circularity. Third, knowledge of these elements is of a different type (presential vs. conceptual), breaking the cycle. The third response is refuted because definition requires conceptualization, not mere presence. The passage then addresses objections against the definition, including those from the Mu'tazila who define command as 'a speaker's statement to a lower person: do.' Objections regarding threats, narration, and a lower commanding a higher are answered. The view that command is intention is also examined with counterexamples and the distinction between creative (takwini) and legislative (tashri'i) intention is introduced. The argument of Abu Ishaq involving oath and debt is presented and critiqued. The discussion is a classic example of Hanafi usul engagement with rival definitions and logical subtleties.

Key Terms
إرادة تكوينية (irada takwiniyya): Creative intention: the divine attribute that necessitates the existence of the intended.
إرادة تشريعية (irada tashri'iyya): Legislative intention: the divine attribute that lays down law and commands, but does not necessarily bring about the intended act.
تهديد (tahdid): Threat; a speech act that uses command form but intends to warn, not to command.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the main problem with defining amr as intention?
A) It makes all commands obligatory.
B) It cannot explain commands that are not intended.
C) It conflates divine and human commands.
D) It lacks linguistic basis.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi definition of amr withstands critiques from rival schools, including the Mu'tazili and intention-based definitions.

Twenty Meanings of the Imperative (If‘al) in Hanafi Usul
Listing and exemplifying the twenty meanings of the imperative form

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the twenty meanings of the imperative form in Arabic and identify which meaning a specific imperative indicates based on its contextual clues.

Core Idea
The imperative 'if'al' indicates obligation by default, but context can shift it to recommendation, permission, threat, or other meanings.

Commands in divine revelation are not always binding duties. Hanafi legal theorists have identified twenty distinct meanings that the imperative form can convey, depending on the presence of contextual indicators (qara'in). This lesson presents this taxonomy with examples from the Quran and hadith, highlighting how context determines legal force.

Guided Path
1
Identify the real meaning
Recognize that the primary meaning of the imperative is obligation (wujub), as exemplified by 'Establish prayer'. This is the default unless a contextual indicator shifts it.
The passage begins with 'The imperative form occurs for twenty meanings: obligation'.
2
Study the secondary meanings
Read through the remaining nineteen meanings: recommendation, discipline, guidance, permission, threat, warning, gratitude, honor, subjugation, incapacitation, humiliation, equalization, supplication, request, wish, hope, contempt, and two more (as enumerated).
Each meaning is illustrated with a Quranic or hadith example.
3
Examine the contextual clues
For each meaning, identify the contextual indicator that prevents the imperative from being obligation. For example, the command to write slaves is not obligation because it is an act of ihsan (benevolence).
The passage sometimes explicitly states why it is not obligation.
4
Distinguish similar meanings
Compare close meanings such as recommendation vs. permission, or threat vs. warning. Note subtle differences: recommendation seeks reward in the afterlife, permission allows an act without reward.
The author also mentions that some philosophers included hope within wish, etc.

Issue: The form 'if'al' occurs for twenty meanings: Obligation (wujub): e.g., 'Establish prayer' — this is the real command. Recommendation (nadb): e.g., '...then write them if you know good in them' [24:33] — this is not an obligation by consensus of jurists except Dawud al-Zahiri and his followers, and it is not permission as some of our shaykhs claimed. Discipline (ta'dib): e.g., the hadith of Amr b. Salama that the Prophet said 'Say Bismillah, eat with your right hand, and eat from what is near you' — al-Shafi'i considers this obligation, which is far-fetched because the addressee is a child not legally responsible. Guidance (irshad): e.g., 'and call to witness' [2:282] — for worldly benefits. Permission (ibaha): e.g., 'Eat and drink' [2:60] — but this could also be obligation, so the better example is 'and when you are free, then hunt' [5:2] which is clearly permissible after ihram. Threat (tahdid): e.g., 'Do as you will; indeed He sees what you do' [41:40]. Warning (indhar): e.g., 'Say: Enjoy yourselves, for your return is to the Fire' [14:30]. Gratitude (imtinan): e.g., 'Eat from what Allah has provided you, lawful and good' [5:88] — this may be obligation due to the restriction. Honor (ikram): e.g., 'Enter it in peace, safe' [15:46] to the people of Paradise. Subjugation (tashkhir): e.g., 'Be apes, despised' [2:65] to those who transgressed the Sabbath. Incapacitation (ta'jiz): e.g., 'If you are in doubt about what We have sent down to Our servant, then bring a surah like it' [2:23]. Humiliation (ihanah): e.g., 'Be stones' [17:50] — not meant to become stones, but to show their lowliness. Equalization (taswiyah): e.g., 'Be patient or impatient' [52:16] — specific when negation is attached. Supplication (du'a): e.g., 'O Allah, forgive me.' Request (iltimas): e.g., 'Do (for an equal).' Wish (tamanni): e.g., Imru' al-Qays' verse: 'Oh long night, don't you clear away?' Hope (tarajji): as above when one is expectant. Contempt (ihtiqar): e.g., Moses' speech to the sorcerers: 'Rather, throw what you are throwing' — intended to show contempt. And that may be done by mere belief without an act indicating contempt, not humiliation. Footnote: The phrase 'Rather, throw what you are throwing' is not in the Quran in this exact order; it is a combination of verses from Surah Taha and Surah al-Shu'ara. Written by the corrector.
Source pages p. 394

Commentary
This passage lists and exemplifies the twenty meanings of the imperative form 'if'al' in Arabic. The author begins with the real meaning: obligation (wujub), citing 'establish prayer' as a clear command. He then discusses recommendation (nadb) using the verse about writing contracts for slaves — it is not obligation by consensus, and not permission as some Hanafis claimed. The distinction is that recommendation aims for reward. Discipline (ta'dib) is illustrated by the Prophet's instruction to a child about eating, which al-Shafi'i wrongly considers obligatory — but the child is not obligated. Guidance (irshad) is for worldly benefits, permission (ibaha) is best exemplified by hunting after ihram. Threat, warning, gratitude, honor, subjugation, incapacitation, humiliation, equalization, supplication, request, wish, hope, and contempt are each given with Quranic or poetic examples. The passage ends with a footnote correcting a Quranic reference: the quoted phrase is not a verbatim ayah but a combination. The key takeaway is that the imperative form is polysemous and context determines its meaning.

Key Terms
صيغة الأمر (sigha al-amr): The imperative form 'if'al' in Arabic, which can indicate multiple meanings including obligation, recommendation, permission, threat, etc., depending on context.
الندب (al-nadb): A command that indicates recommendation, not obligation; it seeks reward in the afterlife.
قرينة (qarinah): A contextual indicator that determines the intended meaning of a linguistically ambiguous expression, such as shifting the imperative from obligation to recommendation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT listed as a meaning of the imperative form in this passage?
A) Obligation (إيجاب)
B) Recommendation (ندب)
C) Threat (تهديد)
D) Prohibition (نهي)

Key Takeaway
The imperative form has twenty possible meanings; its real meaning is obligation, but context determines the actual meaning.

The Prohibition Argument and the Iblis Verse (Arguments 3 & 4)
Presents the third and fourth arguments for الفور with Hanafi rebuttals.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi rebuttals to the prohibition analogy and the Iblis verse arguments for immediate compliance.

Core Idea
Prohibition is subsidiary to command; the Iblis command was restricted. Neither proves الفور.

The third and fourth arguments for immediate compliance rely on the nature of prohibition and a Quranic rebuke of Iblis. The Hanafi responses clarify that these do not establish a general rule for all commands.

Guided Path
1
Identify the opponent's third argument
The opponent argues: prohibition indicates الفور; command is a prohibition against its opposite; therefore command must also indicate الفور, otherwise contradictions arise.
The argument uses the idea that opposite rulings cannot coexist.
2
Analyze the Hanafi response to the third argument
The Hanafi response: the prohibition is subsidiary to the command (تابع للأمر) and does not itself indicate الفور. The contradiction would only arise if the prohibition were independent.
The response draws on the principle of subsidiary rulings.
3
Identify the opponent's fourth argument
The opponent cites Quran 7:12 where Allah rebukes Iblis for not prostrating immediately after the command. This censure shows that the command required immediate compliance, and all commands are of one type.
The verse is used as evidence for الفور from the ذم (censure) on delay.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response to the fourth argument
The Hanafi response: the command to prostrate was not absolute but restricted by Quran 15:29 ('when I have proportioned him…'). It was tied to a specific future event, not an immediate command. The discussion is about absolute commands.
This shows that the verse does not prove الفور for unqualified commands.

The third discourse on linguistic principles. It contradicts, and it returned... For the obtained is not sought, and the necessary from the proof is the present state, so it is only possible in it except the future, either immediately as you hold or after as was said, or absolutely as you say. So the necessary from the proof contradicts your claim, and the claimant is not necessary. This response is a prevention of the complete approximation or a summary refutation that if it were complete, it would necessitate impossibility. It was said: He does not mean by 'present' the 'now' such that what you mentioned would be necessary, but rather parts of the end of the past and the beginning of the future, which is the customary present. So 'immediate' is included in it, so no impossibility and the approximation is complete. I say: If what this speaker said were correct, then the command regarding the required would be connected to the customary present, and it would be like present tense forms, which is contrary to the consensus of Arabic grammarians. And 'immediate' is only the beginnings of the future customarily, so what follows from it is the difficulty [؟] backwards, so ponder. Yes, if it were said in the proof that the principle requires that the command be for the present because it is more frequent and it is not possible, and proximity is like the present, so for proximity the command is made for it, acting as much as possible, it is not far-fetched, as was said regarding the circumstantial accusative (ḥāl) governed by the verb: its origin is that it should be simultaneous with the agent, and since it was not in the past, 'perhaps' is used to be close. So understand. And they said thirdly: The prohibition is for immediate compliance, and the command is a prohibition against its opposite, so it is also for immediate compliance; otherwise, the contradiction of two opposites would be necessary. And the like of this has preceded in the matter of repetition, with the answer that this prohibition is subsidiary to the command and is not for immediate compliance. And they said fourthly: Allah's address to Iblis: 'What prevented you from prostrating when I commanded you?' [7:12] is a censure for not hastening to prostrate, so it is for immediate compliance, and all commands are of one type, so the form indicates it. We say: The command to prostrate is not absolute but restricted by His statement: 'So when I have proportioned him and breathed into him of My spirit, fall down to him in prostration' [15:29]. And the discussion was about the absolute command. As for the restricted command, it is according to the requirement of the restriction, whether immediate or delayed. Here it is restricted to immediacy, so it is for it, so ponder. And they said fifthly: If it were not for immediate compliance and delay were permissible, then either to a specified time, there is no proof for it. Even if it were said the specified time is old age, he said: Old age, for example, is not specified, for how many a young man dies suddenly, so old age is not realized there, so the obligatory act is missed. And how many an old man lives a long time, so the obligatory act can be performed after old age with delay. So old age is not fit to be a specifier for delay. Or to the last possible time, which is unknown, so delaying to it necessitates impossibility. We say: This is refuted by the permissibility of explicitly stating delay, for it is permissible by consensus, even though the premises of the proof also apply to it. And the solution to the proof is that impossibility only necessitates obligating delay to the last possible time, not delegating it to him to come at any time within the times of ability. So ponder. (Benefit) In al-Kashf, the doctrine of Imam Muhammad regarding the permissibility of delaying Hajj with sin due to failing in one's lifetime is invalidated: If a questioner asks: Hajj has become obligatory upon me, is it permissible for me to delay it to the second year while my safety [from death] is doubtful to me? If we say yes, then he does not sin if he dies having missed it. If we say no, then immediacy is necessary. If we say: If in Allah's knowledge you will die, then delay is forbidden, otherwise it is permissible, then it is invalid. Now, what is in Allah's knowledge is unknown to him, and this is close to this reasoning of the proponents of immediacy. Sheikh al-Haddad refuted it by saying that the mufti can answer: It is permissible for you to delay with the possibility of sin due to your death before reaching the next year, and then you are judged to have sinned. The gist is that it is permissible for him to delay while not failing in the entire lifetime, and there is no impossibility in it. It may also be answered that the criterion is based on probability. So for the mufti: It is permissible for you to delay if you think it likely to reach the next year, and then there is no sin, even if he dies suddenly. But if you have no expectation of safety, then delay is not permissible. And it is not hidden from you that the duration of a year prevents the occurrence of probability on either side, for death from prolonged illness over a week or a month is not rare, so where is the probability of safety? So this is an admission of obligation to immediate compliance, unlike Zakat and similar acts, for which one can say delay is permissible until the onset of a serious illness that makes death probable. So understand. And they said sixthly: Allah said: 'And hasten to forgiveness from your Lord' [3:133] and His statement: 'So race to good deeds' [2:148]. What is meant is their cause, for hastening to forgiveness is not intelligible, so its cause, which is obligatory acts, is intended. Similarly, if 'good deeds' means the rewards of the Hereafter, then the cause must be assumed. It can also be said that 'good deeds' are themselves the performance of obligatory acts, so there is no need for assumption. After all this, the noble verse indicates the obligation of hastening to perform obligations, so immediate compliance is necessary. We say: First: then if commands were for immediate compliance, this verse would be a confirmation of them. If they are not for immediate compliance, it would be a foundation. And foundation is preferable to confirmation, so the command is not for immediate compliance, so the proof is turned against them. And you may say: This noble verse indicates the obligation of haste. So if commands were not for immediate compliance, abrogation would be necessary, even by addition, which is against the principle. And confirmation is not of that status, so it is interpreted as such to avoid abrogation. So commands are either set for immediate compliance or used metaphorically, and the second is against the principle, so the first is determined. So ponder. And we said secondly: This command is interpreted as indicating superiority and recommendation; otherwise, anyone who performs an obligation in its time would not be called a 'haster' or 'racer'. And know that this interpretation is transmitted from Imam al-Shafi'i, may Allah have mercy on him, and he used it to prove the recommended nature of performing Fajr early and all prayers. And you know that it does not hold up against the mutawatir transmission from the Companions and those after them of delaying Fajr [until light], and the mutawatir transmission from the Messenger of Allah, peace be upon him, of delaying Zuhr in extreme heat. Moreover, even if it holds as a response, it does not hold as a proof due to the possibility of other interpretations, as will become clear later if Allah wills. And we said thirdly: If the proof were complete, it would indicate immediate compliance legally, but the discussion is about linguistic indication. So it is not relevant: if he means that commands came absolutely, then the condition of immediacy was added later, that is abrogation, which is not resorted to without cause. And if he means that commands became a legal reality for immediacy, then it necessitates linguistic transfer, which is against the principle, even though it would achieve the purpose, for then commands in the Lawgiver's speech would be interpreted as immediate. So ponder. And we said fourthly: If it were complete, it would indicate obligation of haste in all obligations, timed or otherwise, while among them are extended obligations whose delay to the end of the time is permissible, unless specified. Moreover, among them are those for which delay is recommended, such as Zuhr in summer, definitively. And we said fifthly: What is meant by 'hastening' is performing obligations before the arrival of death, and there is no doubt that delay until after death is not permissible, as in His statement: 'And spend from what We have provided you before death comes to one of you...' (63:10) and His statement: 'The repentance accepted by Allah is only for those who do evil in ignorance and then repent soon...' (4:17). And if 'forgiveness' is interpreted as the forgiveness of all sins, its cause is faith, so the utmost that is necessary is that
Source pages p. 417

Commentary
This passage is a detailed usul dispute about whether the imperative form (صيغة الأمر) indicates immediate compliance (الفور) or allows delay (التراخي). The text presents several arguments from those who claim الفور and gives the Hanafi responses. The discussion covers six arguments; here we have arguments three through six. The third argument is that command and prohibition are opposites, and since prohibition indicates immediate compliance, the command must also, otherwise contradictions would arise. The Hanafi response is that the prohibition is subsidiary to the command and does not itself indicate الفور. The fourth argument uses the Quranic verse where Allah rebukes Iblis for not prostrating immediately after the command. The Hanafi response: that command was restricted by the condition "when I have proportioned him...", so it was tied to a specific future event, not an absolute command. The fifth argument is a logical one: if delay were permissible, it would have to be until a specified time or the last possible moment, both of which lead to impossibility. The Hanafi response counters with the fact that one can explicitly say "delay" without contradiction (by consensus), and the impossibility only arises if delay is obligated, not if it is merely permitted. A benefit discusses Imam Muhammad's view on delaying Hajj: it is permissible but sinful if one dies before performing it. Sheikh al-Haddad responds that the mufti can give a conditional answer based on probability of survival, which shifts the assessment. The sixth argument uses the Quranic commands to "hasten" and "race" to good deeds. The Hanafi responses are multiple: first, if commands already indicate الفور, these verses would be a mere confirmation (تأكيد) which is less preferable than foundation (تأسيس); second, these verses are interpreted as recommending haste (ندب), not obligation; third, even if they indicated obligation legally, the discussion is about linguistic meaning; fourth, if taken literally, they would obligate haste in all obligations including those with extended time (like Zuhr in summer), which is false; fifth, 'hastening' means before death, not immediately in the moment.

Key Terms
الأمر (al-amr): The imperative form used to demand an action.
الفور (al-fawr): Immediate compliance; performing the commanded act right after the command.
التراخي (al-tarākhī): Permissibility of delay; the commanded act can be postponed.
نهي (nahy): Prohibition; demanding cessation of an action.
تابع (tābiʿ): Subsidiary; a ruling or element that follows the primary one.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi response in this lesson, why does the third argument (prohibition indicates الفور) fail?
A) The prohibition is subsidiary to the command and does not independently indicate الفور.
B) The argument leads to an infinite regress.
C) The prohibition does not exist in the case of commands.
D) The prohibition is a separate type of speech.

Key Takeaway
The prohibition analogy and the Iblis verse do not prove that the imperative inherently requires immediate compliance, because the prohibition is subsidiary and the command to Iblis was restricted.

15 min
The Logical Impossibility and Quranic Exhortations (Arguments 5 & 6)
Presents the fifth and sixth arguments for الفور with Hanafi rebuttals, including a benefit on delaying Hajj.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi rebuttals to the arguments from the impossibility of indefinite delay and from verses commanding hastening to good deeds.

Core Idea
Permitting delay does not entail impossibility; 'hasten' verses are best interpreted as recommendation or as urging before death.

The fifth and sixth arguments attempt to show that allowing delay leads to logical impossibility and that Quranic commands to 'hasten' prove immediate compliance. The Hanafi responses demonstrate that the impossibility is avoided by mere permission, not obligation, and that the Quranic verses are interpreted as recommendations or as urging actions before death.

Guided Path
1
Identify the opponent's fifth argument
The opponent argues: if delay is permissible, it would have to be to either a specified time (e.g., old age) or the last possible moment. Old age is indefinite, and the last possible moment is unknown, leading to impossibility. Thus, delay cannot be permissible.
This is a reductio ad absurdum.
2
Analyze the Hanafi response to the fifth argument
The Hanafi response: impossibility only arises if delay is obligated to the last moment, not if it is merely permitted. Furthermore, one can explicitly state 'delay' without contradiction, as proven by consensus. The solution is that the command is delegated to the mukallaf to act at any time within his ability.
The response distinguishes between obligated delay and permitted delay.
3
Identify the opponent's sixth argument
The opponent cites Quran 3:133 ('Hasten to forgiveness') and 2:148 ('Race to good deeds'). The argument: these verses command hastening, so the imperative requires الفور.
The verses explicitly use words for haste.
4
Analyze the first Hanafi response to the sixth argument
The first response: if commands already indicate الفور, these verses would be تأكيد (confirmation); if not, they are تأسيس (foundation). Foundation is preferable, so the verses do not prove الفور. Also, they could be interpreted as recommendation (ندب) rather than obligation.
This is a linguistic argument from usul.
5
Analyze the second and third Hanafi responses to the sixth argument
Second response: the verses are interpreted as recommending haste (ندب). Third response: even if they indicate obligation legally, the discussion is about linguistic meaning. Additionally, they would obligate haste in all obligations, including extended ones like Zuhr, which is false.
The point is that the verses cannot be taken literally for every command.

The third discourse on linguistic principles. It contradicts, and it returned... For the obtained is not sought, and the necessary from the proof is the present state, so it is only possible in it except the future, either immediately as you hold or after as was said, or absolutely as you say. So the necessary from the proof contradicts your claim, and the claimant is not necessary. This response is a prevention of the complete approximation or a summary refutation that if it were complete, it would necessitate impossibility. It was said: He does not mean by 'present' the 'now' such that what you mentioned would be necessary, but rather parts of the end of the past and the beginning of the future, which is the customary present. So 'immediate' is included in it, so no impossibility and the approximation is complete. I say: If what this speaker said were correct, then the command regarding the required would be connected to the customary present, and it would be like present tense forms, which is contrary to the consensus of Arabic grammarians. And 'immediate' is only the beginnings of the future customarily, so what follows from it is the difficulty [؟] backwards, so ponder. Yes, if it were said in the proof that the principle requires that the command be for the present because it is more frequent and it is not possible, and proximity is like the present, so for proximity the command is made for it, acting as much as possible, it is not far-fetched, as was said regarding the circumstantial accusative (ḥāl) governed by the verb: its origin is that it should be simultaneous with the agent, and since it was not in the past, 'perhaps' is used to be close. So understand. And they said thirdly: The prohibition is for immediate compliance, and the command is a prohibition against its opposite, so it is also for immediate compliance; otherwise, the contradiction of two opposites would be necessary. And the like of this has preceded in the matter of repetition, with the answer that this prohibition is subsidiary to the command and is not for immediate compliance. And they said fourthly: Allah's address to Iblis: 'What prevented you from prostrating when I commanded you?' [7:12] is a censure for not hastening to prostrate, so it is for immediate compliance, and all commands are of one type, so the form indicates it. We say: The command to prostrate is not absolute but restricted by His statement: 'So when I have proportioned him and breathed into him of My spirit, fall down to him in prostration' [15:29]. And the discussion was about the absolute command. As for the restricted command, it is according to the requirement of the restriction, whether immediate or delayed. Here it is restricted to immediacy, so it is for it, so ponder. And they said fifthly: If it were not for immediate compliance and delay were permissible, then either to a specified time, there is no proof for it. Even if it were said the specified time is old age, he said: Old age, for example, is not specified, for how many a young man dies suddenly, so old age is not realized there, so the obligatory act is missed. And how many an old man lives a long time, so the obligatory act can be performed after old age with delay. So old age is not fit to be a specifier for delay. Or to the last possible time, which is unknown, so delaying to it necessitates impossibility. We say: This is refuted by the permissibility of explicitly stating delay, for it is permissible by consensus, even though the premises of the proof also apply to it. And the solution to the proof is that impossibility only necessitates obligating delay to the last possible time, not delegating it to him to come at any time within the times of ability. So ponder. (Benefit) In al-Kashf, the doctrine of Imam Muhammad regarding the permissibility of delaying Hajj with sin due to failing in one's lifetime is invalidated: If a questioner asks: Hajj has become obligatory upon me, is it permissible for me to delay it to the second year while my safety [from death] is doubtful to me? If we say yes, then he does not sin if he dies having missed it. If we say no, then immediacy is necessary. If we say: If in Allah's knowledge you will die, then delay is forbidden, otherwise it is permissible, then it is invalid. Now, what is in Allah's knowledge is unknown to him, and this is close to this reasoning of the proponents of immediacy. Sheikh al-Haddad refuted it by saying that the mufti can answer: It is permissible for you to delay with the possibility of sin due to your death before reaching the next year, and then you are judged to have sinned. The gist is that it is permissible for him to delay while not failing in the entire lifetime, and there is no impossibility in it. It may also be answered that the criterion is based on probability. So for the mufti: It is permissible for you to delay if you think it likely to reach the next year, and then there is no sin, even if he dies suddenly. But if you have no expectation of safety, then delay is not permissible. And it is not hidden from you that the duration of a year prevents the occurrence of probability on either side, for death from prolonged illness over a week or a month is not rare, so where is the probability of safety? So this is an admission of obligation to immediate compliance, unlike Zakat and similar acts, for which one can say delay is permissible until the onset of a serious illness that makes death probable. So understand. And they said sixthly: Allah said: 'And hasten to forgiveness from your Lord' [3:133] and His statement: 'So race to good deeds' [2:148]. What is meant is their cause, for hastening to forgiveness is not intelligible, so its cause, which is obligatory acts, is intended. Similarly, if 'good deeds' means the rewards of the Hereafter, then the cause must be assumed. It can also be said that 'good deeds' are themselves the performance of obligatory acts, so there is no need for assumption. After all this, the noble verse indicates the obligation of hastening to perform obligations, so immediate compliance is necessary. We say: First: then if commands were for immediate compliance, this verse would be a confirmation of them. If they are not for immediate compliance, it would be a foundation. And foundation is preferable to confirmation, so the command is not for immediate compliance, so the proof is turned against them. And you may say: This noble verse indicates the obligation of haste. So if commands were not for immediate compliance, abrogation would be necessary, even by addition, which is against the principle. And confirmation is not of that status, so it is interpreted as such to avoid abrogation. So commands are either set for immediate compliance or used metaphorically, and the second is against the principle, so the first is determined. So ponder. And we said secondly: This command is interpreted as indicating superiority and recommendation; otherwise, anyone who performs an obligation in its time would not be called a 'haster' or 'racer'. And know that this interpretation is transmitted from Imam al-Shafi'i, may Allah have mercy on him, and he used it to prove the recommended nature of performing Fajr early and all prayers. And you know that it does not hold up against the mutawatir transmission from the Companions and those after them of delaying Fajr [until light], and the mutawatir transmission from the Messenger of Allah, peace be upon him, of delaying Zuhr in extreme heat. Moreover, even if it holds as a response, it does not hold as a proof due to the possibility of other interpretations, as will become clear later if Allah wills. And we said thirdly: If the proof were complete, it would indicate immediate compliance legally, but the discussion is about linguistic indication. So it is not relevant: if he means that commands came absolutely, then the condition of immediacy was added later, that is abrogation, which is not resorted to without cause. And if he means that commands became a legal reality for immediacy, then it necessitates linguistic transfer, which is against the principle, even though it would achieve the purpose, for then commands in the Lawgiver's speech would be interpreted as immediate. So ponder. And we said fourthly: If it were complete, it would indicate obligation of haste in all obligations, timed or otherwise, while among them are extended obligations whose delay to the end of the time is permissible, unless specified. Moreover, among them are those for which delay is recommended, such as Zuhr in summer, definitively. And we said fifthly: What is meant by 'hastening' is performing obligations before the arrival of death, and there is no doubt that delay until after death is not permissible, as in His statement: 'And spend from what We have provided you before death comes to one of you...' (63:10) and His statement: 'The repentance accepted by Allah is only for those who do evil in ignorance and then repent soon...' (4:17). And if 'forgiveness' is interpreted as the forgiveness of all sins, its cause is faith, so the utmost that is necessary is that
Source pages p. 417

Commentary
This passage is a detailed usul dispute about whether the imperative form (صيغة الأمر) indicates immediate compliance (الفور) or allows delay (التراخي). The text presents several arguments from those who claim الفور and gives the Hanafi responses. The discussion covers six arguments; here we have arguments three through six. The third argument is that command and prohibition are opposites, and since prohibition indicates immediate compliance, the command must also, otherwise contradictions would arise. The Hanafi response is that the prohibition is subsidiary to the command and does not itself indicate الفور. The fourth argument uses the Quranic verse where Allah rebukes Iblis for not prostrating immediately after the command. The Hanafi response: that command was restricted by the condition "when I have proportioned him...", so it was tied to a specific future event, not an absolute command. The fifth argument is a logical one: if delay were permissible, it would have to be until a specified time or the last possible moment, both of which lead to impossibility. The Hanafi response counters with the fact that one can explicitly say "delay" without contradiction (by consensus), and the impossibility only arises if delay is obligated, not if it is merely permitted. A benefit discusses Imam Muhammad's view on delaying Hajj: it is permissible but sinful if one dies before performing it. Sheikh al-Haddad responds that the mufti can give a conditional answer based on probability of survival, which shifts the assessment. The sixth argument uses the Quranic commands to "hasten" and "race" to good deeds. The Hanafi responses are multiple: first, if commands already indicate الفور, these verses would be a mere confirmation (تأكيد) which is less preferable than foundation (تأسيس); second, these verses are interpreted as recommending haste (ندب), not obligation; third, even if they indicated obligation legally, the discussion is about linguistic meaning; fourth, if taken literally, they would obligate haste in all obligations including those with extended time (like Zuhr in summer), which is false; fifth, 'hastening' means before death, not immediately in the moment.

Key Terms
تأسيس (ta'sīs): Establishing a new ruling that is not already conveyed.
تأكيد (ta'kīd): Confirmation of an already-established ruling.
ندب (nadb): Recommendation; an act that is encouraged but not obligatory.
تكليف محال (taklīf muḥāl): Obligation of the impossible.
موسعات (mūsa'āt): Extended obligations; those with a wide time window.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

D) Old age is a valid specifier for delay.

Key Takeaway
Permitting delay does not entail impossibility, and Quranic commands to 'hasten' are best interpreted as recommendations or as urgings to act before death, not as requiring immediate compliance.

Rule: Command to Command is Not a Direct Command
Stating a usul rule about command delegation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule that a command to command is not a direct command, and apply it to differentiate between direct and delegated commands.

Core Idea
A command to command another is not a direct command to the third party—obligation only arises from the immediate commander.

This lesson introduces a key usul principle: when a superior says to an intermediary 'Order so-and-so to do X', the third party is not directly obligated by the superior. Understanding this distinction is crucial for correctly assigning legal responsibility in hierarchical commands. The Hanafi school adopts this as the chosen view (mukhtar), which guides fatwa and legal reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Command Chain Components
Break down the situation into three roles: the original commander (amir), the intermediary (ma'mur bi al-amr), and the ultimate addressee (ma'mur). Recognize that the intermediary receives the first command and is tasked with issuing a second command.
2
State the Rule
The rule asserts that 'al-amr bi al-amr' (command to command) is not an 'amr' (command) from the original commander to the ultimate addressee. The ultimate addressee is only bound by the immediate command of the intermediary.
3
Understand the Reasoning
The intermediary is a separate legal agent; the original command is directed to him, not to the final party. The ultimate addressee only becomes obligated when the intermediary actually issues the command. This preserves clarity in legal responsibility.
4
Recognize the Chosen View (Mukhtar)
The text specifies 'ala al-mukhtar' (according to the chosen view), indicating that there is an alternative opinion within or outside the school. The Hanafi school prefers this position for its consistency with principles of direct obligation.
5
Apply to a Practical Example
Consider a judge who tells his deputy 'Order the defendant to pay the debt.' According to the rule, the defendant is not directly obligated by the judge's statement; only the deputy's subsequent command creates an obligation for the defendant.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة الأمر بالأمر بشيء لغيره ليس أمراً من الآمر للثاني على المختار، كقوله
Issue: The command to command something to another is not a command from the commander to the second person, according to the chosen view, like his saying...
Source pages p. 419

Commentary
This is a usul rule: when someone says to another 'Command so-and-so to do such-and-such', that statement is not a direct command to the second person (so-and-so). The second person is only obligated by the command of the immediate commander. This is the chosen position in the Hanafi school. For example, if a judge tells his deputy 'Order the defendant to pay', the defendant is not directly obliged by the judge's statement; he is obliged by the deputy's command. The text cuts off before providing an example.

Key Terms
الأمر بالأمر (al-amr bi al-amr): The act of commanding someone to command another; e.g., A says to B 'Command C to do D.'

Key Takeaway
The command to command is not a direct command—obligation only flows from the immediate commander.

Repeated Commands Without Conjunction Are Confirmatory
Determining when repeated commands are emphatic or independent

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that repeated commands without conjunction are confirmatory and distinguish it from cases where repetition imposes separate obligations.

Core Idea
Repeated commands without conjunction are usually confirmatory, not separate obligations.

In Hanafi Usul al-Fiqh, determining whether a repeated command imposes one or multiple obligations is a key skill. The default rule is that when a command is repeated consecutively without a conjunction, the second command emphasizes the first. This lesson unpacks this rule through three illustrative examples and an exception.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Rule and Its Scope
The passage states: when two commands are repeated consecutively without a conjunction (like 'do X, do X'), the default ruling is that the second command is confirmatory (ta’kīd), i.e., it does not impose a separate obligation. This applies to acts that can be repeated.
The phrase 'فيمَا يَقْبَلُ التَّكْرَارَ' limits the rule to actions that are capable of repetition.
2
Understand the Condition: Repeatability
The rule applies only to commands that can be performed multiple times. For actions that cannot be repeated (e.g., fasting a specific day), repetition is necessarily confirmatory because the act cannot be done twice.
The example 'صُمْ الْيَوْمَ صُمْ الْيَوْمَ' (Fast today, fast today) is mentioned as 'unlike' because fasting today cannot be repeated, but it is still confirmatory.
3
Examine the Three Confirmatory Examples
The passage provides three cases that are confirmatory by agreement: (1) 'Pray two rak‘ahs, pray two rak‘ahs' – because two rak‘ahs is a defined unit that cannot be performed twice in one prayer; (2) 'Give me a drink, give me a drink' – because the definite noun 'the drink' refers to the same object; (3) a case with a contextual indicator (qarīna juz’iyya) showing confirmation.
Each example has a distinct reason for being confirmatory, as explained in the source.
4
Distinguish the Exception
The phrase 'بخِلَاف صُمْ الْيَوْمَ صُمْ الْيَوْمَ' is understood as an exception: when the act does not accept repetition (like fasting a specific day), the repetition is still confirmatory, but it is not a separate instance of the rule. The rule's default is for repeatable acts.
The exception highlights that confirmatory status can stem from the nature of the act rather than the rule's default.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة إذا تكرر أمران متعاقبان غير متعاطفين فيما يقبل التكرار بخلاف صم اليوم صم اليوم التأكيد من تعريف نحو صل الركعتين صل الركعتين (أو غيره، كاسقني (ولا صارف) من اسقني فإنه أي فإن كل واحد من الثلاثة (مؤكد (اتفاقاً أما الأول فظاهر لعدم قبول المحل للفعل مرتين، وأما الثاني فلأن المعاد معرفة عين الأول، وأما الثالث فلدلالة قرينة جزئية،
Issue: If two commands are repeated consecutively without being conjoined by a conjunction (wāw), in what accepts repetition, unlike 'Fast today, fast today' – the repetition is confirmatory (muʾakkad). This is from the definition of something like 'Pray two rak‘ahs, pray two rak‘ahs' (or other, like 'Give me a drink, give me a drink') and there is no indicator to the contrary. For each of these three (is confirmatory) by agreement. As for the first, it is apparent because the locus does not admit the act twice. As for the second, because the repeated phrase is definite and identical to the first. As for the third, due to an incidental contextual indicator (qarīnah juz’iyyah).
Source pages p. 420

Commentary
This short passage presents a usul rule about the effect of repeating a command without a conjunction (like 'do X, do X'). It states that in cases where repetition is semantically possible (i.e., the act can be done multiple times), the repetition is generally confirmatory (ta’kīd), meaning the second command emphasizes the first rather than imposing a new obligation. Three examples are given: (1) 'Pray two rak‘ahs, pray two rak‘ahs' – here the act (two rak‘ahs) cannot be done twice because it is a defined unit, so repetition forces confirmation. (2) 'Give me a drink, give me a drink' – the phrase 'give me a drink' is definite and identical, so it is confirmatory. (3) A case where a contextual indicator shows it is confirmatory. The ruling is that all three are confirmatory by agreement. The text also notes an exception: 'Fast today, fast today' – this is not confirmatory because the act of fasting can be repeated, but due to its nature? Actually the text says 'bikhilāf ṣum al-yawm ṣum al-yawm' – meaning unlike that example, which is not confirmatory? The phrasing is a bit unclear, but the overall rule is that repetition without conjunction is usually confirmatory unless there is evidence to the contrary.

Key Terms
التأكيد (al-ta’kīd): Confirmatory repetition; the second command emphasizes the first without imposing a separate obligation.
المتعاطفين (al-mutaʿāṭifayn): Two things joined by a conjunction (like wāw). Here it refers to commands that are conjoined; if not conjoined, repetition tends to be confirmatory.
القرينة الجزئية (al-qarīnah al-juz’iyyah): A specific contextual indicator that shows the repetition is confirmatory.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to this passage, what is the default ruling when a command is repeated without a conjunction, like 'Pray two rak‘ahs, pray two rak‘ahs'?
A) It imposes two separate obligations to pray two rak‘ahs.
B) It is confirmatory (ta’kīd), emphasizing one obligation.
C) It is invalid because repetition is not allowed.
D) It depends on the intention of the speaker.

Key Takeaway
Repeated commands without conjunction are confirmatory by default; only a contextual indicator or the nature of the act can change this.

The Hanafi Definition of Nahy and Its Essential Components
Definition of nahy and its qualifications

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to dissect the Hanafi definition of nahy into its four components and explain the purpose of each qualification.

Core Idea
Nahy is a categorical demand to refrain, delivered from a position of authority.

The science of usul al-fiqh begins with precise definitions. In this passage, the Hanafi definition of nahy (prohibition) is unpacked word by word. Understanding each qualification—from 'demand' to 'high-handedness'—is essential to applying the correct ruling when encountering a prohibitive form in the Quran or Sunnah.

Guided Path
1
Break down the definition into its four components
Identify the key terms: 'demand' (iqtida'), 'refrain' (kaff), 'categorically' (hattan), 'high-handedness' (isti'la').
Each term isolates nahy from other types of requests.
2
Analyze each qualification
Explain why 'demand' excludes supplication, 'refrain' distinguishes from command, 'categorically' removes recommendations, and 'high-handedness' separates it from pleas.
The definition is hammered out to avoid overinclusion.
3
Address the objection about 'kuff an al-zina'
The phrase 'refrain from zina' appears to be a command, but the act to refrain from is zina itself, not the refraining. The imperative form is used for the demand to refrain, which is the essence of nahy.
This shows the subtlety between form and meaning.
4
Contrast with other definitions
Note al-Qadi's definition ('a statement that necessitates obedience') and others. The Hanafi one is more precise by including 'categorically' and 'high-handedness'.
Comparing definitions sharpens understanding.

Chapter on Prohibition: (Prohibition is a demand to refrain from an act categorically with high-handedness.) And extracting the benefit of the qualifications is analogous to what preceded in the command. (And it was objected) like (refrain from zina, for it is a demand to refrain from zina which is the act categorically, while it is a command. And the scholar answered that what is meant by the act refrained from is (the act that is the root of derivation of the thing demanded) and the root of derivation of 'kuff' (refrain) is not the demand to refrain from it, but rather from another act which is zina. This answer, though complete, yet since it includes an extra qualification, he said: (I say: The closest in answer is that in 'kuff' even though the demand is by the form, because the imperative form is placed for demand, but the demand to refrain is not by it, i.e., by the form, but the demand to refrain by the form is in 'do not commit zina', and it is the demand by the form that is the definition. Understand. (And it was said) in the answer: Refrain from zina (in consideration of the addition to refrain is a command, for it is a request, and in consideration of the addition to zina is a prohibition). This answer is on the assumption that the definition is for the psychological prohibition, but the goal of the usuli is not attached to it, and the definitions of others are learned by comparison to the command by contrast. So it ends on the model of al-Qadi's definition: a statement that necessitates the obedience of the one prohibited by refraining from that which he is prohibited from. And on the model of others' definitions: a person's statement to another 'do not do' with high-handedness, or the statement to one below him 'do not do' or the intention of refraining from the act. And objections arise against them similar to what arises there, and are repelled by what was repelled there. Then its form is used for seven meanings: Prohibition with high-handedness, like His saying: 'And do not kill your children for fear of poverty' [al-Isra: 31] and 'do not accept their testimony ever' [al-Nur?]; Dislike, like his saying: 'None of you should touch his penis' in the Sahih; Supplication, like His saying: 'Do not cause our hearts to deviate after You have guided us' [Al Imran: 8]; Guidance, like His saying: 'Do not ask about things which if they are disclosed to you, will harm you' [al-Ma'idah: 101]; and the apparent context of its revelation is that it is for prohibition; Derogation, like His saying: 'Do not extend your eyes' [Ta-Ha: 131]; Statement of consequence, like His saying: 'And do not think that Allah is unaware' [Ibrahim: 42]; Despair, like His saying: 'Do not make excuses today' [al-Tahrim: 7]. And it may come for other meanings such as equalization, threat, request, and disagreement as to whether it has a specific form placed in exchange for the demand of categorical refraining. And disagreement in its form: is it apparent in prohibition (hathr) without dislike (karaha), as the reliable ones among the people of ijtihad and usul hold, or the opposite, that it is apparent in dislike without prohibition, or a homonym between them, or a univocal term placed for the common meaning between prohibition and dislike, or suspended as preceded in the command. The statement 'it' and 'the disagreement' means the disagreement here is like the disagreement there. And the master reported consensus that it is for prohibition after obligation, not as they differed about the command that occurs after prohibition. And perhaps the consensus is denied, so one may differ in it.
Source pages p. 426

Commentary
This passage defines 'nahy' (prohibition) as a demand to refrain from an act categorically with a tone of high-handedness (istif'a'). The definition is broken down: 'demand' indicates it is a type of request, 'refrain' means to abstain, 'categorically' (hatman) emphasizes the binding nature, and 'high-handedness' distinguishes it from a mere plea. The text then addresses an objection: the phrase 'refrain from zina' itself seems to be a command (since it uses the imperative mood), yet it is a prohibition. The answer is that the act in question is not the refraining itself but the act of zina; the imperative form is used for the demand to refrain, but in 'do not commit zina', the demand is directly for refraining. The commentator favors an answer focusing on the linguistic form. The text then gives various definitions of nahy by other scholars, notably al-Qadi's definition. It then lists seven meanings of the prohibitive form (la taf'al): 1) Tahrim (prohibition) with high-handedness, citing Quranic verses on killing children and accepting testimony. 2) Karaha (dislike), citing a hadith about touching the penis. 3) Du'a (supplication), like the verse about not deviating hearts. 4) Irshad (guidance), like the verse about not asking harmful questions. 5) Tahqir (derogation), like the verse about not coveting. 6) Bayan al-aqiba (statement of consequence), like the verse about not thinking Allah unaware. 7) Ya's (despair), like the verse about not making excuses. Finally, there is a discussion on whether the form of nahy is primarily for tahrim or karaha, with opinions ranging from tahrim only, karaha only, homonymy, shared meaning, or suspended judgement. The author notes that consensus (supposedly) holds it is for tahrim after an obligation, but this consensus is contested.

Key Terms
النهي (al-nahy): A demand to refrain from an act categorically with high-handedness
الحظر (al-hathr): Prohibition that indicates something is haram
الكراهة (al-karaha): Dislike; an act that is not prohibited but discouraged

Seven Contextual Meanings of Nahy and the Default Ruling
List of seven meanings of the prohibitive form and dispute on default

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list the seven contextual meanings of the prohibitive form and argue for the Hanafi position that the default is tahrim.

Core Idea
Though النهي primarily means prohibition, the same form can convey supplication, guidance, or even hope—but the default remains the strongest.

After defining nahy, the text turns to the practical reality: the Arabic imperative negative (لا تفعل) is used in the Quran and Sunnah for various purposes. A scholar must recognize these contextual shifts without abandoning the default rule that the form indicates prohibition. This lesson explores those seven meanings and the debate over the primary meaning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the seven meanings
Go through each meaning as listed: tahrim, karaha, du'a, irshad, tahqir, bayan al-aqiba, ya's.
Memorize them in order.
2
Provide a Quranic example for each
For each meaning, recall the example given in the text: e.g., for tahrim: 'لا تقتلوا أولادكم'; for du'a: 'لا تزغ قلوبنا'.
The examples anchor the meanings.
3
Analyze the default ruling dispute
The text mentions five opinions: the form is for tahrim only, for karaha only, homonym, shared meaning, or suspended. Understand each.
The Hanafi position is that it is for tahrim unless context indicates otherwise.
4
Evaluate the Hanafi position
The majority of usulis and mujtahids hold it is for prohibiton. The text cites consensus (though contested).
This is the mufta bihi view.
5
Apply the rule to a new verse
Take a verse like 'لا تأكلوا الربا' and determine its meaning using the Hanafi default.
Practice applying the default rule.

Chapter on Prohibition: (Prohibition is a demand to refrain from an act categorically with high-handedness.) And extracting the benefit of the qualifications is analogous to what preceded in the command. (And it was objected) like (refrain from zina, for it is a demand to refrain from zina which is the act categorically, while it is a command. And the scholar answered that what is meant by the act refrained from is (the act that is the root of derivation of the thing demanded) and the root of derivation of 'kuff' (refrain) is not the demand to refrain from it, but rather from another act which is zina. This answer, though complete, yet since it includes an extra qualification, he said: (I say: The closest in answer is that in 'kuff' even though the demand is by the form, because the imperative form is placed for demand, but the demand to refrain is not by it, i.e., by the form, but the demand to refrain by the form is in 'do not commit zina', and it is the demand by the form that is the definition. Understand. (And it was said) in the answer: Refrain from zina (in consideration of the addition to refrain is a command, for it is a request, and in consideration of the addition to zina is a prohibition). This answer is on the assumption that the definition is for the psychological prohibition, but the goal of the usuli is not attached to it, and the definitions of others are learned by comparison to the command by contrast. So it ends on the model of al-Qadi's definition: a statement that necessitates the obedience of the one prohibited by refraining from that which he is prohibited from. And on the model of others' definitions: a person's statement to another 'do not do' with high-handedness, or the statement to one below him 'do not do' or the intention of refraining from the act. And objections arise against them similar to what arises there, and are repelled by what was repelled there. Then its form is used for seven meanings: Prohibition with high-handedness, like His saying: 'And do not kill your children for fear of poverty' [al-Isra: 31] and 'do not accept their testimony ever' [al-Nur?]; Dislike, like his saying: 'None of you should touch his penis' in the Sahih; Supplication, like His saying: 'Do not cause our hearts to deviate after You have guided us' [Al Imran: 8]; Guidance, like His saying: 'Do not ask about things which if they are disclosed to you, will harm you' [al-Ma'idah: 101]; and the apparent context of its revelation is that it is for prohibition; Derogation, like His saying: 'Do not extend your eyes' [Ta-Ha: 131]; Statement of consequence, like His saying: 'And do not think that Allah is unaware' [Ibrahim: 42]; Despair, like His saying: 'Do not make excuses today' [al-Tahrim: 7]. And it may come for other meanings such as equalization, threat, request, and disagreement as to whether it has a specific form placed in exchange for the demand of categorical refraining. And disagreement in its form: is it apparent in prohibition (hathr) without dislike (karaha), as the reliable ones among the people of ijtihad and usul hold, or the opposite, that it is apparent in dislike without prohibition, or a homonym between them, or a univocal term placed for the common meaning between prohibition and dislike, or suspended as preceded in the command. The statement 'it' and 'the disagreement' means the disagreement here is like the disagreement there. And the master reported consensus that it is for prohibition after obligation, not as they differed about the command that occurs after prohibition. And perhaps the consensus is denied, so one may differ in it.
Source pages p. 426

Commentary
This passage defines 'nahy' (prohibition) as a demand to refrain from an act categorically with a tone of high-handedness (istif'a'). The definition is broken down: 'demand' indicates it is a type of request, 'refrain' means to abstain, 'categorically' (hatman) emphasizes the binding nature, and 'high-handedness' distinguishes it from a mere plea. The text then addresses an objection: the phrase 'refrain from zina' itself seems to be a command (since it uses the imperative mood), yet it is a prohibition. The answer is that the act in question is not the refraining itself but the act of zina; the imperative form is used for the demand to refrain, but in 'do not commit zina', the demand is directly for refraining. The commentator favors an answer focusing on the linguistic form. The text then gives various definitions of nahy by other scholars, notably al-Qadi's definition. It then lists seven meanings of the prohibitive form (la taf'al): 1) Tahrim (prohibition) with high-handedness, citing Quranic verses on killing children and accepting testimony. 2) Karaha (dislike), citing a hadith about touching the penis. 3) Du'a (supplication), like the verse about not deviating hearts. 4) Irshad (guidance), like the verse about not asking harmful questions. 5) Tahqir (derogation), like the verse about not coveting. 6) Bayan al-aqiba (statement of consequence), like the verse about not thinking Allah unaware. 7) Ya's (despair), like the verse about not making excuses. Finally, there is a discussion on whether the form of nahy is primarily for tahrim or karaha, with opinions ranging from tahrim only, karaha only, homonymy, shared meaning, or suspended judgement. The author notes that consensus (supposedly) holds it is for tahrim after an obligation, but this consensus is contested.

Key Terms
النهي (al-nahy): A demand to refrain from an act categorically with high-handedness
الحظر (al-hathr): Prohibition that indicates something is haram
الكراهة (al-karaha): Dislike; an act that is not prohibited but discouraged

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, which of the following is NOT one of the seven meanings of the prohibitive form?
A) Tahrim (prohibition)
B) Karaha (dislike)
C) Tahqir (derogation)
D) Ibahah (permissibility)

Key Takeaway
The prohibitive form defaults to prohibition unless a clear contextual indicator shifts its meaning.
