Classification of Prohibitory Attributes: Lazim and Mujawir
Classifies prohibitory attributes into necessary and separable

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between a necessary (lazim) and a separable (mujawir) prohibitory attribute and correctly classify examples.

Core Idea
The legal effect of a prohibition depends on whether the cause is inseparable from the act.

In Hanafi usul, not all prohibitions are treated equally. Scholars classify the attribute that makes an act prohibited into two types: necessary (lazim) and separable (mujawir). This classification determines whether the act is haram or makruh tahrimi, and whether qada' is required if the act is performed and broken. This lesson unpacks the first classification.

Guided Path
1
Understand the concept of the 'prohibitory attribute'
Identify that acts are prohibited because of an attribute (al-ghayr) that makes them ugly (qabih). This attribute can be either inseparable or separable.
2
Introduce the two categories: lazim and mujawir
Lazim: the attribute cannot be separated from the act. Mujawir: the attribute may be separate. Note the Arabic terms.
The passage uses 'mujawir' for separable, also called 'mufariq'.
3
Apply the categories to examples
Fasting on Eid: the turning away from hospitality is inseparable → lazim. Selling during Friday call: the neglect of Friday prayer is separable → mujawir. Marriage of tahlil: the intention of tahlil is separable → mujawir.
These examples are from the source text.
4
Understand the ruling for each category
For lazim: if proven by qat'i evidence → haram; otherwise → makruh. For mujawir: always called makruh (tahrimi), never haram.
This distinction is unique to Hanafi usul.
5
Recognize the implication for qada'
The classification directly affects whether the entire act is void from the start, which influences the obligation of qada' (covered in next lesson).
Here we only note the implication; the analysis of qada' is in Lesson 2.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم اعلم أن مشايخنا قسموا الغير الذي به القبح في المنهي عنه إلى لازم كما في صوم العيد، فإن الحرمة للإعراض عن قبول الضيافة ولا ينفك عنه صوم يوم العيد وإن انفكاك الصوم مطلقاً وإلى أمر مجاور قد ينفك عنه، كما في البيع وقت النداء فإنه ما نهى عنه إلا للإخلال بالجمعة، وهو قد ينفك عنه كما في البيع مع السعي ونكاح المحلل فإنه إنما نهي لمقارنته نية التحليل والنكاح قد ينفك عنه، فالقسم الأول إن ثبت بدليل قطعي فيطلقون عليه الحرام وإلا فالمكروه، وعلى القسم الثاني، لا يطلقون لفظ الحرام، إنما يطلقون لفظ المكروه ويقولون: البيع وقت النداء والصلاة في الدار المغصوبة ونكاح المحلل مكروه وأرادوا به كراهة التحريم، ثم إنهم لا يوجبون القضاء على من شرع في صوم العيد ثم أفسده، لأن وجوب القضاء إنما كان لوجوب الإتمام، ووجوب الإتمام لصحة الشروع وصيانة ما أدى والشروع فيه غير صحيح، وما أدى واجب الرفع فلا صيانة، فلا وجوب فلا قضاء، هذا أوجبوا الصلاة بالشروع في الوقت المكروه، وفرقوا بأن الصوم وقته معيار، ففساده يؤثر في فساد الصوم من الأصل، وكل جزء منه مشتمل على معصية، وهي الأعراض بخلاف الصلاة، فإن وقتها غير معيار ولا كل جزء مشتمل على المعصية وإنما تتم بالسجدة وأنت لا يذهب عليك أنه لا دخل فيه للمعيارية، فإن الشروعين متساويان في كونه معصية لأجل الغير، فإن كان هذا آخر أجاله عن سببيته لوجوب الإتمام فهما سيان وإلا وجبا فالأولى أن يكتفي بحديث مقارنة المعصية ويقال إن إتمام الصوم إنما يجب صيانة لما أدّى وكل ما أدى لا يخلو عن الأعراض، والصلاة إنما يجب إتمامها صيانة للتحريمة عن البطلان وليس في التحريمة تشبه بعبادة الكفار فلا معصية، فلا تخرج عن السببية إنما المعصية في أداء ركن من الأركان من القيام والركوع ونحوه، وعلى هذا لا يرد أنه يلزم أن لا يحرم إلا الركعة التامة لا ما دونها لأن ما دون الركعة ليس صلاة، وذلك لأن ما دون الركعة عبادة صلاتية، فتحرم في هذه الأوقات كالركعة لوجود التشبه المنهي.
Then know that our scholars divided the 'other' by which ugliness exists in the prohibited thing into: (1) Necessary (lazim), as in fasting on Eid, for the prohibition is due to turning away from accepting hospitality, and it does not separate from the fast of Eid day—though the separation of fasting absolutely [is possible]; and (2) Adjacent/separable (mujawir) that may separate from it, as in selling at the time of the call (to Friday prayer), for it is only prohibited due to neglecting the Friday prayer, and it may separate from it as in selling while walking to prayer; and the marriage of tahlil, for it is only prohibited due to its accompaniment with the intention of tahlil, and marriage may separate from it. The first category: if established by a definitive proof, they call it haram; otherwise, makruh. As for the second category, they do not use the term haram; they only use the term makruh and say: selling at the time of the call, prayer in a usurped house, and the marriage of tahlil are makruh, meaning makruh tahrimi. Then they do not require qada' for one who begins fasting on Eid and then breaks it, because the obligation of qada' is due to the obligation of completion, and the obligation of completion is due to the validity of the commencement and preserving what was performed. The commencement is not valid, and what was performed is required to be removed, so no preservation, so no obligation, so no qada'. However, they require qada' for prayer commenced in a prohibited time. They differentiated: Fasting's time is continuous (miyar), so its corruption affects the corruption of the fast from the beginning, and every part of it contains disobedience (the turning away). Prayer is different: its time is not continuous, nor does every part contain disobedience; it is only completed by the prostration. You are not unaware that there is no role of continuity here, for both commencements are equal in being disobedience due to something else. If this [disobedience] is the final preventer of its causality for the obligation of completion, then both are equal; otherwise, both are obligated. The better approach is to suffice with the hadith of accompaniment of disobedience and say: The completion of fasting is obligatory only to preserve what was performed, and all that was performed is not free from turning away. Prayer's completion is obligatory to preserve the opening takbir from invalidity, and there is no imitation of the worship of disbelievers in the opening takbir, so no disobedience. Thus, it does not leave causality; the disobedience is only in the performance of a pillar like standing, bowing, etc. Based on this, it is not problematic that only the complete rak'ah is prohibited, not less than that, because less than a rak'ah is not prayer. Rather, less than a rak'ah is a prayer-like act of worship, so it is prohibited in these times like a rak'ah due to the existence of the prohibited imitation.
Source pages p. 438

Commentary
This passage presents a Hanafi classification of prohibitions based on the nature of the attribute (al-ghayr) that makes an act prohibited. Our scholars (mashayikhuna) divide it into two types: (1) Necessary (lazim) attribute—one that cannot be separated from the act, such as fasting on Eid where the turning away from divine hospitality is inseparable from the fast. (2) Separable (mujawir) attribute—one that may be absent, such as selling during the Friday call (it is prohibited due to neglecting Friday prayer, but if one also makes an effort to attend, it becomes permissible) or the marriage of tahleel (prohibited due to the intention of making a woman lawful for her former husband, but if that intention is absent, the marriage is valid). For the first type, if established by a definitive proof, it is called haram; otherwise, makruh. For the second type, they never call it haram, only makruh (meaning makruh tahrimi in many cases). Then the passage discusses qada' (make-up) for such acts. For fasting on Eid, if one begins and breaks it, no qada' is required because the commencement was invalid and the performed act is to be removed. For prayer in a prohibited time, qada' is required because the time is not continuous and the opening takbir contains no disobedience. The author then offers a refined view: the difference lies in the fact that the prohibition in fasting is due to an inseparable attribute of the whole fast, while in prayer, the prohibition attaches to the performance of the pillars, not the opening takbir. Therefore, less than a rak'ah is also prohibited.

Key Terms
لازم (lāzim): An attribute that necessarily accompanies an act and cannot be separated from it.
مفارق (مجاور) (mufāriq (mujāwir)): An attribute that may be separate from the act, i.e., not inherent.
كراهة تحريم (karāhah taḥrīmiyyah): A level of prohibition close to haram, used for acts prohibited by a non-definitive proof or due to a separable attribute.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi scholars, which of the following is an example of a necessary (lazim) prohibitory attribute?
A) Selling during Friday call
B) Fasting on Eid
C) Marriage of tahlil
D) None

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, the division of prohibitory attributes into lazim and mujawir is fundamental: it determines whether an act is haram or makruh tahrimi and whether the entire act is corrupt from the start.

Qada' for Prohibited Acts: Fasting on Eid vs. Prayer in Prohibited Time
Examines the ruling on qada' for prohibited acts using the classification of attributes

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why Hanafis do not require qada' for broken Eid fast but do require qada' for prayer in a prohibited time, and differentiate the underlying reasoning.

Core Idea
No qada' is required when the prohibition makes every part of the act a sin; qada' is required when the sin is only in part of the act.

Once the classification of prohibitory attributes is understood, we can apply it to the ruling on qada' (make-up). The Hanafi scholars rule that one who begins fasting on Eid and breaks it does not owe qada', while one who prays in a prohibited time does. This lesson unpacks the reasoning, including the concepts of continuous (miyar) vs. discontinuous time, and the refined view based on the nature of the act's components.

Guided Path
1
Recall the two examples and their classification
Fasting on Eid (lazim attribute) and prayer in a prohibited time (mujawir attribute). The classification sets the stage for the qada' ruling.
2
State the ruling for each
No qada' for fasting on Eid if broken; qada' is required for prayer in a prohibited time if commenced and invalidated.
This is the Hanafi position.
3
Analyze the reasoning: the continuity (miyar) argument
Fasting's time is continuous: the entire day is one unit, and each part contains the prohibited attribute (turning away). Prayer's time is discontinuous: the act is completed by prostration, and the opening takbir contains no sin.
The author acknowledges this argument has flaws because the commencement is sinful in both cases.
4
Understand the refined view: the hadith of accompaniment
The author suggests relying on the hadith that disobedience accompanies the act. For fasting, every moment includes disobedience; for prayer, the disobedience only attaches to the performance of pillars (standing, bowing), not the opening takbir. Thus, the obligation to preserve the opening takbir does not carry the sin.
This resolves the potential objection about the commencement being sinful in both.
5
Derive the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: Hanafis do not require qada' for Eid fast; Shafi'is (if they do not make this distinction) might require qada'.
This highlights the importance of the classification.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم اعلم أن مشايخنا قسموا الغير الذي به القبح في المنهي عنه إلى لازم كما في صوم العيد، فإن الحرمة للإعراض عن قبول الضيافة ولا ينفك عنه صوم يوم العيد وإن انفكاك الصوم مطلقاً وإلى أمر مجاور قد ينفك عنه، كما في البيع وقت النداء فإنه ما نهى عنه إلا للإخلال بالجمعة، وهو قد ينفك عنه كما في البيع مع السعي ونكاح المحلل فإنه إنما نهي لمقارنته نية التحليل والنكاح قد ينفك عنه، فالقسم الأول إن ثبت بدليل قطعي فيطلقون عليه الحرام وإلا فالمكروه، وعلى القسم الثاني، لا يطلقون لفظ الحرام، إنما يطلقون لفظ المكروه ويقولون: البيع وقت النداء والصلاة في الدار المغصوبة ونكاح المحلل مكروه وأرادوا به كراهة التحريم، ثم إنهم لا يوجبون القضاء على من شرع في صوم العيد ثم أفسده، لأن وجوب القضاء إنما كان لوجوب الإتمام، ووجوب الإتمام لصحة الشروع وصيانة ما أدى والشروع فيه غير صحيح، وما أدى واجب الرفع فلا صيانة، فلا وجوب فلا قضاء، هذا أوجبوا الصلاة بالشروع في الوقت المكروه، وفرقوا بأن الصوم وقته معيار، ففساده يؤثر في فساد الصوم من الأصل، وكل جزء منه مشتمل على معصية، وهي الأعراض بخلاف الصلاة، فإن وقتها غير معيار ولا كل جزء مشتمل على المعصية وإنما تتم بالسجدة وأنت لا يذهب عليك أنه لا دخل فيه للمعيارية، فإن الشروعين متساويان في كونه معصية لأجل الغير، فإن كان هذا آخر أجاله عن سببيته لوجوب الإتمام فهما سيان وإلا وجبا فالأولى أن يكتفي بحديث مقارنة المعصية ويقال إن إتمام الصوم إنما يجب صيانة لما أدّى وكل ما أدى لا يخلو عن الأعراض، والصلاة إنما يجب إتمامها صيانة للتحريمة عن البطلان وليس في التحريمة تشبه بعبادة الكفار فلا معصية، فلا تخرج عن السببية إنما المعصية في أداء ركن من الأركان من القيام والركوع ونحوه، وعلى هذا لا يرد أنه يلزم أن لا يحرم إلا الركعة التامة لا ما دونها لأن ما دون الركعة ليس صلاة، وذلك لأن ما دون الركعة عبادة صلاتية، فتحرم في هذه الأوقات كالركعة لوجود التشبه المنهي.
Then know that our scholars divided the 'other' by which ugliness exists in the prohibited thing into: (1) Necessary (lazim), as in fasting on Eid, for the prohibition is due to turning away from accepting hospitality, and it does not separate from the fast of Eid day—though the separation of fasting absolutely [is possible]; and (2) Adjacent/separable (mujawir) that may separate from it, as in selling at the time of the call (to Friday prayer), for it is only prohibited due to neglecting the Friday prayer, and it may separate from it as in selling while walking to prayer; and the marriage of tahlil, for it is only prohibited due to its accompaniment with the intention of tahlil, and marriage may separate from it. The first category: if established by a definitive proof, they call it haram; otherwise, makruh. As for the second category, they do not use the term haram; they only use the term makruh and say: selling at the time of the call, prayer in a usurped house, and the marriage of tahlil are makruh, meaning makruh tahrimi. Then they do not require qada' for one who begins fasting on Eid and then breaks it, because the obligation of qada' is due to the obligation of completion, and the obligation of completion is due to the validity of the commencement and preserving what was performed. The commencement is not valid, and what was performed is required to be removed, so no preservation, so no obligation, so no qada'. However, they require qada' for prayer commenced in a prohibited time. They differentiated: Fasting's time is continuous (miyar), so its corruption affects the corruption of the fast from the beginning, and every part of it contains disobedience (the turning away). Prayer is different: its time is not continuous, nor does every part contain disobedience; it is only completed by the prostration. You are not unaware that there is no role of continuity here, for both commencements are equal in being disobedience due to something else. If this [disobedience] is the final preventer of its causality for the obligation of completion, then both are equal; otherwise, both are obligated. The better approach is to suffice with the hadith of accompaniment of disobedience and say: The completion of fasting is obligatory only to preserve what was performed, and all that was performed is not free from turning away. Prayer's completion is obligatory to preserve the opening takbir from invalidity, and there is no imitation of the worship of disbelievers in the opening takbir, so no disobedience. Thus, it does not leave causality; the disobedience is only in the performance of a pillar like standing, bowing, etc. Based on this, it is not problematic that only the complete rak'ah is prohibited, not less than that, because less than a rak'ah is not prayer. Rather, less than a rak'ah is a prayer-like act of worship, so it is prohibited in these times like a rak'ah due to the existence of the prohibited imitation.
Source pages p. 438

Commentary
This passage presents a Hanafi classification of prohibitions based on the nature of the attribute (al-ghayr) that makes an act prohibited. Our scholars (mashayikhuna) divide it into two types: (1) Necessary (lazim) attribute—one that cannot be separated from the act, such as fasting on Eid where the turning away from divine hospitality is inseparable from the fast. (2) Separable (mujawir) attribute—one that may be absent, such as selling during the Friday call (it is prohibited due to neglecting Friday prayer, but if one also makes an effort to attend, it becomes permissible) or the marriage of tahleel (prohibited due to the intention of making a woman lawful for her former husband, but if that intention is absent, the marriage is valid). For the first type, if established by a definitive proof, it is called haram; otherwise, makruh. For the second type, they never call it haram, only makruh (meaning makruh tahrimi in many cases). Then the passage discusses qada' (make-up) for such acts. For fasting on Eid, if one begins and breaks it, no qada' is required because the commencement was invalid and the performed act is to be removed. For prayer in a prohibited time, qada' is required because the time is not continuous and the opening takbir contains no disobedience. The author then offers a refined view: the difference lies in the fact that the prohibition in fasting is due to an inseparable attribute of the whole fast, while in prayer, the prohibition attaches to the performance of the pillars, not the opening takbir. Therefore, less than a rak'ah is also prohibited.

Key Terms
لازم (lāzim): An attribute that necessarily accompanies an act and cannot be separated from it.
مفارق (مجاور) (mufāriq (mujāwir)): An attribute that may be separate from the act, i.e., not inherent.
كراهة تحريم (karāhah taḥrīmiyyah): A level of prohibition close to haram, used for acts prohibited by a non-definitive proof or due to a separable attribute.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why do Hanafis not require qada' for fasting on Eid?
A) Because the prohibition is only makruh.
B) Because the fast is invalid from the start due to an inseparable prohibitory attribute, so no valid commencement to preserve.
C) Because Eid fast is not obligatory.
D) Because the Sahibayn disagreed.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi position on qada' for prohibited acts hinges on whether the prohibitory attribute is inseparable (lazim) or separable (mujawir), with only the latter requiring make-up due to the presence of a valid commencing element.

Causality in Conditional Divorce: Hanafi vs. Logicians
Analyzing whether a conditional statement is an immediate legal cause in talaq

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi usul principle that a conditional statement in talaq is a sabab in the present and evaluate the logicians' counterargument.

Core Idea
A conditional divorce phrase is a cause now, though the divorce itself is delayed until the condition is met.

This passage delves into a classic usul debate: does a conditional divorce statement (e.g., 'If you enter, you are divorced') have causal power at the moment of utterance, or is it merely a hypothetical? The Hanafi school, led by Abu Hanifa, asserts that it is a cause (sabab) in the present, with the effect suspended. Logicians (ahl al-mizan) argue it only expresses logical necessity, not real causality. Understanding this debate sharpens your ability to analyse how shari'a rulings operate through conditional language.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify what Abu Hanifa holds: the conditional phrase is a cause in the present because it conveys the reality of future divorce. The condition only delays the effect, not the causality.
2
Identify the logicians' objection
Logicians argue that a conditional (e.g., 'If P then Q') only states a hypothetical necessity (luzum) between condition and consequence; it does not cause Q to occur. The statement has no causal power until the condition is fulfilled.
3
Explain the Hanafi response using ta'liq
The Hanafis counter that shari'a recognises suspended divorces (ta'liq) as valid. The conditional statement is a complete utterance indicating intent; the condition does not negate causality but only postpones the effect. Legal practice confirms this.
4
Trace the second proof (whole without part analogy)
Some argue that a cause without its effect is like a whole without a part, which is impossible. Therefore, if the effect (divorce) is absent now, the cause must also be absent. The text critiques this analogy, noting that the cause's necessity for the effect is not like a whole-part relation.
5
Conclude with the preferred view
The text favours the Hanafi position, showing that the logicians' analysis fails to account for the legal operation of shari'a. The conditional statement is a cause from the moment of utterance, though the effect is suspended.

For the case of its actual occurrence, and if it is beneficial for the ruling, it becomes a cause leading to it, so resorting to it benefits at that time. This is exactly as Imam Abu Hanifa says: that the added (al-mudaf) like 'you are divorced tomorrow' is a cause in the present, because it conveys the actual occurrence of divorce but tomorrow. But you could say in the exposition that this is only valid if the meaning of conditionality is the establishment of the consequence (jaza) in reality upon the fulfillment of the condition, which is false and not taken into account. So what is suitable for intent is the establishment of its ruling upon the assumption of the existence of the condition, in the manner of a hypothetical categorical (hamliyya taqdiriya), which is equivalent to the logical conditional (shartiyya mizaniyya), so there is no leading (ifda') nor causality (sababiyya). Yes, Abu Hanifa benefits from resorting to the school of logicians (ahl al-mantiq), because since the sum of the condition and consequence is a meaningful statement, and the consequence is like part of the sentence, it does not benefit anything, so it is not leading to occurrence, so there is no causality at all. As for the sum of condition and consequence, it only benefits suspension (ta'liq), so it does not necessitate the occurrence of the suspended thing, since its truth does not require the occurrence of either side. Similarly, the performative (insha'iyya) only benefits the creation of a binding relation between one thing and another, and does not necessitate the occurrence of that thing. Rather, if you reflect, you would find that the truth is the statement of this great Imam, the scholar of excellence, may mercy and pleasure be upon him, for if the ruling pertains to the relation between condition and consequence, you have already known, and if the ruling pertains to the consequence, it is not a real ruling but a hypothetical one, as in the hypothetical categorical, which is equivalent to the logical conditional, so it does not necessitate the occurrence of the suspended thing nor lead to it. And from what we have established, you see the refutation of what was said: that the school of the logicians (ahl al-mizan) is not suitable for building the negation of causality, because its outcome reduces to the necessity (luzum) of the condition for the consequence, and this does not contradict causality nor necessitate it. So understand and be grateful. The opener of divine secrets, Abi (may God sanctify his soul) said: This only indicates that the consequence alone is not a cause, and it is possible that the sum of condition and consequence is a cause in the present, but the occurrence in the future upon existence of the condition, which is what Islamic legal codes require. How could it be otherwise? The husband did not perform any act except this conditional statement, and no act occurred from him at the time of the condition's existence to be the cause; rather, he becomes a divorcer (mutliq) due to the issuance of this conditional statement. How could it be otherwise, since he may not be qualified for action at the time of the condition's existence, as if he becomes insane or another obstacle occurs. And if the cause is the conditional speech, then the meaning of the invalidity of its causality is that 'if you are divorced' invalidates its causality due to the condition in the real world. Rather, the causality belongs to the conditional statement (shartiyya) for the occurrence of divorce at the arrival of the condition. And if the condition is made a preventer (mani') from the ruling, then its meaning is that 'you are divorced' was a cause leading to the occurrence of divorce if the condition had not prevented it, for it prevented it from causing the ruling of occurrence of divorce. And on this basis, it is not correct to derive the validity of suspending divorce upon ownership unless it is established that ownership is not required for the soundness of causality of this suspended thing, and that is extremely difficult. So deliberate. And you could say: The cause is what leads to the existence of the effect, and it is clear that the sum of condition and consequence only benefits a suspensive ruling of necessity of one for the other. As for the actual occurrence of the consequence, it does not benefit it, so it has no leading (ifda') to be a cause. Yes, after the condition is fulfilled, the consequence occurs, so at that time it leads to the effect. So if he says 'If you enter, you are divorced,' nothing has come from him except the ruling of correlation (mulazama) between them, so he is not a divorcer at present, but after entry he becomes a divorcer. And the absence of action from him at the time of the condition's existence is conceded, but something came from him that becomes an action at the time of the condition's existence by virtue of divine law. And this—that something that came from him becomes an action—does not necessitate the establishment of legal capacity (ahliyya) at that time, but rather after he is a divorcer. It prevents prevention. The third article on linguistic principles: 'Ahlan': at the time of existence of that thing, how is it that insanity does not negate his being a divorcer or a manumitter, but rather negates the validity of speech, and his speech during insanity is not considered. Here, the speech occurred at the time of sanity, and its becoming a divorce according to him by divine law, and there is no harm in it. So on this basis, suspending divorce and manumission upon ownership is valid, because it is speech and not a divorce in the present, so it does not necessitate the establishment of legal capacity. Rather, it becomes a divorce at the condition, which is ownership, and then there is no obstacle to validity. So understand. And here occurred a type of verbosity, but we preferred it because it was a place of slipping of the feet of the deeply rooted scholars, so we established it, hopefully not exceeding the truth from what we informed you. And upon God is reliance, for He is the Knower of His rulings. And he argued firstly: Causality (sababiyya) is established only by effect (ta'thir) in the locus (mahall), because the cause is an action from a qualified person applied to a locus. Hence, sale of a free person is not a cause for ownership due to lack of a locus (mahall). And suspension (ta'liq) prevents effect, so no causality. (I say: It is directed towards him: prevention of prevention, i.e., prevention of suspension from effect, for it is possible that it does not prevent effect, but rather only delays the ruling. How is this anything but a repetition of the claim? And there is consideration in it, for it is a premise reasoned in the exposition. That is because the condition only enters upon the cause (sabab) not upon the ruling (hukm), so the cause becomes suspended, so there is no causality nor effect before it. How is it? If he says 'If you enter, you are divorced,' he intended only the divorce at entry, not in the present. And it was objected to by the opener of divine secrets, Abi (may God sanctify his soul): First, that the cause is not 'you are divorced' but the sum of condition and consequence. As for 'you are divorced' alone, it is a cause for occurrence of divorce in the present, but it has left causality due to being accompanied by the condition, and the sum becomes a cause for occurrence of divorce upon entry, and you have known that it is not suitable for causality. So remember. Second, we concede that the consequence alone is a cause, but the thing suspended by the condition is the occurrence of separation (furqa), not the effectuation from the husband. If he claims otherwise, it is forbidden. There must be evidence. Rather, by this, the woman becomes such that she is divorced upon entry, even if this attribute was not from before. And this is a type of effect. This is solid speech, but you could say: Divorce (tatliq) is only the import of 'you are divorced,' especially in the view of the Shafi'is. And if it is suspended, the divorce becomes suspended as well, not only the occurrence of divorce. And if it becomes suspended, it has no effect at all. And the meaning of the woman being such that she is divorced upon entry is only that she is suitable for divorce to occur upon entry by the attachment of the necessary divorce from him to her, just as at the time of marriage she is such that she is divorced upon attachment of the husband's divorce. As for her being in that state by the existing divorce now, it is false, because it is suspended. So deliberate. And you could establish that suspension prevents effect by saying that it only benefits the ruling of necessity of one for the other, not the establishment of anything in actual fact, so it has no effect on occurrence nor leading. And then the objection is not directed towards him, so understand. And it was objected upon the proof that if suspension prevents attachment to the locus, then it must be void like immediate (tanjiz) in the case of a foreign woman (ajnabiyya) is void due to lack of finding a locus, and like sale of a free person is void. And it was answered that the desired thing (marjuw) is within the scope of causality, so it benefits at that time and is not void. And it is void like 'you are divorced if God wills' i.e., its like, which is not desired because knowledge of the occurrence of the condition is uncertain. And he argued secondly: The cause (sabab) without it, i.e., without the ruling, is like the whole without the part, because it is necessary for it (luzum) like that. And the existence of the whole without the part is false, so likewise the existence of the cause without the ruling. And the ruling is absent by agreement, so the cause is likewise. And there is consideration in it, because the necessity (mulzumiyya) of the cause for the effect is denied such that it would be like the whole without the part. The better way is to say: The principle regarding the cause is that the ruling is necessary for it, because it is...
Source pages p. 466

Commentary
This passage from 'Fawatih al-Rahmut bi Sharh Musallam al-Thubut' presents a detailed usul al-fiqh analysis of causality (sababiyya) in conditional statements, specifically regarding divorce (talaq). The author examines the Hanafi position, attributed to Imam Abu Hanifa, that a conditional phrase like 'if you enter, you are divorced' constitutes a cause (sabab) at the moment of utterance, even though the effect (the divorce) occurs later upon fulfillment of the condition. This is contrasted with the view of logicians (ahl al-mizan) who argue that such a statement only expresses a hypothetical judgment (qadiyya shartiyya) without causing any real effect. The author defends Abu Hanifa by showing that the logical analysis of conditionals does not adequately account for the legal operation of shari'a rulings. He addresses various objections, including the argument that the conditional statement merely creates a relation of necessity (luzum) between condition and consequence, and that it does not cause anything until the condition occurs. The concept of 'ta'liq' (suspension) is central: the suspended matter (mu'allaq) is not brought into existence by the initial statement; rather, the statement prepares the ground for the effect upon condition. The author also discusses the role of legal capacity (ahliyya) and the locus (mahall), arguing that the husband's statement is an act of speech that, by divine law, becomes operative at the time of the condition. The passage also touches on the validity of suspending divorce upon ownership, citing that such suspension is valid because it does not require immediate capacity. Throughout, the author engages with the objections of 'Muth al-Asrar al-Ilahiyya' (Abi) and provides rebuttals. The passage concludes with a second proof: that a cause without its effect is like a whole without a part, which is impossible, but this analogy is contested. The preferred view is that the cause inherently necessitates the ruling. Overall, the passage reinforces the Hanafi usul principle that conditional statements in legal contexts (like talaq and 'itaq) are effective as causes in the present, with the effect being suspended until the condition is realized.

Key Terms
سبب (sabab): A cause that leads to a legal effect (hukm). In conditional divorce, the statement is a sabab for divorce occurring upon condition.
تعليق (ta'liq): Suspension of a ruling upon a future condition. The ruling is not immediate but becomes effective when the condition is met.
جزاء (jaza'): The consequence or result of a condition; in a conditional divorce, it is the statement 'you are divorced' or the divorce itself.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position defended in this passage, what is the status of the statement 'if you enter, you are divorced'?
A) It is a cause (sabab) for divorce immediately upon utterance, with no waiting for the condition.
B) It is not a cause at all; it is merely a hypothetical statement with no legal effect.
C) It is a cause in the present, but the divorce effect is postponed until the condition (entry) occurs.
D) It is a cause only if the husband is legally competent at the time of the condition.

Key Takeaway
A conditional divorce statement is a cause from the moment of utterance, with the divorce suspended until the condition is met.

The Requirement of Immediate Conduciveness in Legal Causes
Defense of immediacy of cause using sale with option

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to defend the Hanafi principle that a legal cause must be immediately conducive to its effect, using the example of sale with option.

Core Idea
A cause must be immediately conducive; in sale with option, the contract is the cause, only the ruling is delayed.

In Hanafi usul al-fiqh, a cause (sabab) is defined as something that leads to its legal effect directly and without delay. Critics point to sale with option (bay' bi al-khiyar) as a counterexample: ownership is delayed, so how can the contract be a cause? This lesson unpacks the Hanafi response: the contract is immediate; only the right to cancel is suspended. The delay in ownership is due to a recognized legal excuse (necessity), not a defect in the cause.

Guided Path
1
State the principle: cause must have ifda'
A sabab is something that naturally leads to its hukm without delay. This is the baseline rule in Hanafi usul.
Ifda' means direct conduciveness; without it, something is not a cause.
2
Identify the objection: sale with option seems to violate ifda'
In a sale with option, ownership is not immediately transferred; it is postponed until the option period ends. This appears to contradict the requirement of immediate effect.
The objector argues that if the contract were a true cause, ownership would occur instantly.
3
Analyze the Hanafi response: the contract is an immediate cause; only the ruling is delayed
The Hanafi response distinguishes between the cause (the contract) and the ruling (ownership). The contract is immediate; the suspension is only of the right to cancel (khiyar), which is a separate matter. The delay in ownership is due to necessity (darurah) to prevent harm (ghubn), not because the cause is suspended.
The necessity only affects the ruling, not the cause itself. The contract remains a cause that will produce ownership once the option is resolved.
4
Understand the role of istihsan and the condition 'ala'
The passage also uses the linguistic point that the word 'ala' in conditional statements indicates suspension of what follows, not of the main clause. Thus, in 'I sold to you on condition that you have the option', the sale is immediate; only the option is suspended.
This linguistic analysis reinforces that the cause (sale) is not suspended; only the option (a secondary matter) is suspended.

دخول الدار وقوع الطلاق) وهو الحكم (لا الإيقاع ضرورة و) قالوا (ثانياً لو لم يكن المعلق بالشرط (سبباً عند التعليق لم يكن سبباً عند وجود الشرط) فلم يقع الطلاق
He has a way to it except through an external evidence, like menstruation for performing the fast. So here also it remains upon the original rule as long as no evidence indicates deviation, and there is no evidence; so reflect. And he cited the sale with option and the deferred divorce (like 'you are divorced tomorrow') as causes, and the ruling—ownership in sale and occurrence of divorce in the deferred—is delayed due to the preventer of option and restriction. Perhaps they are also cited against the first evidence: that they only become causes when they encounter the locus and effect it, while option and restriction prevent that. The truth is that neither of them refutes the evidence, for we only claimed that suspension prevents effect and conduciveness because it does not result in the occurrence of anything in actual reality, and there is no suspension here; rather it is restriction, and its purport is the realization of this restricted [thing] in actual reality, so it has conduciveness and effect. The utmost of the matter is that the effect only occurs at the time of the existence of the restriction, so understand. And it was answered regarding the first: that option in it is contrary to analogy out of necessity to repel harm, and analogy requires the bindingness of the contract. And it (the necessity) is to the extent of the ruling only, so the ruling is attached to it. As for the cause, its attachment is without necessity, for its attachment necessitates the attachment of the ruling also without the opposite, and analogy rejects its attachment, so it does not attach without evidence. And also answered: that the condition 'ala indicates the suspension of what follows it, as was said: 'I will come to you on condition that you come to me' meaning: if I come to you, come to me. And if the suspended thing is what follows, which is the option, then the sale is immediate, and only the option regarding cancellation is suspended due to the existence of the sale. If you say: then why is the ruling of ownership not established despite the existence of the cause? He said: 'And the suspension of the ruling is only to repel harm' from the one who has the option. And perhaps you say: it was mentioned earlier that 'if you are divorced for a thousand' means: if you pay a thousand, you are divorced, and divorce is suspended upon payment, so what is before 'ala is suspended by what follows. The better approach is to omit from the answer the hadith about the condition 'ala indicating suspension of what follows; rather it is said: the sale is immediate, only the option to cancel is suspended, for the intent is that I sold and I have the option to cancel, based on a contextual indication in it. Then the answer is truly the permissibility of deviation due to evidence, except with regard to the difference in chain of transmission, so understand. And it was answered regarding the second: that a suspended oath is for negation, meaning its purpose is the non-existence of the condition; otherwise this problematic consequence would follow, so it does not lead to existence usually, but rather to atonement, so it does not become a cause. As for attribution, it realizes the attributed [thing]; for 'you are divorced tomorrow' indicates that the divorce is realized tomorrow, so the intent is the realization of divorce, so this becomes a divorce in the present, leading to occurrence tomorrow, so it becomes a cause. And it was refuted that the oath may be for incitement and urging the occurrence of the condition, not for negation, like 'if you give me news of my son's arrival, you are free'—so it should become a cause. However, it is said: since that which is for prevention does not become a cause, that which is for incitement also does not become a cause due to the lack of a distinction. And perhaps a distinction is made based on risk and doubt and their absence, meaning that in suspension the thing suspended upon is of doubtful existence, so it usually does not lead to the result, so it does not become a cause. As for the attributed, the restriction in it is not doubtful but realized, so it leads to the realization of what was restricted, so it becomes a cause. And a marginal note was found written with this expression: i.e., if the oath is regarding a harmful matter like divorce and the like, it is for negation; otherwise for incitement. According to this, risk prevents prevention. This contradicts the reliable sources in which this statement is transmitted, and perhaps it is a scribal error. Rather, it was related to what preceded it from the refutation, so the gist of the refutation is that negation is only when the oath is regarding a harmful matter; otherwise for incitement. The claim of negation generally in every oath is unreasonable, so ponder. (But this distinction) necessitates the impermissibility of hastening charity in the case when he says: 'upon me is charity on the day Zayd arrives' because Zayd's arrival is of doubtful existence, so it is not a cause in the present for the obligation of charity, like suspension by a condition; so hastening is performance before the obligation. And it necessitates that 'when tomorrow comes, you are free' is like 'when I die, you are free' because the coming of tomorrow is certain like death, so the suspended by tomorrow becomes a cause for freedom in the present like the suspended by death; thus selling both scenarios is not permissible due to the existence of the cause for freedom in both, while they distinguish and permit selling in the former but not the latter. I say regarding the first—i.e., when he says 'upon me is charity on the day Zayd arrives'—the expression is for actuality because the ruling in it is established in reality, but at a specific time, so it does not involve doubt and risk; rather it is attached externally, so the effectuation occurs from the vow-maker, so it becomes a cause. Unlike suspension, for the expression in it is merely to indicate obligation without regard to the realization of either or both sides, so there is no effectuation on the part of the speaker in this speech; rather effectuation occurs from him only upon the existence of the antecedent, so ponder. And I say regarding the second—i.e., suspension of emancipation upon death—the suspension is a cause now for tadbir (deferred manumission) by law, which is another transaction besides emancipation, rather of the type of will, and what leads to it is the suspension by death, so it is the cause, not the suspended thing, i.e., the cause is not the suspended thing, which is emancipation, such that a refutation occurs. And we have already explained that the suspended thing is not a cause for emancipation due to lack of conduciveness and lack of a legal evidence—unlike the case 'when tomorrow comes, you are free' because it is not a cause for emancipation legally nor for another transaction. And we have already explained that suspension does not qualify as a cause for emancipation due to lack of conduciveness to it. Know that the coming in 'when tomorrow comes' is of doubtful existence, for the condition is only the arrival of tomorrow before the death of the slave, for that is what is suitable for emancipation to be attached to. So this suspension and the suspension 'if you enter' are the same. But the suspended thing when it is death, as in 'when I die', is not death absolutely, but rather death before the slave's death, which is also doubtful, so it should not become a cause. So the problem is such except what the objector determined. And what the author said, although it wards off this, we have shown the statement of the verifying jurists in it, for he said in al-Hidayah and others: this is considered a cause now because the time of the suspended thing is not suitable for emancipation, for the time of death nullifies ownership, which is a condition for emancipation. And this, as it invalidates the author's answer, also serves as an answer to the original objection. However, Shaykh al-Hidad objected that the third remains in the deceased's ownership and provides a locus for the execution of bequests. This is also of the type of will, so it does not conflict with the execution of death. It is not hidden from you that the continuance of ownership for the deceased is inconceivable. As for the execution of the bequest, it is because the bequest is a transaction established during life, and its effect is to prevent the heirs from succeeding to ownership, for the testator has a successor in the amount of the bequest up to one-third, even if the legatee is not specified, but only in the approximate amount, like this bequest. So its effect appears in the last part of life and prevents its transfer to the heirs. So this condition, due to its specificity, does not prevent causality, nor the sequence of the result before the occurrence of the condition. This is a presentation of their statements according to their intent. Afterward, there remain hidden things in corners, and Allah knows best His rulings. (So understand) The Shafi'is said: First: suspension is to prevent the descent of the suspended thing, nothing else, like the suspension of a lantern, for it prevents its descent, not necessitates its descent. And the suspended is the ruling, because the antecedent of entering the house is the occurrence of divorce—it is the ruling, not the effectuation necessarily. And they said secondly: if the suspended by condition were not a cause at the time of suspension, it would not be a cause upon existence of the condition, so the divorce would not occur.
Source pages p. 468

Commentary
This passage from the sharh (expansion) of Musallam al-Thubut deals with a crucial usul al-fiqh principle: a cause (sabab) must produce its legal effect immediately; it cannot be suspended. The commentator discusses two counterexamples—sale with an option (bay' bi al-khiyar) and deferred divorce (talaq mudaf)—and shows that they do not violate the principle upon closer analysis. In sale with option, the cause (the contract) is immediate; only the right to cancel is suspended, so the ownership effect is delayed by a recognized legal excuse. In deferred divorce (e.g., 'you are divorced tomorrow'), the expression is an attribution (idafa), not a suspension (ta'liq), so it constitutes an immediate cause that produces its effect at the specified future time. The commentary then explores the distinction between suspension (ta'liq) and attribution (idafa), noting that suspension (e.g., 'if you enter, you are divorced') typically involves a condition of doubtful occurrence, thus not constituting an immediate cause. It further contrasts this with cases like vowing charity on a specific future event, which is considered an immediate cause because the expression indicates actuality (fi'liyya). The discussion concludes with the Shafi'i objection that suspension is merely to prevent the descent of the ruling, not to cause it, and that if it were not a cause at the time of suspension, it could not become a cause later. The Hanafi response, woven throughout, relies on istihsan (juristic preference) and the specific legal constructions of tadbir and other mechanisms. The teacher should emphasize that this passage demonstrates how Hanafi usul uses reason and textual analysis to resolve apparent contradictions, always maintaining the integrity of the original principle.

Key Terms
سبب (sabab): That which, by its very nature, leads to the existence of its effect (hukm) directly and without delay.
تعليق (ta'liq): Suspension of a ruling upon a future condition; the suspended thing (mu'allaq) is not considered a cause in the present because it lacks conduciveness (ifda').
إضافة (idafa): Attribution of an action to a specific future time, making it a present cause that will produce its effect at that time.
خيار (khiyar): Option in a contract; in sale with option, the contract is immediate but the right to cancel is a legal excuse that delays the finality of ownership.
إفضاء (ifda'): Conduciveness; the quality of a cause that leads directly to its effect without intermediary.
ضرورة (darurah): Necessity; a legal excuse that permits deviation from the general rule.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why does sale with option not violate the principle that a cause must be immediately conducive?
A) The option itself is the cause.
B) The contract is immediate; only the ruling is delayed by necessity.
C) The sale is suspended until the option is exercised.
D) The cause is also suspended.

Key Takeaway
A cause must be immediately conducive; in sale with option, the contract is the cause, and the delay in ownership is due to a recognized necessity, not a suspension of the cause.

Distinguishing Ta'liq from Idafa: Suspension vs Attribution
Hanafi analysis of ta'liq vs idafa and applications to divorce, charity, and emancipation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi distinction between ta'liq (suspension) and idafa (attribution) to classify statements like 'you are divorced tomorrow' and 'upon me is charity when Zayd arrives' as either causes or non-causes.

Core Idea
Ta'liq suspends on a doubtful condition and creates no cause; idafa attributes to a definite future time and creates an immediate cause.

A key distinction in Hanafi usul is between ta'liq (suspension) and idafa (attribution). While ta'liq involves a condition of doubtful occurrence and does not constitute a legal cause, idafa involves a definite future time and does constitute an immediate cause. This lesson explores these concepts through examples of deferred divorce, charity vows, and emancipation upon a future event. It also examines the case where a suspension on death is treated as a tadbir (a separate cause), and addresses the Shafi'i objection that if a suspended condition were not a cause now, it could never become one.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish ta'liq and idafa terminologically
Ta'liq is suspension of a ruling upon a future condition that is doubtful in occurrence. Idafa is attribution of a ruling to a definite future time (e.g., 'tomorrow'). The former is for negation; the latter for realization.
The key difference is certainty: idafa involves a certain time; ta'liq involves a doubtful condition.
2
Analyze deferred divorce: 'you are divorced tomorrow'
This is idafa. The word 'tomorrow' indicates a definite time, so the statement constitutes an immediate cause (sabab) for divorce, which will occur at that future time. There is no suspension of causality.
Contrast with 'if you enter, you are divorced'—that is ta'liq and not a cause.
3
Examine the charity vow: 'upon me is charity on the day Zayd arrives'
Although Zayd's arrival is doubtful, the vow is considered a cause because the expression is for actuality (fi'liyya). The ruling is established in reality, but at a specific time. It does not involve suspension; it is a promise with a time condition.
The passage notes that this is not ta'liq; the speaker intends to effectuate the charity at that time, so it becomes a cause.
4
Compare emancipation suspended on 'when tomorrow comes' vs 'when I die'
'When tomorrow comes, you are free' is ta'liq (doubtful because the slave might die before tomorrow), so it is not a cause for emancipation. 'When I die, you are free' is treated as tadbir (a will-like transaction), which is a separate cause in Hanafi law.
The distinction is based on the nature of the condition: death is a certain event, but the condition 'before the slave's death' introduces doubt; however, due to the specific ruling of tadbir, it is considered a cause.
5
Respond to the Shafi'i objection: 'if it's not a cause now, it never will be'
The Shafi'is argue that if a suspended condition is not a cause at the time of utterance, it cannot become a cause when the condition occurs. The Hanafi response is that the suspended condition itself is not intended to be a cause; rather, the legal system recognizes specific mechanisms (like istihsan, tadbir) that allow certain conditional statements to be causes despite the general rule.
This objection forces precision: Hanafis do not claim that all ta'liq creates causes; they claim that idafa and certain specific constructs (like tadbir) do.

حر لأنه ليس سبباً للعتاق شرعاً ولا لتصرف آخر وقد بينا سابقاً أن التعليق لا يصلح سبباً للعتق لعدم الإفضاء إليه، واعلم أن مجيء إذا جاء غد، مشكوك الوجود فإن الشرط ليس إلا مجيء الغد قبل موت العبد، فإنه هو الصالح لأن يتعلق به الإعتاق فهذا التعليق وتعليق إن دخلت سواء، لكن المعلق به إذا كان الموت كما في إذا مت فليس الموت مطلقاً، بل الموت قبل موت العبد، وهو مشكوك أيضاً، فينبغي أن لا ينعقد فالإشكال هكذا إلا ما قرره المعترض، وما قال المصنف وإن كان دافعاً له لكن بينا فيه كلام المحققين من الفقهاء فإنه قال في الهداية وغيرها إن هذا إنما اعتبر سبباً الآن، لعدم صلوح زمان المعلق به للإعتاق لأن وقت الموت معدم للملك، وهو من شرط الإعتاق، وهذا كما يبطل جواب المصنف يصلح جواباً عن أصل الإيراد أيضاً، لكن أورد عليه الشيخ الهداد أن الثالث يبقى في ملك الميت ويفيد محلاً لنفاذ الوصايا، وهذا أيضاً من قبيل الوصية، فلا ينافي نفاذ الموت، وأنت لا يذهب عليك أن بقاء الملك للميت مما لا يعقل، وأما نفاذ الوصية فلأن الوصية تصرف ثابتة حال الحياة وأثرها أن تمنع خلافة الورثة في الملك، له خليفة في مقدار الوصية إلى الثلث، وإن لم يكن الموصى له معيناً، بل في القرب فقط، كهذه الوصية، فيظهر أثره في آخر جزء من الحياة، ويمنع انتقاله إلى الورثة، فهذا الشرط لخصوصية فيه لا يمنع السببية، وترتب الجزاء قبل وقوع الشرط، هذا تقرير كلامهم على طبق مرادهم، وبعد بقي خبايا في الزوايا والله أعلم بأحكامه (فافهم) الشافعية قالوا: أولاً: التعليق لمنع نزول المعلق لا غير (كما في تعليق القنديل) فإنه يمنع نزوله لا اقتضاء نزوله (والمعلق الحكم لأن ملزوم دخول الدار وقوع الطلاق) وهو الحكم (لا الإيقاع ضرورة و) قالوا (ثانياً لو لم يكن المعلق بالشرط (سبباً عند التعليق لم يكن سبباً عند وجود الشرط) فلم يقع الطلاق
He has a way to it except through an external evidence, like menstruation for performing the fast. So here also it remains upon the original rule as long as no evidence indicates deviation, and there is no evidence; so reflect. And he cited the sale with option and the deferred divorce (like 'you are divorced tomorrow') as causes, and the ruling—ownership in sale and occurrence of divorce in the deferred—is delayed due to the preventer of option and restriction. Perhaps they are also cited against the first evidence: that they only become causes when they encounter the locus and effect it, while option and restriction prevent that. The truth is that neither of them refutes the evidence, for we only claimed that suspension prevents effect and conduciveness because it does not result in the occurrence of anything in actual reality, and there is no suspension here; rather it is restriction, and its purport is the realization of this restricted [thing] in actual reality, so it has conduciveness and effect. The utmost of the matter is that the effect only occurs at the time of the existence of the restriction, so understand. And it was answered regarding the first: that option in it is contrary to analogy out of necessity to repel harm, and analogy requires the bindingness of the contract. And it (the necessity) is to the extent of the ruling only, so the ruling is attached to it. As for the cause, its attachment is without necessity, for its attachment necessitates the attachment of the ruling also without the opposite, and analogy rejects its attachment, so it does not attach without evidence. And also answered: that the condition 'ala indicates the suspension of what follows it, as was said: 'I will come to you on condition that you come to me' meaning: if I come to you, come to me. And if the suspended thing is what follows, which is the option, then the sale is immediate, and only the option regarding cancellation is suspended due to the existence of the sale. If you say: then why is the ruling of ownership not established despite the existence of the cause? He said: 'And the suspension of the ruling is only to repel harm' from the one who has the option. And perhaps you say: it was mentioned earlier that 'if you are divorced for a thousand' means: if you pay a thousand, you are divorced, and divorce is suspended upon payment, so what is before 'ala is suspended by what follows. The better approach is to omit from the answer the hadith about the condition 'ala indicating suspension of what follows; rather it is said: the sale is immediate, only the option to cancel is suspended, for the intent is that I sold and I have the option to cancel, based on a contextual indication in it. Then the answer is truly the permissibility of deviation due to evidence, except with regard to the difference in chain of transmission, so understand. And it was answered regarding the second: that a suspended oath is for negation, meaning its purpose is the non-existence of the condition; otherwise this problematic consequence would follow, so it does not lead to existence usually, but rather to atonement, so it does not become a cause. As for attribution, it realizes the attributed [thing]; for 'you are divorced tomorrow' indicates that the divorce is realized tomorrow, so the intent is the realization of divorce, so this becomes a divorce in the present, leading to occurrence tomorrow, so it becomes a cause. And it was refuted that the oath may be for incitement and urging the occurrence of the condition, not for negation, like 'if you give me news of my son's arrival, you are free'—so it should become a cause. However, it is said: since that which is for prevention does not become a cause, that which is for incitement also does not become a cause due to the lack of a distinction. And perhaps a distinction is made based on risk and doubt and their absence, meaning that in suspension the thing suspended upon is of doubtful existence, so it usually does not lead to the result, so it does not become a cause. As for the attributed, the restriction in it is not doubtful but realized, so it leads to the realization of what was restricted, so it becomes a cause. And a marginal note was found written with this expression: i.e., if the oath is regarding a harmful matter like divorce and the like, it is for negation; otherwise for incitement. According to this, risk prevents prevention. This contradicts the reliable sources in which this statement is transmitted, and perhaps it is a scribal error. Rather, it was related to what preceded it from the refutation, so the gist of the refutation is that negation is only when the oath is regarding a harmful matter; otherwise for incitement. The claim of negation generally in every oath is unreasonable, so ponder. (But this distinction) necessitates the impermissibility of hastening charity in the case when he says: 'upon me is charity on the day Zayd arrives' because Zayd's arrival is of doubtful existence, so it is not a cause in the present for the obligation of charity, like suspension by a condition; so hastening is performance before the obligation. And it necessitates that 'when tomorrow comes, you are free' is like 'when I die, you are free' because the coming of tomorrow is certain like death, so the suspended by tomorrow becomes a cause for freedom in the present like the suspended by death; thus selling both scenarios is not permissible due to the existence of the cause for freedom in both, while they distinguish and permit selling in the former but not the latter. I say regarding the first—i.e., when he says 'upon me is charity on the day Zayd arrives'—the expression is for actuality because the ruling in it is established in reality, but at a specific time, so it does not involve doubt and risk; rather it is attached externally, so the effectuation occurs from the vow-maker, so it becomes a cause. Unlike suspension, for the expression in it is merely to indicate obligation without regard to the realization of either or both sides, so there is no effectuation on the part of the speaker in this speech; rather effectuation occurs from him only upon the existence of the antecedent, so ponder. And I say regarding the second—i.e., suspension of emancipation upon death—the suspension is a cause now for tadbir (deferred manumission) by law, which is another transaction besides emancipation, rather of the type of will, and what leads to it is the suspension by death, so it is the cause, not the suspended thing, i.e., the cause is not the suspended thing, which is emancipation, such that a refutation occurs. And we have already explained that the suspended thing is not a cause for emancipation due to lack of conduciveness and lack of a legal evidence—unlike the case 'when tomorrow comes, you are free' because it is not a cause for emancipation legally nor for another transaction. And we have already explained that suspension does not qualify as a cause for emancipation due to lack of conduciveness to it. Know that the coming in 'when tomorrow comes' is of doubtful existence, for the condition is only the arrival of tomorrow before the death of the slave, for that is what is suitable for emancipation to be attached to. So this suspension and the suspension 'if you enter' are the same. But the suspended thing when it is death, as in 'when I die', is not death absolutely, but rather death before the slave's death, which is also doubtful, so it should not become a cause. So the problem is such except what the objector determined. And what the author said, although it wards off this, we have shown the statement of the verifying jurists in it, for he said in al-Hidayah and others: this is considered a cause now because the time of the suspended thing is not suitable for emancipation, for the time of death nullifies ownership, which is a condition for emancipation. And this, as it invalidates the author's answer, also serves as an answer to the original objection. However, Shaykh al-Hidad objected that the third remains in the deceased's ownership and provides a locus for the execution of bequests. This is also of the type of will, so it does not conflict with the execution of death. It is not hidden from you that the continuance of ownership for the deceased is inconceivable. As for the execution of the bequest, it is because the bequest is a transaction established during life, and its effect is to prevent the heirs from succeeding to ownership, for the testator has a successor in the amount of the bequest up to one-third, even if the legatee is not specified, but only in the approximate amount, like this bequest. So its effect appears in the last part of life and prevents its transfer to the heirs. So this condition, due to its specificity, does not prevent causality, nor the sequence of the result before the occurrence of the condition. This is a presentation of their statements according to their intent. Afterward, there remain hidden things in corners, and Allah knows best His rulings. (So understand) The Shafi'is said: First: suspension is to prevent the descent of the suspended thing, nothing else, like the suspension of a lantern, for it prevents its descent, not necessitates its descent. And the suspended is the ruling, because the antecedent of entering the house is the occurrence of divorce—it is the ruling, not the effectuation necessarily. And they said secondly: if the suspended by condition were not a cause at the time of suspension, it would not be a cause upon existence of the condition, so the divorce would not occur.
Source pages p. 468

Commentary
This passage from the sharh (expansion) of Musallam al-Thubut deals with a crucial usul al-fiqh principle: a cause (sabab) must produce its legal effect immediately; it cannot be suspended. The commentator discusses two counterexamples—sale with an option (bay' bi al-khiyar) and deferred divorce (talaq mudaf)—and shows that they do not violate the principle upon closer analysis. In sale with option, the cause (the contract) is immediate; only the right to cancel is suspended, so the ownership effect is delayed by a recognized legal excuse. In deferred divorce (e.g., 'you are divorced tomorrow'), the expression is an attribution (idafa), not a suspension (ta'liq), so it constitutes an immediate cause that produces its effect at the specified future time. The commentary then explores the distinction between suspension (ta'liq) and attribution (idafa), noting that suspension (e.g., 'if you enter, you are divorced') typically involves a condition of doubtful occurrence, thus not constituting an immediate cause. It further contrasts this with cases like vowing charity on a specific future event, which is considered an immediate cause because the expression indicates actuality (fi'liyya). The discussion concludes with the Shafi'i objection that suspension is merely to prevent the descent of the ruling, not to cause it, and that if it were not a cause at the time of suspension, it could not become a cause later. The Hanafi response, woven throughout, relies on istihsan (juristic preference) and the specific legal constructions of tadbir and other mechanisms. The teacher should emphasize that this passage demonstrates how Hanafi usul uses reason and textual analysis to resolve apparent contradictions, always maintaining the integrity of the original principle.

Key Terms
تعليق (ta'liq): Suspension of a ruling upon a future condition; does not create a cause because it lacks conduciveness.
إضافة (idafa): Attribution of a ruling to a definite future time; creates an immediate cause.
يمين (yamin): Oath; a type of suspended statement usually intended to prevent something from happening.
تدبير (tadbir): Deferred manumission; a will-like transaction where a slave is freed upon the master's death.
فعلية (fi'liyya): Actuality; indicates that the speaker intends to effectuate the act at the specified time.
شك (shakk): Doubt; the presence of doubt in the condition is key to distinguishing ta'liq from idafa.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is the statement 'you are divorced tomorrow' considered a cause (sabab) while 'if you enter, you are divorced' is not?
A) Because 'tomorrow' is a definite time, making it idafa, which is an immediate cause.
B) Because 'if you enter' is more likely to happen.
C) Because the Hanafi school only recognizes idafa as a cause for divorce.
D) Because both are not causes according to the Shafi'i school.

Key Takeaway
Idafa (attribution to a definite future time) constitutes an immediate cause, while ta'liq (suspension on a doubtful condition) does not; this distinction is central to Hanafi causation theory.
